Page  7
A. 
Program Purpose 
and 
Project Development Objective 
1. 
Program 
purpose 
and 
program 
phasing: 
This 
program 
will 
support 
Chile’s  transition 
to 
a 
knowledge-based economy 
by 
investing 
in 
the 
innovation 
system 
and the stock 
of 
human 
capital. The overarching 
goals 
are 
to diversify 
the Chilean 
economy, 
reduce 
its 
vulnerability 
to 
external 
shocks, 
and, 
in 
the 
long 
run, 
provide 
the  basis 
for 
sustainable 
growth. 
The program 
goes 
to 
the 
heart 
of 
what 
has 
been 
identified 
as 
a 
critical 
ingredient 
of 
high productivity 
and 
growth: 
The 
need 
for 
integrated 
innovation 
and human 
capital policies 
and systems 
(De 
Ferranti, Perry 
et 
al., 
2002 and 
2003). 
Chile 
is 
among 
the 
countries 
in 
the 
region 
best 
prepared 
to 
take 
a 
leap 
forward 
in 
this 
area 
and 
follow 
other 
nations that 
have 
achieved 
long 
term 
growth 
through well-matched education 
and 
technology policies. 
Given 
the 
long-term 
vision 
necessary 
to 
consolidate 
institutional 
and 
behavioral 
changes 
in 
the 
S&T 
sector, 
a program 
approach 
is 
proposed 
using 
the 
Adaptable Program 
Lending (APL) 
instrument. 
The 
program 
will 
comprise 
two 
phases. 
The 
first 
phase 
will 
cover a 
3% year 
period 
(2003 
- 
2007) and 
will 
support the establishment 
of 
a 
coherent 
policy 
strategy and 
a strong 
policy 
framework. 
It 
will 
also 
provide 
for 
the 
continuation 
of 
the 
Millennium 
Science 
Initiative 
and 
a 
further 
strengthening 
of 
the 
science 
base 
by 
scaling up 
funding 
for 
research, 
develop 
public-private 
linkages 
in 
Chile’s  national 
innovative 
system 
and 
enhance 
the 
R&D 
capacity 
of 
companies. Based 
on a 
positive 
evaluation 
and 
the 
attainment 
of 
predefined triggers, 
the 
second 
phase 
(2007-2010) 
will 
continue 
activities 
to 
strengthen 
Chile’s 
science 
base 
and 
scale 
up 
funding 
for 
initiatives 
launched 
in 
the 
first 
phase, 
especially 
with 
a 
view 
to 
enhancing 
private 
sector 
R&D 
activities. 
2. 
Project 
development 
objective: 
(see 
Annex 
1) 
The 
project 
seeks 
two 
development 
objectives, which, 
in 
agreement 
with 
the Government’s 
strategy, 
are 
expected 
to 
place 
Chile 
on the 
path 
to 
a knowledge-based 
economy: 
First, 
and foremost, 
the 
project 
will 
support 
the 
development 
of 
an 
effective 
innovation 
system. 
It 
will 
do 
so 
by 
establishing a strong 
and coherent 
policy 
framework, 
promoting 
high-quality 
and 
relevant 
science 
and 
technology 
activities 
and 
by 
supporting 
key 
interfaces 
in 
the 
innovation 
system, 
especially between 
the 
public 
and 
private 
sector 
as 
well 
as 
international 
linkages. 
Second, 
and subordinate 
to 
the 
first 
goal, 
the 
project 
will 
improve 
the 
stock 
of 
human 
capital 
in 
the 
Chilean 
science 
and 
technology 
sector, 
a development 
objective 
which 
is 
highly 
complementary 
to 
the 
establishment 
of 
an 
effective  innovation 
system and 
per 
se 
a 
critical 
precondition 
for 
establishing a 
competitive 
knowledge-based economy. 
The 
project 
development 
objectives 
will 
be 
pursued through 
the 
implementation 
of 
three components: 
(i) 
Improving Chile’s 
Science, 
Technology and 
Innovation 
System; 
(ii) 
Strengthening 
Chile 
’s 
Science Base, 
and 
(iii) 
Enhancing 
Public-Private 
Linkages. 
See 
section 
C.l 
for 
a 
detailed  description 
of 
the 
components. 
3. 
Key 
performance 
indicators: 
(see 
Annex 
1) 
A 
number 
of 
indicators 
will 
be 
monitored 
in 
order 
to 
assess 
the 
long-term impact 
of 
the 
project. 
These 
indicators 
are 
not 
expected 
to 
fully 
materialize 
within 
the 
project, 
but 
considerable 
change 
is 
nevertheless expected 
at 
the 
end 
of 
the 
project implementation 
period. 
The 
program 
will 
contribute 
to 
improvement 
in 
the 
following 
long-term 
key 
performance indicators: 
-3- 
Page  8
0 
0 
Total 
Factor 
Productivity 
will 
increase. 
Level 
of 
embedded 
technology 
and 
knowledge 
in 
current and 
future 
products 
will 
rise. 
Further, 
the 
following 
key 
indicators 
will 
be 
used 
to 
measure 
performance: 
0 
0 
0 
0 
0 
No. 
of 
domestic patents 
will 
increase. 
Private 
sector research 
and 
development 
will 
increase. 
Coordination between 
different 
parts 
of 
the 
innovation 
system 
will 
increase 
Public-private 
S&T 
cofinancing 
will 
increase. 
No. 
of 
researchers 
relative 
to 
the 
size 
of 
the 
population 
will 
increase. 
B. 
Strategic 
Context 
1. 
Sector-related 
Country 
Assistance 
Strategy 
(CAS) 
goal 
supported 
by 
the project: 
(see 
Annex 
1) 
Document number: 
23329 
The 
project 
will 
in 
particular contribute 
to 
the 
Country 
Assistance 
Strategy 
(CAS)  goal 
of 
sustaining 
overall 
economic 
growth 
and 
social 
progress 
in 
Chile. 
In 
addition, 
the 
project 
will 
support 
the 
ongoing  modernization 
of 
the 
state 
by 
(i) 
supporting core 
capacities 
such 
as 
strategic 
policy 
formulation 
and 
monitoring 
and evaluation, 
and 
(ii) 
identifying 
main 
obstacles 
to 
innovation 
and 
human 
capital formation 
and 
providing 
capacity 
building 
to 
key 
government 
institutions 
where appropriate. 
Finally, 
the 
proposed 
project 
will, 
together 
with 
the 
Bank’s 
higher 
education improvement 
project 
( 
MECE-SUP), 
the 
Bank’s 
Lifelong 
Learning  project 
(Chile  Culifca), 
and 
IADB’s 
efforts 
in 
the 
technology 
sector, 
form 
a 
coherent 
set 
of 
intervention 
policies 
and investments 
that 
would 
improve 
Chile’s 
competitiveness. 
Date 
of 
latest 
CAS 
discussion: 
January 
23, 
2002 
2. 
Main 
sector 
issues 
and 
Government 
strategy: 
This 
section 
deliberately 
frames 
the 
Chilean 
science, 
technology and 
innovation 
sector 
in 
the 
context 
of 
its 
global 
rather than 
regional 
competitors 
(Brunner, 
2003), 
since 
Chile’s  ambition 
is 
to 
reach 
OECD 
levels 
of 
competitiveness, 
productivity, 
and 
social development. 
The 
Need 
for 
Increased 
Application 
of 
Knowledge 
in 
the 
Economy 
Unlike 
most 
developed 
economies, 
the Chilean 
economy 
is 
to 
a large extent 
based 
on 
natural 
resources, 
notably 
mining, fishing,  forestry 
and 
agriculture. 
Copper, 
for 
example, accounts 
for 
more 
than 
10% 
of 
GDP 
and more 
than 
40% 
of 
export 
earnings. 
Likewise, 
exports 
from 
the 
fruit 
sector 
grew 
at 
a rate 
of 
20% annually 
in 
the 
period 
1974-1994. 
Application 
of 
knowledge 
is 
equally  important 
for 
the 
exploitation 
of 
natural 
resources 
as 
it 
is 
in 
High-tech 
sectors such 
as 
information 
and 
communication. 
As 
pointed 
out 
in 
the 
recent 
World 
Bank 
publication, 
From 
Natural 
Resources 
to 
the Knowledge 
Economy 
(World 
Bank, 
2002g), 
it 
is 
important 
to 
keep 
in 
mind 
that 
the 
concept 
of 
the 
knowledge economy 
is 
primarily 
targeted 
at 
“how”  the 
economy 
produces, 
rather than 
“’what”. 
Evidence 
shows 
that 
productivity 
gains 
from 
technology 
can 
be equally 
high 
and 
even 
higher 
in 
natural resource 
sectors 
(such 
as 
agriculture) 
than 
in 
manufacturing. 
Science and 
Technology 
strengthens 
comparative 
advantages 
in 
three 
ways: 
(i) 
create 
niches 
in 
existing 
areas 
of 
comparative 
advantage; 
(ii) 
codify 
and 
commercialize 
tacit 
knowledge 
in 
existing 
comparative 
advantages, 
and 
(iii) 
build 
new comparative 
advantages 
in 
connecting 
multidisciplinary 
areas 
of 
existing 
knowledge. The development 
path 
followed 
by 
countries 
like 
Australia, 
Sweden, 
and 
Finland 
is 
a clear 
testimony 
to 
these 
facts. 
-4- 
Page  9
In 
Chile,  the natural 
resource 
sectors 
are 
to 
some 
extent 
based 
on the 
application 
of 
sophisticated 
technology. 
Chile 
has 
boosted 
productivity 
and 
output 
of 
these sectors 
by 
adapting 
and 
improving 
new 
technologies. Development 
of 
the 
mining 
sector 
was 
facilitated 
by 
Australian and 
Canadian 
know-how 
and 
investments. 
Likewise, 
the 
fruit 
sector owes 
its 
success 
to 
private 
sector 
agents, 
who successfully 
adapted 
foreign 
technologies 
to local 
conditions. 
Hence, 
the 
achievements 
of 
both 
sectors 
underscore the 
importance 
of 
innovation 
and 
technological 
development. 
Despite 
these 
success 
stories, 
the  Chilean 
economy 
is 
in 
need 
of 
further 
development. 
Chile’s 
manufacturing valued 
added 
per 
capita, 
although higher than 
the 
regional 
average, 
is 
still 
much 
lower 
than 
that 
of 
the 
OECD 
countries (Agapitova 
and 
Holm-Nielsen, 
2002 
and 
Brunner, 
2003). 
This 
is 
partly 
due 
to 
the 
domination 
of 
Chile’s 
economy 
by 
micro, small and 
medium 
enterprises, 
which 
lag 
behind 
in 
terms 
of 
technological  development 
and 
knowledge  formation. 
In 
consequence, 
high-tech  products 
constitute a 
low 
share 
of 
total 
export 
earnings compared 
to 
economies 
such 
as 
Argentina, 
Mexico 
and 
Brazil, 
and 
growth 
is 
to 
a 
high 
extent 
based 
on factor accumulation rather than 
innovative 
approaches 
to 
production. 
Therefore, 
Chile 
must 
embrace 
the 
knowledge 
economy. 
The 
Four 
Pillars 
of 
the 
Knowledge Economy 
The 
“knowledge 
economy” 
is 
a broad complex 
concept 
coined 
in 
recent 
years 
to 
indicate 
that 
learning 
and 
knowledge increasingly 
has 
become 
the 
critical 
conditions 
for 
commercial 
success. 
At 
its 
core, 
the 
concept 
stresses 
the 
role 
of 
knowledge 
as 
a  determinant 
of 
economic  growth.  The 
essence 
and 
complexity 
of 
the 
concept 
is 
captured 
in 
the 
World 
Bank 
framework, 
“the 
four pillars 
of 
the knowledge 
economy”, 
pioneered 
by 
Dahlman 
(2000). 
The 
assumption 
is 
that 
for 
a knowledge economy to 
be 
successful, the 
following 
conditions 
must 
be 
met: 
(i) 
An 
efficient policy 
framework  and strong economic incentives 
which 
induce human 
capital formation 
and 
innovation; 
(ii) 
a 
high 
level 
of 
human 
capital 
to 
take 
advantage 
of 
scientific 
and 
technological 
progress; 
(iii) 
an 
efective 
innovation 
system, 
which 
adapts 
existing  innovations 
successfully 
to 
the 
economy 
and  expands 
the 
existing  frontier 
of 
knowledge,  and 
finally 
(iv) 
a 
high 
penetration 
of 
information  and communications technologies, 
which 
reduces 
the 
costs 
of 
transaction and 
increases 
flows 
of 
information. 
The 
following 
four 
sub-sections 
present 
the 
main 
issues 
within 
each 
pillar. 
Policy 
framework 
and 
economic incentives 
At 
the  most basic 
level, 
Chile 
benefits 
from 
an 
institutional 
framework 
far 
better than 
most 
LAC 
countries. The 
rule 
of 
law, 
for 
example, 
is 
consolidated and 
property 
rights 
are 
well 
protected. 
Likewise, 
levels 
of 
corruption 
are 
less 
serious 
in 
Chile 
than 
in 
most other 
LAC 
countries 
and the 
government 
continues 
to 
improve 
these 
fundamentals 
of 
development. 
Chile 
was 
the 
first 
LAC 
country 
to 
abandon 
the 
import 
substitution 
strategy 
and 
open 
the economy 
to 
foreign 
trade 
and 
investment. 
Hence, 
for 
more 
than 
two 
decades 
the 
Chilean 
economy 
has 
been 
subjected 
to 
intense 
international 
competition. 
This 
has 
increased the 
incentive 
to 
improve 
productivity 
and 
has 
prepared Chilean 
companies 
well 
for 
the 
global 
market 
(Brunner, 
2003). 
Notwithstanding 
these 
strengths, 
Chile’s 
policy 
framework 
and 
incentives 
for 
innovation 
and human 
capital formation 
are 
inadequately 
functioning 
in 
the 
following 
areas: 
Insufficient 
coherence 
in 
sector 
policies 
and strategies. 
First, 
of 
all 
Chile 
lacks 
a 
coherent 
government 
strategy 
for 
human 
capital 
formation 
and 
innovation. 
As 
pointed 
out 
in 
Agapitova 
and 
Holm-Nielsen 
(2002), 
no 
single 
high-level 
body 
is 
responsible 
for 
coordinating 
science, 
technology and 
innovation 
policies 
in 
Chile 
and 
public 
S&T 
policies 
have consequently 
evolved 
in 
an ad-hoc 
manner. 
Given 
the 
fact that 
human 
capital 
formation 
is 
critical 
to 
the development 
of 
a 
high 
quality 
S&T 
system, 
it 
is 
also 
lamentable that Chilean 
policy 
makers 
do 
not 
coordinate 
policies 
across 
different 
sectors. 
One 
of 
the 
implications 
has 
been 
low 
relevance 
of 
science 
to 
society and 
insufficient 
private-public 
knowledge 
flow. 
The 
development 
of 
a 
holistic, 
coherent 
government 
strategy 
is 
therefore 
critical. 
Weak 
Intellectual 
Property Rights 
regime. 
Chile’s 
intellectual 
property regime 
is 
judged 
to 
be 
one 
of 
the 
best 
in 
the 
LAC 
region. 
Even 
so, 
a 
survey 
by 
the 
World 
Economic 
Forum 
reveals that 
the 
level 
of 
-5- 
Page  10
intellectual 
property 
protection 
is 
inadequate. 
Recent 
initiatives 
on 
the 
part 
of 
the 
GoC 
to 
amend 
the 
LPR-regulation 
are 
unlikely 
to 
bring 
the regime 
fully 
into 
agreement 
with 
WTO-regulations (Agapitova 
and 
Holm-Nielsen, 
2002). 
ZnsufJicient 
access 
to 
risk 
capital. 
In 
general 
terms, 
the 
financial 
markets 
in 
Chile 
and 
the 
bank 
system 
are 
well 
consolidated. 
However, investors 
are 
highly 
conservative 
and risk-averse, 
which 
puts 
a 
damper 
on 
innovation 
and 
business 
diversification 
in 
Chile. 
Although 
recent 
initiatives 
such 
as 
matching 
grants 
offered 
by 
the 
Government through 
COW0 
call 
for 
optimism, 
there 
is 
a 
need 
to 
provide 
greater 
access 
to 
venture 
capital 
(Dahlman, 
Hanna, 
Leipziger, 
Motta 
et 
al., 
(2003) 
and 
Agapitova 
and 
Holm-Nielsen, 
2002). 
Existence 
of 
administrative barriers. 
Obstacles 
to 
set 
up 
new 
businesses 
are less 
prevalent 
in 
Chile 
than 
in 
most other 
LAC 
countries. However, 
the 
time 
and cost 
of 
business 
registration 
far 
exceed 
the 
OECD 
average, 
hampering 
attempts 
to 
foster 
a 
culture 
of 
entrepreneurship 
(Djankov 
et 
al., 
2001). 
The 
Chilean 
Patent 
Office 
has 
also 
been 
criticized for 
operating 
in 
an 
bureaucratic 
and 
inflexible 
manner, 
reducing 
the 
incentive 
of 
private 
sector 
agents 
to 
solicit 
patents and 
invest 
in 
R&D. 
ZnsufJicient 
monitoring 
and evaluation. 
Finally, 
as 
a crosscutting 
concern, 
it 
should 
be 
pointed 
out 
that 
the Chilean  administration  lacks 
experience and 
tradition 
for  monitoring 
and evaluating 
policies 
on 
science, 
technology and innovation. 
This 
in 
turn follows 
from 
the 
fact that 
these 
areas 
have 
enjoyed 
less 
prominence than 
other 
policy 
areas, 
such 
as 
basic 
education 
where 
a successful 
monitoring 
system 
(SIMCE) 
is 
in 
place. 
The 
implication 
is 
that 
S&T 
policies 
and 
programs 
that 
do not 
lead 
to 
the 
desired 
outcome 
can 
continue and 
thereby reduce 
the 
quality 
and 
efficiency 
of 
S&T 
expenditure. 
Human 
capital 
Human capital 
is 
a 
key 
ingredient 
in 
the 
knowledge 
economy. 
Thus, 
a constant 
flow 
of 
skilled 
workers 
is 
a 
necessity 
if 
an 
economy 
is 
to 
take 
full 
advantage 
of 
constantly 
improving 
technologies. Enhancing 
Chile’s  innovation 
capacity  especially  calls 
for 
relevant 
and 
high-quality  tertiary 
education 
and 
post-graduate 
training. Chile’s education 
system 
is 
a product 
of 
numerous 
policy 
reforms launched over 
the 
past 
decades. 
Various  objectives 
have 
guided 
these  changes. 
The 
first 
wave 
of 
reforms 
was 
introduced 
in 
the early 
1980s 
with 
a 
view 
to improving 
cost-efficiency 
and enrollment. 
Subsequent 
reforms 
have, 
by 
contrast, been 
more focused on educational output 
and 
have 
significantly contributed 
to 
increasing 
the 
quality 
and relevance 
of 
education. 
However, 
numerous 
challenges remain. 
The 
stock 
of 
researchers 
is 
low. 
The Chilean 
system 
suffers 
from 
a 
lack 
of 
research 
personnel. 
The 
supply 
of 
PhDs 
is 
very 
low 
compared 
to 
leading 
LAC 
economies and 
a 
critical 
mass 
of 
highly 
skilled 
scientists 
is 
yet 
to 
emerge. 
The 
annual 
PhD 
production 
has 
been 
increasing over the last 
years, 
but 
the 
number 
of 
doctoral 
graduates 
in 
engineering  and  technology 
is 
significantly 
behind 
the  levels 
of 
countries 
such 
as 
Argentina, 
Cuba 
and 
Mexico. 
Consequently, 
the 
attempts 
to 
increase 
the 
stock 
of 
PhDs 
need 
to 
be 
further 
supported 
if 
Chile 
is 
to 
establish a 
“critical 
mass” 
of 
researchers 
and 
scientists. 
Inadequate human 
capital 
decreases 
the 
knowledge  absorptive capacity 
of 
Chilean 
firms 
and 
their 
tendency 
to 
collaborate 
in 
R&D, 
which 
hampers 
innovation. 
Low 
professional 
level 
of 
S&T  teachers 
in 
basic education. 
Chile 
is 
among 
the 
regional 
leaders 
as 
regards 
years 
of 
schooling 
of 
teachers 
in 
basic 
education 
(World 
Bank, 
1999b). 
Accordingly, 
Chile 
has 
experienced 
substantial improvement 
of 
student 
performance 
in 
mathematics and 
science 
during 
the 
last 
decades. 
However, 
further 
improvements 
are 
impeded 
by 
mediocre 
S&T 
training 
of 
teachers 
in 
teacher 
colleges. 
As 
a 
result, 
students 
in 
basic 
education 
do 
not 
acquire satisfactory 
levels 
of 
proficiency 
in 
hard 
sciences 
and 
are 
reported 
to 
be 
ill 
prepared 
for 
advanced 
tertiary 
education 
in 
S&T. 
A 
recent 
international 
TIMSS-test 
benchmarking the 
mathematics and science 
ability 
of 
students 
at 
the eighth 
grade 
level 
revealed that Chilean 
pupils 
rate 
far 
below 
the 
mean 
international 
score 
and 
notably behind 
comparable 
countries 
in 
Asia 
and 
Eastern Europe 
(World 
Economic Forum, 
2002). 
Along 
with 
recent 
improvements 
to 
the 
science 
curriculum 
designed 
to 
give 
science 
a 
higher 
profile 
in 
primary 
and 
-6- 
Page  11
secondary 
schools, 
Chile 
is 
likely 
to 
benefit 
from 
improving 
the 
science 
skills 
of 
teachers 
in 
basic 
education. 
Quality 
and relevance 
of 
tertiary 
education 
varies considerably. 
As 
pointed 
out 
in 
World 
Bank 
(1998) 
the 
quality 
of 
tertiary 
education 
differs 
substantially 
in 
Chile 
with 
public 
universities generally 
ahead 
of 
private 
universities. 
Local 
variations  notwithstanding, 
it 
remains 
without 
doubt 
that 
the 
quality 
of 
Chilean tertiary 
education lags 
far 
behind 
the 
levels 
of 
OECD 
countries. 
University 
education 
in 
Chile 
“meets 
the 
needs 
of 
a competitive 
economy” 
to 
a higher 
degree 
than 
in 
other 
LAC 
countries 
such 
as 
Mexico, 
Brazil 
and Argentina 
(IMD, 
2001). 
Still, 
the 
relevance 
of 
Chilean higher education 
is 
far behind 
countries 
such 
as 
Israel, 
Finland, 
Ireland 
and 
USA. 
Moreover, 
Chile 
has 
been 
relatively 
unsuccessful 
in 
supplying 
“skills 
for 
the knowledge economy” and 
fall 
behind 
with 
regard 
to 
the 
development 
of 
higher 
order 
skills 
in 
science 
and engineering. 
By 
contrast,  there 
has  been 
a 
proliferation 
of 
“soft  study” 
programs 
such 
as 
journalism, 
law 
and 
business 
administration. 
As 
pointed 
out 
by 
Brunner 
(2001), 
the 
lack 
of 
hard 
skills 
constitutes a 
major 
bottleneck 
to 
the 
development 
of 
the 
Chilean 
economy. 
Innovation 
system 
Analyses 
of 
technological development 
have 
traditionally 
focused on 
input 
factors 
such 
as 
the absolute 
level 
of 
R&D 
expenditures 
and 
the 
different 
actors 
in 
the 
system: 
The 
enterprise 
sector, 
universities 
and 
public 
research 
institutions.  However, 
when 
Lundvall 
(1992)  elaborated 
the 
concept 
of 
a “national 
innovation 
system”, 
the 
intention 
was 
to 
promote a 
systemic 
approach, 
which 
in 
addition 
to 
traditional 
concerns 
draws attention 
to 
the importance 
of 
interactions among 
the 
different 
actors 
in 
the 
system. 
The 
rationale 
is 
that 
the 
effectiveness 
of 
an 
innovation 
system 
will 
increase 
if 
stakeholders 
have 
access 
to 
the 
existing 
pool 
of 
knowledge and cooperation 
is 
widespread. 
In 
consequence, 
government 
policies 
should 
also 
focus 
on 
mobility 
patterns, 
cooperation  and 
stimulation 
of 
knowledge 
flows. 
The 
following 
paragraphs 
highlight 
the 
main 
issues 
in 
the 
Chilean 
innovation 
system. 
Total 
R&D 
expenditures 
lag 
OECD 
levels. 
A 
precondition 
for 
an 
effective  innovation 
system 
is 
the 
availability 
of 
sufficient 
resources. 
In 
the 
case 
of 
Chile, expenditures 
for 
R&D 
do 
not 
only 
lag 
the 
most 
developed 
countries, 
but 
are 
also 
behind 
a 
number 
of 
countries 
in 
the 
region. 
Hence, 
Chile 
spends 
less 
than 
both 
Brazil 
and Costa 
Rica 
in 
terms 
of 
expenditures 
as 
share 
of 
GDP, 
and 
is 
even 
further 
behind 
countries 
such 
as 
the 
United 
States 
and 
Korea, 
which on 
average spend 
around 3% 
of 
GDP 
on 
R&D, 
whereas 
Chile 
spends 
0.5% 
of 
GDP. 
Inadequate 
quality 
of 
research. 
Chile’s  scientific  community 
is 
well 
respected 
in 
the 
region 
and 
worldwide. 
In 
several 
areas, 
Chilean 
researchers 
are 
among the 
international 
leaders. 
However, 
while 
progress 
has 
been 
substantial 
it 
has 
not 
been 
sufficient 
for 
the 
country’s 
aspirations 
and 
much remains 
to 
be 
done. 
Firm 
level 
data 
suggest 
that 
the 
quality 
of 
Chile’s 
scientific 
institutions 
is 
lower 
than countries 
such 
as 
Korea 
and 
India 
and 
far 
behind, 
for 
example, 
Finland 
and 
the 
United 
States 
(Maloney, 
2003). 
Mediocre quality 
of 
science 
significantly 
reduces 
incentives 
for 
the 
globally 
competing 
private 
sector 
to 
cooperate 
with 
public 
researchers 
and 
consequently 
diminishes 
innovation. 
Deficient 
participation 
in 
international 
networks. 
The Chilean 
trade and investment regime 
provides 
for 
a considerable transfer 
of 
technology 
from 
international 
trade 
and 
investment 
partners. 
As 
already 
indicated, 
this 
has 
played 
an 
important 
role 
in 
developing 
the natural 
resource-based 
sectors 
in 
Chile. 
Despite 
its 
integration 
into 
the 
world 
economy, 
Chile 
is 
yet 
to 
take 
full 
advantage 
of 
international 
knowledge networks 
as 
a 
source 
of 
innovation 
(Maloney, 
2003). 
The 
number 
of 
Chilean 
scientific 
articles 
with 
international 
co-authorship 
has 
grown 
in 
recent 
year, 
indicating 
a 
rising 
level 
of 
international 
cooperation 
by 
academia 
in 
Chile. 
Still, 
the 
growth 
rates 
observed 
in 
Chile 
are 
lagging 
the 
levels 
of 
other 
LAC 
countries 
such 
as 
Argentina 
(NSF, 
2002). 
Noteworthy 
is 
also 
the 
low 
international 
labor-mobility 
of 
well-educated Chileans. 
A 
recent 
survey 
by 
the 
Institute 
for 
Management Development 
reveals that 
Chile 
ranks 
second 
lowest 
in 
terms 
of 
international 
labor-immobility 
within 
a 
sample 
of 
49 
middle 
and 
high 
income countries 
(IMD, 
2001). 
While 
it 
is 
promising 
that 
Chile 
is 
able 
to 
retain 
most 
of 
-f- 
Page  12
its 
highly 
skilled 
labor 
force, 
the 
propensity 
to 
stay 
in 
Chile 
does 
at 
the 
same 
time 
imply 
that 
knowledge 
import 
and 
export through 
international 
mobility 
is 
dismal. 
InsufJicient  relevance 
of 
research 
and  lack 
of 
cooperation. 
Chile’s 
public 
R&D 
system 
has 
been 
successful 
in 
the 
sense 
that 
Chile 
scores 
higher than other 
Latin 
American countries 
in 
terms 
of 
scientific 
publications per 
resident. 
Still, 
the added 
value 
of 
this 
research remains 
questionable 
and 
has 
been 
of 
little 
use 
to 
the 
private 
sector, 
directly 
and 
indirectly. 
The 
number 
of 
resident patent applications, 
for 
example, 
has 
remained 
low 
throughout 
the 
1990s. 
This 
reflects 
the 
inward 
orientation 
of 
the academic 
personnel, 
which 
traditionally 
has 
paid 
little 
attention to the 
needs 
of 
the 
enterprise 
sector 
(Lundvall, 
2002b). 
Symptomatically, 
cooperation 
and 
interaction 
between 
the 
stakeholders 
of 
the 
Chilean 
innovation 
system 
has 
left 
much 
to 
be desired. 
Although 
on 
the 
rise, 
partnerships 
and 
joint 
ventures 
are 
still 
not 
seen 
as 
crucial 
drivers 
for 
growth 
by 
firms 
and 
academia. 
By 
contrast, 
a 
recent survey reveals 
that 
most 
firms 
consider 
their 
own 
personnel 
to 
be 
the 
chief 
source 
of 
innovation 
(Melo, 
2001). 
Weak 
patterns 
of 
cooperation 
and 
low 
private 
sector 
activity 
translate 
into 
low 
labor 
mobility 
between 
the 
public 
and 
private 
sectors 
of 
the 
system 
and, 
in 
turn, 
insufficient diffusion 
and 
application 
of 
knowledge. 
Limited 
private 
sector 
R&D. 
Presently, 
the 
bulk 
of 
Chilean 
research 
is 
financed 
by 
the 
Government 
and 
carried 
out 
by  public 
research 
institutions 
and 
universities.  The 
private 
sector 
is 
responsible 
for 
executing 
only 
about 10% 
of 
the 
total 
R&D 
expenditures 
in 
Chile, a 
share, 
which 
is 
significantly lower 
than that 
of 
the 
private 
sector 
in 
Argentina 
(29.5%), 
Brazil 
(43.5%) 
and 
the 
United 
States 
(75%). 
Thus, 
there 
is 
a considerable 
potential 
for 
improving 
Chile’s  innovation 
system 
by 
complementing 
public 
research 
with 
a boost 
in 
private 
sector 
R&D. 
This 
constitutes a 
significant 
challenge 
and 
it 
may 
not 
be 
sufficient 
to 
reform 
the 
institutional  framework 
and 
provide 
strong economic incentives. 
A 
change 
of 
mind-set 
and 
a 
stronger 
knowledge absorption capacity 
in 
the 
private 
sector 
may also be warranted. 
Information 
and communications 
infrastructure 
A 
highly 
developed 
information 
and 
communication 
infrastructure 
reduces 
the cost 
of 
transactions 
and 
increases 
the 
flow 
of 
information, 
thereby 
supporting 
innovation 
processes 
and 
knowledge 
diffusion. 
In 
addition 
to 
being 
the communication 
and 
transaction backbone 
of 
the 
knowledge 
economy, 
information 
and 
communication  technologies 
(ICT) 
are 
in 
themselves 
key 
products/services 
that 
result 
from 
a 
knowledge 
economy. 
As 
pointed 
out 
by 
Dahlman, 
Hanna, 
Leipziger, 
Motta 
et 
al. (2003), 
an 
early 
and sustained 
commitment 
to 
ICT 
by 
the 
GoC 
has 
led 
to 
considerable results.  Chile’s 
information 
infrastructure 
in 
its 
different 
dimensions 
(service 
rollout, 
quality, 
costs, 
and 
efficiency) 
compares 
well 
with 
developed countries 
in 
the 
region 
(Argentina, 
Brazil, 
Mexico 
and 
Uruguay) and countries 
in 
the 
world 
with 
a 
similar 
income level. 
Notably, 
impressive 
growth 
rates 
in 
mobile 
telephony and the 
Internet 
has 
been 
achieved. 
As 
regards 
ICT 
skills, 
a 2001 
survey 
by 
the 
World 
Economic 
Forum 
revealed that 
Chilean 
employers regard 
the 
domestic 
supply 
of 
ICT-workers 
to 
be 
among 
the 
best 
in 
Latin 
America. 
Despite 
advances 
in 
the 
information 
and 
communications 
infrastructure 
several 
issues 
remain 
to 
be 
addressed. 
Private 
sector 
use 
of 
ICT 
such 
as 
e-commerce and 
e-business 
is 
limited, 
software 
production 
is 
low 
and 
disparities 
in 
access 
to 
ICT 
are 
deeper 
in 
Chile 
than 
in 
comparable countries, 
mainly 
because 
of 
income inequalities 
and 
low 
population 
density 
in 
rural 
areas. 
Whilst 
of 
high 
importance, 
these 
issues 
are 
not 
believed to 
be 
significantly 
hampering Chile’s 
innovation 
and knowledge 
formation 
capacity. 
Government 
strategy 
The 
Lagos administration 
focuses 
on securing 
sustainable 
and equitable 
growth 
through 
the 
provision 
of 
a conducive 
macroeconomic 
framework,  strong 
public 
institutions, 
investments 
in 
human 
capital 
and 
social 
protection. The 
private 
sector 
is 
seen 
as 
the 
driver 
of 
economic growth, 
and 
the 
public 
sector 
as 
a 
facilitator for 
growth 
in 
the 
private 
sector. 
Education 
is 
one 
of 
the  core 
elements 
in 
this 
strategy 
and  the 
GoC 
is 
consequently 
committed 
to 
-8- 
Page  13
improving 
the 
quality 
and 
relevance 
of 
education 
and 
has, 
inter 
alia, 
expressed 
the 
need 
for 
expanding 
access 
to 
tertiary 
education through 
student 
loans 
and 
scholarships. 
The 
government 
is 
also 
emphasizing 
the 
need 
for 
stimulating innovation 
and 
technological development. 
Chile’s 
investments 
in 
S&T 
doubled 
during 
the 
nineties, and 
the present 
Government 
has 
stated 
as 
its 
target 
to 
double 
the 
country’s 
S&T 
investments 
from 
0.5% 
of 
GDP to 
1.2% 
by 
the 
year 
2006. 
Chile 
currently 
features 
a 
number 
of 
funding 
mechanisms 
designed 
to 
promote 
science, 
technology 
and 
innovation, 
notably 
FONDECYT, 
FONDEF, 
FONTEC, 
FONDAP 
and 
MSI. 
They 
all 
apply 
competitive 
funding 
mechanisms, 
which 
in 
turn 
have 
stimulated a 
competitive 
culture 
in 
the 
sector. 
Despite 
the 
fact that 
the 
GoC 
has 
pledged 
to 
increase 
R&D 
spending 
significantly, 
Chile 
faces 
severe 
fiscal restraints, 
which 
in 
turn 
jeopardizes 
the 
possibilities 
for 
supporting the 
national innovation 
system 
with 
sufficient 
funds. 
It 
is 
against 
this 
background, 
the  current  program 
has 
been 
proposed 
as 
stated 
in 
the 
Letter 
of 
Development Program 
dated 
March 
31, 
2003 
attached 
as 
Annex 
13 
to 
this 
document. The 
letter 
has 
been 
endorsed 
by 
CONICYT 
in 
a 
letter 
dated 
April 
3, 
2003. 
The 
performance triggers 
for 
the 
launch 
of 
the 
program’s 
second 
phase 
has 
been 
provided 
in 
a 
letter 
of 
April 
3, 
2003 
from 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Finance. 
These 
letters 
are 
in 
the 
project 
file. 
It 
is 
of 
critical 
importance 
to 
support the current 
initiatives 
of 
the 
GoC 
by 
securing continuous 
and 
adequate 
flows 
of 
investment 
to 
the 
Chilean 
science 
base, 
strengthening 
the 
stock 
of 
human 
capital 
and 
stimulating 
the 
critical 
interfaces 
in 
the 
innovation 
system. 
Countries 
often 
fail 
to capitalize 
on 
their 
investments 
in 
S&T 
because 
of 
interruption 
of 
funding. 
The 
best 
of 
science 
cannot compete 
internationally 
in 
a 
“stop-go” 
system. 
3. 
Sector 
issues 
to 
be 
addressed 
by 
the 
project 
and 
strategic 
choices: 
Drawing 
on the 
four 
pillar 
framework 
of 
the 
knowledge-economy, the proposed 
project 
will 
address 
the 
following 
sector issues: 
(i) 
insufficient 
coherence 
of 
sector 
policies 
and strategic 
framework; 
(ii) 
lack 
of 
critical 
mass 
of 
high-quality scientific 
research 
of 
high 
relevance 
to 
society due 
to 
inadequate 
availability 
of 
R&D 
personnel and 
state-of-the-art equipment, 
and 
(iii) 
low 
research 
capacity 
in 
the 
private 
sector 
and 
poorly 
developed linkages between the 
public 
and the 
private 
sector. 
Efficient 
policy 
framework 
and 
strong 
economic 
incentives 
As 
pointed 
out 
it 
is 
paramount that a more coordinated approach 
to 
S&T 
is 
adopted 
in 
Chile. 
This 
entails 
stimulating  horizontality 
in 
government and 
improving 
the 
ability 
to 
collaborate 
across 
sectors 
to 
establish 
an 
efficient 
policy 
framework 
with 
strong economic incentives 
and feed-back 
mechanisms. 
Coherent 
strategy 
for 
innovation 
and 
human 
capital. 
In 
full 
agreement 
with 
the 
Government’s 
development 
strategy, 
it 
is 
proposed 
that 
key 
public 
and 
private 
stakeholders 
are 
brought 
together 
to 
establish a 
coherent 
policy 
framework 
in 
support 
of 
innovation 
and human 
capital 
formation. 
Priority 
will 
be 
given 
to 
strengthening 
the 
Government’s  capacity 
to 
formulate  long-term 
policy 
visions 
supported 
by 
concrete 
policy 
measures. 
Conducive innovation 
policies 
and instruments. 
Accordingly, 
it 
is 
proposed 
to 
review Chile’s 
current 
policies 
and 
instruments 
pertaining 
to 
Science 
Technology and 
Innovation. Particular 
attention 
will 
be 
paid 
to 
the 
need 
for 
a strong IPR-regime 
based 
on 
flexible 
management, 
removal 
of 
administrative 
barriers 
for 
business 
registration, 
promoting 
of 
knowledge intensive foreign direct 
investment 
and 
fiscal 
incentives 
for private 
sector 
R&D. 
Focus 
will 
also 
be 
placed 
on policies 
that support 
the 
maturation 
of 
innovative 
ideas 
and 
improve 
access 
to 
risk 
capital. 
Continual monitoring 
and evaluation. 
Finally, 
the development 
of 
a comprehensive 
policy 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation mechanism 
will 
be 
supported. Based 
on 
both 
qualitative 
and 
quantitative indicators, 
the 
objective 
is 
to 
assess 
the 
societal impact 
of 
implemented 
S&T 
initiatives 
with 
a 
view 
to 
targeting 
-9- 
Page  14
resources 
and 
improving 
effectiveness. 
High 
level 
of 
human 
capital 
The 
basic 
education 
system 
in 
Chile, 
encompassing 
primary 
and 
secondary 
education, 
has 
already 
been 
successfully 
addressed 
in 
two 
major 
World 
Bank 
projects 
(see 
World 
Bank, 1999a and 
2001), 
which 
in 
turn 
has 
contributed 
to increasing educational attainment 
as 
well 
as 
quality 
and 
relevance. 
In 
regard 
to 
tertiary 
education 
and 
lifelong 
learning 
there 
are 
two 
active 
World 
Bank 
projects: 
(i) 
MECE-SUP 
stimulates 
relevance, 
quality, 
governance 
and 
efficiency 
in 
tertiary 
education; 
and 
(ii) 
Chile 
Califica 
facilitates 
policy-coherence,  expansion 
and 
formal 
skills 
formation 
for 
the 
adult 
population. 
Notwithstanding 
these measures, 
there 
is 
a 
need 
for 
paying 
greater 
attention 
to 
expanding 
the 
stock 
of 
high-quality 
research 
personnel 
in 
Chile 
and 
increasing 
the 
supply 
of 
“hard 
skills”. 
Increasing 
the 
number 
of 
doctoral 
graduates. 
The 
quality 
of 
the 
public 
S&T 
system 
will 
be 
supported 
by 
a 
number 
of 
financially 
attractive 
PhD 
scholarships. 
Providing 
a 
sound 
financial 
basis 
for 
pursuing 
studies 
at 
the 
doctorate 
level 
is 
expected 
to 
enhance 
the 
availability 
of 
skilled 
research personnel. 
The 
scholarships 
will 
be 
distributed 
on 
a 
highly 
competitive 
basis, 
ensuring that 
the 
best 
candidates 
are 
selected 
for 
the program. 
Awareness 
and 
high 
quality 
S&T 
education. 
The 
inadequate 
supply 
of 
workers 
with 
training 
in 
natural 
and exact 
sciences such 
as 
technical workers 
and 
engineers 
is 
believed, 
in 
part, 
to 
be 
a result 
of 
low-quality 
basic 
science 
education and a 
lukewarm 
perception 
of 
hard 
sciences 
in 
the 
Chilean 
population. 
For 
this 
reason, 
the 
project 
will 
support 
efforts 
to 
improve 
science and 
technology 
awareness 
in 
the 
Chilean population 
at 
large 
and 
specifically 
in 
the 
business 
community. 
To 
supplement 
this 
effort, 
the 
Chilean 
Ministry 
of 
Education 
has 
expressed 
its 
intention 
to 
launch a 
program 
aimed 
at 
upgrading 
science 
and 
technology 
skills 
among 
teachers 
in 
basic education. 
Increased 
skills 
and 
awareness 
of 
sciences are 
expected 
to 
stimulate enrollment 
in 
S&T 
programs and 
a perception 
of 
R&D 
as 
a 
driver for 
economic growth. 
Effective 
innovation 
system 
In 
framing 
this 
project, 
due 
account 
has 
been 
taken 
of 
the 
need 
for 
public 
sector 
S&T 
activities. 
As 
pointed 
out 
in 
OECD 
(2000) 
the 
optimal level 
of 
research 
and 
development 
is 
unlikely 
to 
be 
achieved 
without 
public 
sector 
intervention, 
seeing 
that 
the 
private 
costs 
of 
conducting 
R&D 
in 
many 
cases 
outgrow 
the 
private 
benefits. 
This 
is 
particularly 
the 
case 
for 
basic 
research 
activities. 
At 
the 
same 
time, 
the 
strategy acknowledges 
the 
need 
for 
realigning 
the 
public 
sector research 
agenda 
with 
the 
needs 
of 
the 
private 
sector, 
enhancing linkages 
in 
the 
innovation 
system 
and 
improving 
the 
quality 
and 
relevance 
of 
current 
research 
(Brennan, 
1994). 
State-of-the-art scientific  community. 
The 
quality 
of 
the 
public 
S&T 
system 
will 
be 
improved 
through 
the 
support 
of 
institutions 
of 
excellence 
and research 
teams. 
This 
will 
allow 
the 
selected 
institutions 
to 
attract 
highly qualified 
researchers 
for 
long-term 
projects,  a 
necessary 
condition for 
establishing  a 
critical 
mass 
of 
scientists. 
Further, 
the 
existing scientific 
equipment 
will 
be 
upgraded 
to 
reach 
world 
standards. 
The 
awards 
of 
funds 
will 
take 
into 
account 
two 
key 
priorities: 
(i) 
the 
need 
for 
relevance, and 
(ii) 
the 
desire 
of 
an 
equitable 
regional 
development. 
Thus, 
preference 
will 
be 
given 
to 
proposals, 
which 
are 
attuned 
to 
the 
R&D 
needs 
of 
the 
private 
sector, 
and 
to 
research centers 
outside 
of 
the 
capital. 
Providing 
access 
to 
international 
knowledge networks. 
The 
international 
linkages 
of 
the 
innovation 
system 
will 
be  strengthened 
by 
explicitly  prioritizing 
the 
participation 
of 
Chilean 
researchers 
in 
international 
research ventures. 
This 
in 
turn 
will 
facilitate 
the 
flow 
of 
knowledge 
to 
and 
from 
Chile, give 
researchers 
valuable 
insight 
and 
experience, and 
help 
bring 
Chilean 
Science 
to 
the 
global 
knowledge 
frontier. 
Enhancing 
public-private 
linkages. 
Interaction 
and 
transfer 
of 
knowledge 
between 
sectors 
are 
paramount 
to 
create synergies 
in 
Chile’s 
national innovative 
system and 
increase the relevance 
of 
research 
carried 
- 
10- 
Page  15
out 
in 
public 
institutions. 
To 
enhance 
linkages, support 
will 
be 
given 
to 
a 
number 
of 
cooperative 
research 
consortia. 
These 
consortia 
will 
comprise 
both 
public 
and 
private 
researchers 
and 
will 
be 
co-financed 
by 
participating  private 
companies. 
Along  with 
new 
developments 
of 
importance 
to 
Chile’s  industry, 
cooperative 
research 
consortia 
will 
provide 
cases 
of 
good  practice 
and  hence  pave 
the 
way 
for 
cross-sectoral 
research 
collaboration 
in 
all 
sectors 
of 
the 
Chilean 
economy. 
Boosting 
private 
sector  research 
capacity. 
Making 
world-class  knowledge  and discoveries 
of 
high 
relevance available 
to 
Chilean 
companies 
is 
necessary, 
but 
not 
sufficient 
to 
bolster 
innovation 
and 
growth. 
To 
bring 
about 
first-mover 
advantages 
and 
exploit 
comparative 
advantages, 
the 
private 
sector 
must 
have 
the 
capacity 
to 
develop 
and 
commercialize products and 
services. 
To 
stimulate 
industry R&D, 
it 
is 
proposed 
to 
support 
the 
employment 
of 
young 
PhDs 
in 
Chilean 
companies 
to 
conduct 
research. 
Insertion 
grants 
are 
expected 
to 
promote a 
culture 
of 
innovation 
in 
private 
enterprises 
and 
provide 
for 
a 
substantial transfer 
of 
knowledge 
from 
the 
public 
S&T 
system 
to 
the 
private 
sector 
through the 
mobility 
of 
highly skilled 
labor. Moreover, 
experience 
from 
other countries 
shows 
that 
researchers 
benefiting 
from 
time-limited 
insertion 
grants 
are 
likely 
to 
find 
long-term 
employment 
in 
the 
private 
sector, 
leading 
to 
an 
enduring 
increase 
in 
the sector’s 
R&D 
capacity. 
High 
penetration 
of 
information 
and 
communications 
technologies 
The 
proposed 
project 
will 
not 
contain 
measures 
directly 
targeted 
at 
increasing 
the 
penetration 
of 
ICT-technologies 
in 
Chile. 
First, 
the 
private 
sector 
in 
Chile, 
in 
cooperation 
with 
foreign 
investors, 
has 
already 
been 
quite 
successful 
in 
pursuing 
this 
objective. 
Second, 
the 
GoC 
has 
committed 
itself to 
expand 
the 
number 
of 
computer-literates 
(Business 
Latin 
America, 
2001, 
p.6.). 
So 
far, 
one 
of 
the 
most 
significant 
policy 
initiatives 
is 
the 
Enlaces 
program, 
which 
is 
delivering 
computers 
and Internet connection 
to 
public 
schools. 
In 
consequence, 
almost 
75 
percent 
of 
primary 
schools and 
more 
than 
90 
percent 
of 
secondary 
schools have 
access 
to 
computers and 
the 
Internet. 
Finally, 
the 
Inter-American 
Development Bank 
(IADB) 
is 
involved 
in 
projects 
in 
this 
particular 
field 
in 
Chile. Approved 
in 
November 
2000, 
the 
IADB 
Technology Development and 
Innovation 
Program 
aims 
at 
increasing 
the 
competitiveness 
of 
the 
Chilean 
economy 
by 
supporting technological 
innovation 
and 
development 
in 
strategic 
areas 
of 
the 
national 
economy. 
Especially,  focus 
is 
given 
to 
transferring 
new 
technologies 
to 
the 
entrepreneurial 
sector, 
particularly 
among 
Chile’s 
vast 
number 
of 
SMEs. 
Out 
of 
a 
total 
investment 
of US$200 
million, 
30 
percent 
is 
earmarked 
to 
accelerate and 
encourage 
the 
introduction 
of 
information 
and 
communications technologies 
in 
the 
productive 
sector. 
4. 
Program 
description 
and 
performance triggers 
for 
subsequent 
loans: 
The 
program 
will 
consist 
of 
three 
components: 
(i) 
Improving Chile’s 
Science, 
Technology 
and 
Innovation 
System; 
(ii) 
Strengthening 
Chile’s 
Science 
Base, 
and 
(iii) 
Enhancing 
Public-Private 
Linkages. 
The 
proposed 
program 
will 
be 
implemented 
over a 
6% year 
period 
and 
consists 
of 
two 
phases. 
Phase 
I(2003-2007): 
The 
first 
phase 
will 
support the establishment 
of 
a 
coherent 
policy 
strategy and 
a 
strong 
policy 
framework. 
It 
will 
also 
provide 
for 
the 
continuation 
of 
the 
Millennium 
Science 
Initiative 
and 
a  further  strengthening 
of 
the 
science  base 
by 
scaling 
up 
funding 
for 
basic 
research, 
develop 
public-private 
linkages 
in 
Chile’s 
national 
innovative 
system  and 
enhance 
the 
R&D 
capacity 
of 
companies.

and

especially

1. 
C. 
Program 
and 
Project 
Description 
Summary 
1. 
Project 
components 
(see 
Annex 
2 
for 
a detailed description 
and 
Annex 
3 
for 
a detailed 
cost 
breakdown): 
To 
address 
the  key 
development 
objectives, 
this 
proposed  project  includes 
the 
following 
three 
components: 
(i) 
Improving 
Chile’s 
Science, 
Technology and 
Innovation 
System; 
(ii) 
Strengthening 
Chile 
’s 
Science 
Base, 
and 
(iii) 
Enhancing 
Public-Private 
Linkages. 
(i) 
Improving 
Chile’s 
Science, 
Technology 
and 
Innovation 
System. 
This 
component 
is 
designed 
to 
help 
Chile 
develop  a 
coherent 
policy 
framework 
and 
an 
enabling  environment 
for 
innovation. 
It 
will 
consolidate 
and 
modernize 
the 
country’s 
existing 
instruments 
and programs 
in 
science 
and 
technology 
to 
achieve higher levels 
of 
internal 
and 
external 
efficiency 
among 
researchers 
and 
their 
organizations and to 
nurture a more collaborative 
research 
culture 
among 
Chilean 
scientists. 
The component 
consists 
of 
three 
sub-components: 
(a) 
establishment 
of 
an 
independent 
Advisory 
Board 
and 
support 
for 
project 
management 
and 
implementation; 
(b) 
development 
of 
a 
coherent 
strategy, 
conducive polices 
and 
S&T 
awareness, 
and (c) development 
of 
a 
monitoring 
and evaluation capacity. 
This 
component 
will 
finance 
technical 
assistance  and 
capacity-building 
measures 
including 
operating 
costs 
of 
committees  and 
associated 
secretariats 
plus 
goods 
and 
services  necessary 
to 
help 
related 
groups  achieve  the 
set 
objectives. 
(ii) 
Strengthening 
Chile’s 
Science 
Base. 
Chile’s 
science 
base 
comprises 
its 
scientific 
and 
technological 
research 
personnel, 
its 
research 
infrastructure, 
and 
its 
capacity 
to 
access 
in 
a 
timely 
fashion 
the 
knowledge 
generated 
in 
other 
countries. 
This 
component 
will 
increase 
the 
capacity 
of 
the 
science 
base 
and 
accordingly contribute 
to 
Chile’s 
social 
and 
economic development 
as 
the 
country 
moves 
toward 
a 
knowledge 
economy. 
The  objectives 
will 
be 
achieved through 
three 
competitive 
programs 
for: 
(a) 
excellence 
in 
science, 
(b) 
advanced 
human capital, 
and (c) 
improvement 
the research 
infrastructure. The 
component 
will 
finance 
grants, 
scholarships and 
the 
establishment 
of 
a 
program 
for 
major  scientific 
equipment. 
(iii) 
Enhancing 
Public-Private 
Linkages. 
This 
third 
component 
will 
support 
activities 
that strengthen 
linkages 
between 
the 
Chilean 
scientific 
community, 
industry 
and 
public 
sector 
users 
of 
research 
findings 
and 
build 
private 
sector 
research 
capacity. 
This 
will 
be 
achieved through the establishment 
of 
three 
competitive 
programs 
for: 
(a) 
cooperative 
research 
consortia; 
(b) 
researchers 
in 
industry, 
and  (c) 
international 
cooperation 
in 
research.

2. 
Key 
policy 
and 
institutional 
reforms 
supported 
by 
the 
project: 
The 
proposed 
program 
will 
support 
a number 
of 
ongoing 
policy 
reforms 
in 
Chile, 
some 
of 
which 
have 
been 
initiated 
in 
consultation 
with 
the 
Bank. 
First, 
the 
program 
will 
focus on 
policy 
visions and 
the 
need 
for 
strategic 
planning 
and coherence 
across 
policy 
areas. 
It 
is 
considered 
critically 
important that the 
GoC 
and 
local 
stakeholders discuss 
and 
formulate 
a 
vision 
for 
Chile’s  future 
development, 
which 
in 
particular 
specifies 
how Chile 
can 
develop 
into 
a 
productive 
and 
competitive 
knowledge 
based 
economy. 
This 
strategy must 
be 
supported 
by 
a 
plan 
of 
action, 
with 
a clear 
division 
of 
labor, 
explicit 
milestones 
and 
the 
support 
of 
competent 
policy 
bodies. 
Accordingly, 
the 
project 
will 
not 
only 
support a 
much 
needed 
policy 
discussion 
but 
will 
also 
lend 
support 
to 
key 
Government bodies 
involved 
in 
promoting 
human capital formation, 
science, 
technology and 
innovation 
in 
Chile. 
Second, 
the 
program 
will 
focus 
on 
a  number 
of 
specific 
policies 
and 
institutions 
pertinent 
to 
the 
development 
of 
advanced 
human 
capital 
and 
a 
competitive 
innovation 
system. 
Within 
the 
framework 
of 
the 
recently completed 
Millennium 
Science 
Initiative, 
a number 
of 
initiatives 
have 
already 
been 
launched 
to 
improve 
the 
policy 
framework 
for 
science, 
technology and innovation. 
These 
initiatives  include 
the 
establishment 
of 
competitive 
funding 
mechanisms and 
a 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
process, 
which 
allows 
policymakers 
and 
other 
stakeholders 
to 
evaluate 
policy 
initiatives 
and 
trace recent 
developments 
in 
the 
sector. 
Realizing 
that 
this 
is 
a 
long-term 
process 
which 
calls 
for 
behavioral 
change at 
all 
policy 
levels, 
it 
is 
suggested 
to 
continue 
this 
effort 
within 
the proposed 
program 
by 
offering 
further 
training, 
- 
14- 
Page  19
capacity 
building 
and 
access 
to international 
fora 
for 
policy 
discussion 
for 
key 
Government 
bodies. 
3. 
Benefits 
and 
target population: 
The 
project 
has 
a 
number 
of 
tangible, immediate beneficiaries 
as 
well 
as 
long-term 
benefits. 
Conducive 
innovation policies 
and increased 
investment 
in 
R&D 
and human 
capital 
is 
expected 
to 
facilitate 
Chile’s 
transition 
to 
a 
knowledge-based economy. 
The 
improved innovative 
environment 
and 
the 
resulting 
economic 
growth 
will 
benefit 
the 
Chilean society 
at 
large. 
More 
specifically 
the 
immediate beneficiaries 
are:

Private 
companies 
benefiting

system 
and

stock 
of

capital. 
Private 
companies 
participating 
in 
cooperative 
research 
consortia 
and 
the

in

program, thereby 
enhancing their 
research 
capacity 
at 
a 
low 
cost. 
Researchers 
employed 
at 
the selected 
institutions 
of 
excellence who 
will 
benefit 
from 
improved 
working 
conditions 
and 
interaction 
with 
scientific 
peers. 
Researchers 
who 
wish 
to 
work 
together 
in 
teams 
to 
address 
large-scale 
problems. 
PhD-scholars 
and 
post-doctoral 
fellows 
benefiting 
from 
the 
industry insertion 
program. 
Researchers 
receiving 
funding 
for 
increased 
interaction 
with 
international 
colleagues. 
Students 
and 
researchers 
with 
access 
to 
state 
of 
the 
art 
scientific 
equipment. 
4. 
Institutional 
and 
implementation 
arrangements: 
Project implementation 
period for 
the 
full 
program 
will 
be 
6% 
years: 
2003-2010. 
An 
independent 
Advisory  Board 
at 
the 
government 
level 
will 
be established 
by 
Presidential 
decree. 
It 
will 
be 
chaired 
by 
the 
Minister 
of 
Education 
and 
include 
three 
other ministers (Economy, 
Agriculture 
and 
Mining), 
three 
high 
level 
representatives 
from 
the 
private 
sector, 
and 
three 
high 
representatives 
from 
the 
scientific community. 
The 
Advisory Board 
will 
(i) 
have oversight functions 
for 
the entire operation; 
(ii) 
advise 
on 
public-private 
interfaces 
in 
order to 
bring 
out 
the 
innovative potential 
of 
the 
private 
sector; 
(iii) 
and 
in 
particular provide 
guidance 
for 
the 
implementation 
of 
component 
1. 
CONICYT 
has 
been 
designated 
by 
the  government 
as 
the 
project  implementing 
agency. 
A 
project 
coordination 
team 
will 
be established 
within 
CONICYT 
to 
oversee 
the 
daily 
implementation 
of 
the 
project. 
In 
relation 
to 
project 
administration 
and 
management, 
the 
following 
criteria 
should 
be 
taken 
into 
account: 
the 
management 
should 
have 
the  capacity 
to 
operate 
a 
transparent 
competitive 
selection 
and 
allocation 
procedure, 
which 
must 
include international 
peer 
review; 
the  support 
of 
the 
best 
scientists 
in 
Chile 
must be 
flexible, 
and 
in 
accordance 
with 
a clear 
and 
effective timeline 
for 
submission 
of 
proposals, peer 
evaluation, 
allocation 
of 
funds, 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation; 
long 
term 
sustainability 
will 
depend 
on 
how 
the 
Government 
decides 
to 
move 
from 
a 
system 
of 
largely 
ad 
hoc 
procedures 
to 
a  consolidated 
management 
system, 
which 
included 
specific, 
but 
minimal 
rules 
and 
regulations 
in 
accordance 
with 
the 
project’s 
objectives; 
and 
the 
Advisory  Board 
should 
be 
maintained independently 
of 
the 
formal location 
of 
the 
project 
to 
preserve 
the 
project’s 
credibility 
and 
autonomy.

D. 
Project 
Rationale 
1. 
Project 
alternatives 
considered 
and 
reasons 
for 
rejection: 
Supply 
side 
versus 
demand 
side 
interventions. 
The 
team 
considered 
the 
possibility 
of 
increasing focus on 
demand 
side 
interventions. 
It 
was 
agreed 
to 
incorporate  a 
sub-component 
on 
the 
establishment 
of 
cooperative 
research 
consortia 
with 
significant private 
sector 
participation 
and 
a 
sub-component that 
supports the 
private 
sector 
in 
building 
adequate 
human 
capital 
for 
R&D. 
Two 
arguments 
speak 
strongly 
for 
the fact that 
the 
agreed 
action 
lines 
are 
optimal given 
the 
economic 
and 
institutional 
framework 
for 
the 
project: 
(a) 
economic: 
OECD 
and 
Bank 
research 
underlines 
that 
an 
inadequate 
level 
of 
R&D 
of 
the 
private 
sector 
can 
be 
traced back 
to 
a 
combination 
of 
low 
technological absorption capacity 
of 
domestic 
firms, 
failures 
in 
technology 
transfer 
between 
sectors, 
and 
insufficient 
high 
quality, 
relevant 
science, 
which 
is 
a prerequisite 
for 
public 
science 
and 
technology 
to 
be 
attractive 
for 
the 
private 
sector 
competing 
on a 
world 
market, 
and 
(b) 
institutional: 
as 
the 
implementing 
agency, 
the 
government 
has 
designated 
CONICYT, 
which 
has 
no 
mandate 
or 
experience 
with 
demand 
side 
interventions. 
Hitherto 
such 
interventions 
pertain 
to CORFO under 
the 
auspices 
of 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Economy, 
which 
- 
supported 
under 
an 
IADB-loan 
- 
operates  several 
funds 
directly 
financing 
firms' 
participation 
in 
research 
and 
development. 
Only 
investment 
in 
basic 
science versus 
sectoral approach. 
It 
was 
considered 
to 
continue 
the 
Millennium 
Science 
Initiative 
and 
similar 
basic 
science 
programs 
without  introducing  additional 
components. 
However, 
such 
a 
stand 
alone 
measure 
is 
not 
supported 
by 
international 
best 
practice  and 
was 
recommended against 
by 
the 
Bank's 
external evaluation 
of 
the 
global 
MSI, 
which 
called 
for 
a 
holistic 
approach encompassing 
mutual supportive 
components. 
On 
these 
grounds 
the proposal 
was 
rejected, and 
a sectoral 
component 
was 
introduced 
in 
order 
to 
coordinate 
investments, 
policies 
and 
competences 
within 
science, 
technology 
and 
innovation, 
notably 
between the 
private 
and 
public 
sector 
as 
well 
as 
within 
the  government. 
This 
component 
would 
be 
key 
for 
increased 
coordination 
and  enhanced 
regulation 
of 
the 
national innovation 
system. 
Reorient  existing  programs  and 
concentration 
of 
resources 
on 
most 
effective  instruments 
versus 
increasing 
resources 
to 
unmodified 
programs. 
Only 
increasing 
resources 
to 
unmodified 
programs 
were 
rejected on 
the 
ground 
that 
efforts 
to 
create 
knowledge 
and human capital relevant 
to 
the 
needs 
of 
the 
productive 
sector 
as 
well 
as 
interaction 
with 
the 
private 
sector 
could 
be 
strengthened. 
It 
was 
decided 
to 
reorient 
existing 
programs 
towards 
the 
above-mentioned goals among other 
things 
by including 
PhD 
scholarships and 
cooperative 
research 
consortia, 
and 
thereby 
avoiding 
the  creation 
of 
new 
funding 
instruments. 
Further, 
it 
was 
decided 
to 
target 
financing 
to 
a 
limited 
number 
of 
instruments 
which 
are 
likely to 
become 
highly 
effective, thereby 
maximizing 
impact 
and 
achieving 
economy 
of 
scale 
in 
the 
S&T 
sector. 
Further, 
this 
strategic 
choice 
will 
improve 
complementarity  and organization 
of 
funding 
instruments 
in 
the 
program 
portfolio. 
Focus 
on 
the 
innovation  capacity 
of 
SMEs. 
It 
was 
considered 
to 
focus  the 
project 
on  knowledge 
formation 
and 
innovation 
in 
SMEs, 
considering  their 
dominance 
of 
the 
private 
sector 
and 
limited 
capability 
to 
undertake 
R&D. 
The 
proposal 
was 
rejected 
due 
to 
the 
existence 
of 
several programs 
run 
by 
CORFO 
(under 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Economy) 
with 
a strong 
involvement 
of 
the 
Inter-American 
Development 
Bank, 
targeted 
at 
supporting 
technological 
innovation 
and development and 
their 
transfer 
to 
SMEs 
(IADB, 
2000).

3. 
Lessons 
learned 
and 
reflected 
in 
the 
project 
design: 
The 
proposed 
project 
takes 
a 
holistic 
approach 
to 
the 
knowledge 
economy. The 
project 
design 
reflects 
lessons 
learnt 
from 
various 
Bank 
projects 
in 
Chile, 
notably 
the 
MSI 
and 
the 
MECE-SUP 
project. 
Lessons 
have also 
been 
included 
from 
a 
recent 
review 
by 
the Operations 
Evaluation 
Department (OED) 
of 
World 
Bank 
operations 
in 
Chile 
in 
the 
period 
1985-1999: 
Chile:  Country 
Assistance 
Evaluation, 
and 
from 
the 
Bank's 
portfolio 
of 
projects 
in 
post-secondary 
education,  and 
science and 
technology 
in 
the region, 
particularly 
in 
Brazil 
and 
Mexico. 
First, 
the 
MSI 
completion 
report  and 
the 
recent 
MECE-SUP 
mid-term 
review 
stress 
the  need 
for 
sustainability. 
It 
is 
critical 
to 
consolidate the emerging 
trends 
of 
a 
competitive 
culture 
in 
Chile 
and 
ensure 
that 
scientists 
for 
example, 
have a 
time horizon sufficiently 
long 
to 
commit 
fully 
to 
the 
project. 
This 
in 
turn 
is 
a 
necessity 
for 
attracting 
a 
critical 
mass 
of 
scientists. 
It 
is 
for 
that 
reason 
that 
the 
project 
proposes 
to 
continue 
to 
support 
these 
activities. 
Second, 
several 
policy 
recommendations 
from 
"Closing 
the 
Gaps 
in 
Education 
and Technology", 
World 
- 
17- 
Page  22
Bank 
(2002), 
are 
fully 
reflected 
in 
the 
proposed 
project 
design. 
The 
most 
important 
are: 
(i) 
need 
for 
accelerating 
human 
capital  accumulation: 
(ii) 
high 
complementarity  between 
human 
slulls 
and 
technological 
development; 
(iii) 
concentration 
of 
skills 
in 
networks 
and, 
(iv) 
increase 
knowledge 
diffusion 
between 
the 
public 
research 
providers 
and 
private 
enterprises 
in 
order 
to 
boost 
private 
sector 
R&D. 
With 
regard 
to 
the 
specific program 
components, 
the 
project integrates 
numerous 
lessons 
from 
the 
MSI 
and the 
MECE-SUP 
project 
as 
well 
as 
the 
OED 
review 
of 
World 
Bank 
assistance 
to 
Chile. 
The 
need 
to 
promote 
long 
term 
strategic 
planning 
and coherent 
policy 
visions 
was 
highlighted 
in 
reviews 
of 
the 
MSI 
project, 
for 
example. 
The decision 
to 
address 
institutions 
and economic 
incentives 
is 
in 
agreement 
with 
the 
recommendations 
of 
the 
OED-report, 
which 
points 
to 
the 
need 
for 
strong 
institutions 
and economic incentives 
for 
private 
sector 
participation. 
The 
rationale 
is 
that 
the 
private 
sector, 
given 
the 
right 
circumstances, 
is 
in 
a better 
position 
than the 
public 
sector 
to 
deliver 
services and 
manage 
natural 
resources 
in 
an 
efficient 
and 
effective 
way. 
The 
high 
importance attributed 
to 
competitive 
grants 
reflects the 
positive 
evaluations 
given 
to 
these 
grants 
in 
the 
MSI 
and 
MECE-SUP 
reviews. 
Thus, 
the 
MSI 
implementation 
completion 
report 
concludes 
that 
even 
limited 
investments 
can have 
a strong impact upon performance and 
productivity 
in 
the 
S&T 
system, 
if 
funds 
are 
allocated according 
to 
transparent, 
objective 
and 
competitive criteria. 
In 
other 
words, 
applicants 
and 
other  stakeholders  have  responded 
positively 
to 
being 
judged 
on 
transparent  and 
competitive criteria, 
even 
when they 
did 
not 
qualify 
for 
funding. 
Once 
the 
World 
Bank 
has 
ensured 
that 
the 
bidding 
process 
is 
transparent and 
fair, 
it 
should 
be 
left 
to 
the 
borrower 
to 
decide 
which 
activities 
should receive 
support. 
This 
ensures 
ownership 
of 
the 
project 
and 
increases 
efficiency 
by 
reducing 
bureaucratic 
barriers. 
The 
mid-term 
review 
of 
the 
MECE-SUP  program 
likewise 
notes  that 
the 
introduction 
of 
a competitive 
fund 
has 
spurred 
a 
change 
of 
mind-set towards 
competition 
in 
the 
Chilean 
university 
sector. 
In 
consequence, 
the 
Bank 
has 
very good experience 
with 
introducing 
competitive 
funds 
in 
Chile 
and 
letting 
the 
borrower 
take 
responsibility 
for 
selection 
of 
grantees  once 
they  have 
been 
properly 
set 
up. 
The decision 
to 
include private 
sector 
insertion 
grants and 
highlight 
private 
sector relevance 
on 
the 
public 
S&T 
system 
reflects 
the 
evaluation 
of 
the 
MSI 
implementation 
completion 
report, 
which 
pointed 
out 
that 
the 
achievements 
of 
the 
Chilean S&T 
system 
had reduced 
implications 
for 
the enterprise 
sector 
and that 
the 
private 
and 
public 
sector appeared 
to 
be 
isolated 
from 
each 
other. 
Proposed components 
take 
account 
of 
experience 
from 
the 
region showing 
that 
private 
sector components 
are 
inherently 
difficult 
to 
design. 
Insufficient 
private 
sector 
R&D 
stems 
not 
primarily from 
unavailability 
of 
funds, 
but 
is 
also 
a symptom 
of 
deficient 
private 
sector 
R&D 
capacity. Projects supporting 
private 
sector 
R&D 
in 
Brazil 
and 
Mexico 
were 
recently 
restructured, 
acting 
on 
the 
fact that 
less 
than 
20 
percent 
of 
matching-funds 
were disbursed 
after 
five 
years. 
Drawing 
on 
lessons 
learned, 
this 
project 
would 
boost 
private 
sector 
R&D 
demand and 
absorption  capacity 
by 
supporting 
industry 
R&D, 
encouraging 
outreach 
activities, 
promoting 
cross-sectoral 
researcher 
mobility 
and 
fostering 
a 
culture 
of 
partnership between 
the 
public 
and 
private 
sector. 
- 
18- 
Page  23
4. 
Indications 
of 
borrower 
commitment 
and ownership: 
The 
Lagos  administration 
has 
frequently 
emphasized 
the 
need 
for 
stimulating  innovation 
and 
technological 
development. 
As 
pointed 
out 
above, 
Chile’s 
investments 
in 
S&T 
increased 
during 
the 
nineties, 
and the 
present 
Government 
has 
stated 
as 
its 
target 
to double 
the 
country’s 
S&T 
investments 
from 
0.5% 
of 
GDP 
to 
1.2% 
by 
the 
year 
2006. 
This 
strong 
backing 
was 
one 
of 
the 
crucial 
factors 
behind 
the 
highly 
satisfactory implementation 
status 
of 
the 
Millennium 
Science 
Initiative 
and 
the 
MECE-SUP 
projects.  Further, 
the 
commitment 
is 
equally 
underscored 
by 
the 
government 
of 
Chile’s 
invitation to 
the 
Bank 
to 
assist 
in 
designing 
and 
implementing 
this proposed 
follow-on 
project 
of 
the 
MSI. 
5. 
Value 
added 
of 
Bank 
support 
in 
this 
project: 
The 
World 
Bank 
has 
had 
a 
long 
and 
positive 
cooperation 
with 
Chile 
in 
various 
sectors. 
One 
of 
the 
core 
intervention 
areas 
has 
been, 
and 
still 
is, 
human 
capital 
and 
education. 
Hence, 
the proposed 
project 
lies 
in 
continuation 
of 
previous Bank 
operations 
in 
Chile 
and 
capitalizes, 
as 
pointed 
out 
above, 
on 
the 
many 
lessons 
which 
the 
bank 
has 
accrued over 
time. 
In 
consequence 
of 
this 
long 
and 
fruitful 
engagement 
in 
Chile, 
the 
Bank 
has 
established 
a valuable 
network 
and 
has 
made 
contacts 
with 
most stakeholders 
in 
the 
Chilean education 
and 
science 
and 
technology 
sectors. 
This 
in 
turn 
gives 
the 
bank a unique 
opportunity 
to 
be 
at 
the 
forefront 
of 
developments 
in 
Chile 
and 
correctly 
address 
the 
needs 
for 
assistance 
within 
these 
sectors. 
At 
a more 
general 
level, 
the 
Bank benefits 
from 
its 
extensive experience 
in 
supporting 
human 
capital 
development 
and 
S&T 
in 
various 
countries. 
Thus, 
the 
Bank 
is 
able 
to 
draw 
on 
a 
vast 
reservoir 
of 
experience 
and 
consequently 
put 
the 
most 
effective 
solutions 
in 
place. 
As 
a 
case 
of 
best 
practice, 
components 
developed 
for 
this 
project 
are expected 
to 
inspire 
other 
countries 
in 
the 
region 
to 
take 
similar 
steps 
to 
embrace 
the 
knowledge economy. 
Involvement 
of 
the 
Bank 
will 
facilitate 
the 
dissemination 
of 
lessons 
learned 
in 
Chile. 
In 
particular,  the 
Bank 
will 
be 
able 
to 
advise 
countries 
currently 
involved 
in 
science 
and 
technology projects 
in 
the 
region (Mexico, 
Colombia, 
Brazil 
and 
Venezuela) and elsewhere 
in 
regard 
to 
coherence 
of 
S&T 
policies, 
monitoring 
and 
stimulation 
of 
private 
sector 
innovation. 
E. 
Summary 
Project 
Analysis 
(Detailed 
assessments 
are 
in 
the 
project 
file, 
see

8) 
1. 
Economic 
(see 
Annex 
4): 
0 
Cost 
benefit 
0 
Cost 
effectiveness 
Other (specify) 
NPV=US$ 
million;

% 
(see 
Annex 
4) 
First, 
the 
economic 
annex 
argues 
based 
on  economic 
history, 
growth-accounting 
exercises 
and 
case-studies 
that 
the 
ability 
of 
a society  to 
understand, 
produce, 
adapt, 
diffuse, 
and 
commercialize 
scientific 
and 
technological knowledge 
is 
critical for 
continuous 
productivity 
improvements, 
sustained 
economic 
growth 
and 
improved 
quality 
of 
life. 
Second, 
the analysis 
discusses 
market failures, 
such 
as 
the 
high 
uncertainty 
of 
outcomes, 
information 
symmetries, 
appropriability 
problems, 
economies 
of 
scale, 
need 
for 
knowledge 
diffusion 
and 
existence 
of 
non-sellable knowledge, 
which 
rationalizes 
the 
large 
sums 
that developed countries invest 
in 
Science, 
Technology 
and 
Innovation-in 
many 
cases 
up 
to 
1 
percent 
of 
GDP-to 
benefit  optimally 
from 
knowledge transfer and creation. 
- 
19- 
Page  24
Third, 
it 
is 
shown 
that 
R&D 
investments 
attract substantial social 
return 
that range 
from 
20 
percent 
to 
100 percent decreasing 
with 
level 
of 
development and 
increasing 
with 
the reliance 
of 
natural 
resources 
in 
the economy. 
For 
a country 
with 
the 
income 
per 
capita 
of 
Chile, 
the return 
is 
estimated 
to 
be 
higher 
than 
50 
percent. 
Fourth, 
the 
annex presents 
empirical 
economic 
analysis on the 
determinants 
of 
the 
production 
of 
national 
innovation 
systems. 
These 
investigations 
find 
that 
a country’s 
science 
base 
plays a 
crucial 
role for 
innovation 
through 
three 
factors: 
(i) 
Overall 
R&D 
investments; 
(ii) 
Quality 
of 
basic 
science, 
and 
(iii) 
Advanced human capital.  Private-public 
collaboration 
is 
found 
to 
influence, 
but 
not 
always statistically 
significantly,  commercial knowledge 
creation. 
Further, 
educational attainment, specific factors related 
to 
Latin 
America 
and ICT-penetration 
are 
found not 
to directly 
impact innovative activity. 
The 
proposed 
program 
is 
fully 
aligned 
with 
these 
findings. 
The 
analysis 
benefited tremendously 
from 
economic 
research 
by 
Maloney 
and 
Lederman (forthcoming) 
on 
the 
national innovation 
systems 
in 
Latin 
America 
and the Caribbean. 
2. 
Financial 
(see 
Annex 
4 
and 
Annex 
5): 
NPV=US$ 
million; 
FRR 
= 
% 
(see 
Annex 
4) 
The project 
is 
expected 
to 
invest 
US$ 
50 
million 
in 
Science, 
Technology 
and 
Innovation 
over 
a 3% 
year 
period. 
This 
will 
raise 
total 
Science, 
Technology and 
Innovation 
spending 
from 
the 
current 
level 
of 
US$ 
450 
million 
in 
2001 
(0.5 
percent 
of 
GDP) 
to 
approximately 
US$ 
500 
million 
per 
year. 
Per 
capita 
spending 
will 
rise 
from 
US$ 
29.0 
in 
2001 to 
US$ 
33.3, 
which 
compares 
to 
US$ 
41.7, 
36.1 
and 917 
in 
Brazil, Argentina 
and 
USA, 
respectively. 
Given, 
the 
above 
reported 
estimates 
of 
returns 
to 
investments 
in 
science and 
technology 
of 
approximately 
50 
percent, 
it 
is 
found 
that 
the 
project 
has 
a 
small 
and 
manageable short 
term 
negative fiscal 
impact, 
and 
potentially 
a 
large long-term 
positive 
impact 
on 
the 
country’s 
gross 
national 
product, 
tax 
base 
and 
government 
revenue. 
3. 
Technical: 
The 
technical content 
of 
the 
project 
has 
been 
extensively 
reviewed 
in 
several 
Bank 
studies 
of 
Chile’s 
national 
innovation 
system. 
In 
order 
to 
effectively 
address 
technical 
issues 
in 
each 
component, 
state 
of 
the 
art  instruments 
have 
been 
identified 
on  the 
basis 
of 
international 
best 
practice. 
Some 
of 
the 
instruments, 
which 
have been 
given 
special attention, 
are: 
Competitive 
funds. 
An 
open, 
competitive 
and 
merit-based 
allocation 
of 
funds 
is 
essential 
to 
ensuring 
quality 
of 
research 
and confidence 
in 
the 
selection 
process. 
Application 
of 
this 
instrument 
builds 
on 
the practices 
established 
by 
the 
Millennium 
Science 
Initiative. 
Cooperative 
research 
consortia.  Supporting  and 
formalizing 
cross-sectoral 
research 
is 
broadly 
recognized 
as 
a  method 
for 
getting  public-private 
research 
collaboration 
off 
the 
ground. 
The 
instrument 
is 
expected 
to 
foster a 
culture 
of 
partnership 
in 
the 
Chilean scientific 
community and 
offer 
an 
opportunity 
for 
Chile’s 
high 
number 
of 
SMEs 
to 
participate 
in 
cutting-edge 
research. 
Researcher 
insertion program. Support 
of 
science-industry human 
capital 
mobility 
schemes has 
been 
found 
to 
strengthen 
the 
flow 
and 
uptake 
of 
research 
by 
the 
private 
sector 
and 
improve 
employment 
possibilities 
for 
young 
researchers. 
In 
Chile,  the instrument 
will 
provide 
a 
much-needed 
boost 
to 
private 
sector 
knowledge absorption 
and 
industry 
research 
capacity.

4. 
Institutional: 
4.1 
Executing 
agencies: 
CONICYT, 
the 
National 
Commission 
for 
Science 
and 
Technology, 
which 
is 
under 
the 
auspices 
of 
Ministry 
of 
Education, 
has 
been chosen 
by 
the 
Government 
as 
the 
implementing 
agency. 
CONICYT 
has 
a 
well-structured 
and 
efficient 
organization 
and 
has 
long 
experience 
in 
implementing 
programs 
in 
the 
science 
and 
technology 
field. 
The complete organigram 
of 
CONICYT 
is 
in 
the 
project 
file. 
Given 
the 
objective 
of 
redirecting 
science 
towards 
areas 
of 
economic importance, 
CONICYT 
will 
be guided 
by 
an 
Independent 
Advisory 
Board 
established 
to 
have 
oversight 
of 
the 
project 
(see 
C.4). 
4.2 
Project 
management: 
The administration 
and management 
of 
the 
project 
is 
conducted 
by 
CONICYT. 
The implementing 
agency 
is 
responsible 
for 
collecting 
all 
relevant 
information 
from 
universities 
and 
other awarded 
research 
entities 
and 
submitting 
consolidated reports 
to 
the 
World 
Bank. 
4.3 
Procurement 
issues: 
A 
procurement capacity 
assessment was 
carried out 
of 
the 
proposed organization 
and 
staffing 
of 
the 
CONICYT 
as 
well 
as 
the project  implementation 
arrangements; 
both 
were deemed 
satisfactory. The 
implementing 
agency 
will 
be 
responsible 
for 
carrying 
out 
and/or 
exercising 
quality 
control 
over 
all 
procurement 
activities 
and 
for 
technical coordination, planning, 
monitoring 
and evaluation. The 
Project 
Operational 
Manual 
will 
include 
procurement 
procedures. 
No 
special  procurement  problems 
are 
anticipated 
while 
implementing 
the 
project. 
CONICYT 
will 
receive technical 
assistance 
from 
the 
Bank 
on 
procurement 
procedures. 
4.4 
Financial 
management 
issues: 
The 
implementing 
agency, 
CONICYT, 
has 
extensive 
prior 
experience 
implementing 
S&T 
projects, 
including 
development 
projects 
(IADB

with 
a cadre 
of 
high level 
professional 
staff 
warrant 
satisfactory 
arrangements 
for 
the

management 
concerns 
of 
this 
new project. 
CONICYT’s 
present 
organization structure 
responds 
to 
the 
multiple 
activities 
it 
carries out 
in 
the 
fields 
of 
science 
and 
technology. 
CONICYT 
has 
not 
had 
direct 
experience 
with 
Bank 
financed  projects. 
Nevertheless, 
during project 
preparation 
and 
at 
appraisal, 
staff 
assigned 
to 
the 
financial 
management 
area 
of 
the 
new 
project 
quickly 
grasped 
the 
essentials 
on 
the 
subject, 
thus 
anticipating 
an 
effective 
and 
efficient 
operation 
in 
this 
regard 
during 
project 
implementation. 
The 
additional workload 
stemming 
from 
the 
new operation may 
cause 
delays 
in 
the administrative 
and 
financial 
processes. 
As 
a 
part 
of 
CONICYT’s 
continuous 
efforts 
to 
rationalize 
the 
organizational 
structure 
and 
in 
agreement 
reached 
with 
the 
Bank 
during 
negotiations, the 
management 
of 
CONICYT 
is 
reviewing 
the 
function 
of 
the 
Finance  and 
Administration 
Department 
with 
a 
view  to 
increasing 
efficiency, 
assuring 
adequate 
staffing 
and 
improving 
the 
integration 
of 
the  Department 
into 
the 
organization. 
These 
efforts 
will 
be 
closely monitored 
by 
the 
Bank 
team. 
During 
the 
FM 
Assessment, 
agreement 
was 
reached 
on the

for

FMRs, 
which

purposes 
only, 
not

at

of

for

(see

Social: 
6.1 
Summarize 
key social 
issues 
relevant 
to 
the 
project 
objectives, and specify the project's social 
development 
outcomes. 
The 
project 
is 
expected 
to 
reinforce 
and consolidate 
the 
social 
learning 
stemming 
from 
the 
MSI 
project 
with 
respect 
to 
the 
operation 
of 
fair 
and 
open 
competition 
for 
resources, 
merit-based 
awards 
and 
transparent 
administration. 
The 
MSI 
project 
has 
created 
a general intolerance 
for 
the 
traditional, 
less 
efficient 
means 
of 
awarding 
and 
administering 
grants. 
This, 
in 
turn, 
has 
lead 
to 
the 
adoption  of 
competitive 
principles 
elsewhere 
in 
the 
Chilean scientific 
community. 
Focusing on 
cross-sectoral 
linkages 
is 
expected 
to 
have far-reaching 
ramifications 
for 
the 
value 
attributed 
to 
public-private 
cooperation 
among 
researchers. 
Highlighting 
the value 
and necessity 
of 
cooperation 
is 
likely 
to 
foster a 
culture 
of 
collaboration 
and a 
greater 
acceptance 
among 
researchers 
of 
the 
need 
to 
engage 
in 
outreach 
activities. 
Hence, 
the 
project 
is 
expected 
to 
bolster 
the 
emergence 
of 
an 
open 
public 
- 
22 
- 
Page  27
research 
environment, 
which 
places 
higher 
priority 
on 
the 
needs 
of

sector. 
Supporting  improvements 
in 
basic 
science 
education 
and

is 
anticipated 
to 
raise 
awareness 
of 
S&T 
and 
its 
importance 
for

among the

and members 
of 
Chile's

The 
likely

include a

to

enrollment

The

to 
entry

as
