Participatory 
Approach:

has

important inputs

Third,

for 
2003, 
where

(scientists,

and

The

of

to

will 
subsequently 
allow 
CONICYT 
to

in

will

valuable  evaluative

stakeholders 
of

S&T 
sector.

by 
the 
establishment 
of 
Cooperative 
Research 
Consortia. 
Bringing 
together 
researchers 
from 
universities,  government 
laboratories 
and 
private industry 
or public-sector 
agencies, 
in 
collaborative 
arrangements 
is 
expected 
to 
provide 
a 
solid 
framework 
for 
development and education 
activities 
that 
achieve outcomes 
of 
national 
economic 
and 
social significance. 
The 
formation 
of 
skills 
and 
awareness 
of 
sciences 
will 
be 
supported 
by 
a program aimed 
at 
updating 
the 
professional 
level 
and 
pedagogical 
methods 
of 
science 
teachers 
in 
basic education. 
Moreover, 
funded 
research 
institutions 
will 
be required 
to 
engage 
in 
outreach 
activities 
targeting 
the 
Chilean 
population 
at 
large 
and 
private 
businesses 
in 
particular.

How

Page  28
A 
wide

instruments 
will 
be 
used 
to 
monitor 
performance. 
First,

number 
of 
quantitative

will 
be 
used 
to 
assess 
impact.

an 
annual

will 
be 
conducted, 
aiming 
at 
evaluating 
the 
function, administration 
and 
financing 
of 
the 
project. 
Finally, 
a

of 
independent 
expert 
scientists 
will

qualitative 
evaluations 
of 
research 
institutions 
and 
the 
project 
in 
general 
(see 
annex 
1 
and 
11).

Annex 
2: 
Detailed Project Description 
CHILE: 
Science 
for 
the 
Knowledge 
Economy 
To 
address 
the  key 
development 
objectives, 
this 
proposed 
project 
includes 
the 
following 
three 
components: 
(i) 
Improving 
Chile’s 
Science, 
Technology and 
Innovation 
System; 
(ii) 
Strengthening 
Chile 
’s 
Science 
Base, 
and 
(iii) 
Enhancing 
Public- 
Private 
Linkages 
By 
Component: 
Project 
Component 
1 
- 
US$6.40 
million 
(Phase 
2: 
$2.3 
million) 
Improving 
Chile’s 
Science, 
Technology 
and 
Innovation 
System. 
This 
component 
consists 
of 
three 
sub-components: 
0 
0 
0 
Oversight, Management 
and 
Implementation 
- 
$1.1 
million 
($0.9 
million 
in 
phase 
2) 
Strategy, 
Policies 
and Awareness 
for 
Innovation 
and 
Human Capital 
- 
$4.4 
million 
($0.9 
million 
in 
phase 
2) 
Monitoring 
and 
Evaluation 
Capacity 
- 
$0.9 
million 
($0.9 
million 
in 
phase 
2) 
This 
component 
is 
designed 
to 
strengthen 
help 
Chile’s 
policy 
framework 
and 
environment 
for 
innovation. 
Consolidating 
and 
modernizing existing 
instruments 
and 
programs 
in 
science 
and technology 
will 
create 
the 
conditions 
for 
achieving 
high 
levels 
of 
internal 
and external 
efficiency 
among 
researchers 
and 
a 
collaborative 
research 
culture 
among Chilean scientists. 
This 
first 
component consists 
of 
three 
distinct 
sub-components: 
(a) 
project 
oversight, 
management 
and 
implementation; 
(b) 
development 
of 
a 
coherent 
strategy, 
conducive 
policies 
and 
S&T 
awareness, 
and 
(c) 
development 
of 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
capacity. 
The 
component 
will 
finance 
technical 
assistance 
and 
capacity-building 
measures 
including 
operating 
costs 
of 
committees 
and 
associated 
secretariats 
as 
well 
as 
goods 
and 
services 
necessary 
to 
help related 
groups 
achieve 
the 
set 
objectives. 
Sub-component 
1 
- 
Oversight, Management 
and 
Implementation 
This 
sub-component 
will 
support 
capital 
and 
operating 
expenses 
for 
project 
oversight, 
management 
and 
implementation. 
An 
independent 
Advisory  Board 
at 
the  government 
level 
will 
be 
established 
by 
Presidential 
decree. 
It 
will 
be 
chaired 
by 
the 
Minister 
of 
Education 
and 
include 
three other 
ministers 
(Economy, 
Agriculture 
and 
Mining), 
three 
high 
level 
representatives 
from 
the 
private 
sector, 
and three 
high 
representatives 
from 
the 
scientific 
community. 
The Advisory Board 
will 
(i) 
have oversight functions 
for 
the 
entire operation; 
(ii) 
advise 
on 
public-private 
interfaces 
in 
order 
to 
bring 
out  the 
innovative 
potential 
of 
the 
private 
sector; 
(iii) 
and 
in 
particular 
provide 
guidance 
for 
the 
implementation 
of 
component 
1. 
CONICYT 
has 
been 
chosen 
as 
the 
project implementing 
agency. 
A 
project 
coordination 
team 
will 
be 
established 
within 
CONICYT 
to 
oversee 
the 
daily 
implementation 
of 
the  project. 
The 
sub-component 
will 
finance additional 
costs 
for 
consultants, 
minor 
ofjcice 
equipment, 
and 
travel 
costs 
associated 
with 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
project. 
Sub-component 
2 
- 
Strategy, 
Policies 
and 
Awareness 
for 
Innovation 
and Human 
Capital 
Formation 
This 
sub-component 
will: 
(i) 
assist 
the GoC 
and 
the relevant 
stakeholders 
to 
develop 
an 
overall vision 
and  strategy 
for 
Chile’s 
path 
toward 
a  more  knowledge-based  economy. 
This 
would 
include 
the 
formulation 
of 
a 
coherent 
policy 
framework 
for 
expanding human capital, expanding 
research 
capacity 
in 
science and 
technology, 
and 
fostering 
a 
culture 
of 
innovation 
among 
scientific 
researchers 
in 
Chile; 
(ii) 
roll 
out the 
S&T 
strategy 
developed 
by 
the 
aforementioned 
activities 
by 
transforming 
it 
into 
concrete 
- 
35 
- 
Page  40
policy 
modifications. 
Policy 
instruments 
and 
activities 
will 
include 
not 
only 
supply but 
also demand 
mechanisms 
aimed 
at 
enhancing 
innovation 
and 
knowledge absorption 
in 
all 
sectors. 
Areas 
of 
focus 
may 
include Intellectual 
Property Rights 
(with 
particular 
emphasis 
on 
patenting), 
tax 
incentives, 
risk 
capital, 
seed 
funding, 
firm 
creation, 
commercialization 
of 
products and 
processes, 
business 
registration 
and 
licensing,  technology 
transfer, 
spin 
offs,  technology forecasting 
as 
well 
as 
analyses 
and suggestions 
regarding 
national 
science and 
technology 
policy 
and 
budget; 
and 
(iii) 
improve 
the 
general basis 
for 
technological penetration 
by 
enhancing 
S&T 
awareness 
throughout 
the 
Chilean society 
and 
in 
particular 
within 
the 
business 
sector. 
In 
addition,  this 
sub-component 
will 
support 
Chile’s 
participation 
in 
the 
OECD 
Committee 
for 
Science, 
Technology 
and 
Industry 
to 
improve 
the 
country’s 
exposure 
other 
innovation 
regimes  and 
enhance 
access 
to 
international 
best 
practice 
in 
the 
area 
of 
ST&I. 
This 
sub-component 
will 
finance 
the 
cost 
of 
commissioned  studies, 
consultants,  workshops, 
travel 
and 
disseminating 
activities. 
Sub-component 
3 
- 
Monitoring 
and 
Evaluation 
Capacity 
This 
sub-component 
will 
develop a 
framework 
for 
collection 
of 
data 
necessary 
to 
trace 
the 
effect 
of 
strategy 
implementation. 
The 
OECD 
“Frascati and 
Oslo 
manuals” together 
with 
an 
established 
set 
of 
qualitative 
and 
quantitative indicators 
will 
serve 
as 
a major 
point 
of 
reference 
(see 
Annex 
11). 
Data 
and 
analysis 
provided 
through 
this 
M&E 
initiative 
will 
be 
shared 
among government 
agencies 
and 
key 
stakeholders 
to 
facilitate 
an 
informed 
policy 
discussion 
and 
inform 
decision 
makers 
as 
well 
as 
the general 
public. 
This 
sub-component 
will 
finance 
the 
cost 
of 
upgrading 
Chile’s 
M&E 
system, 
consultants, 
necessary 
software 
and 
hardware. 
Project 
Component 
2 
- 
US$19.50 
million 
(Phase 
2: 
$21.9 
million) 
Strengthening 
Chile’s 
Science Base. 
This 
component consists 
of 
three sub-components: 
0 
0 
0 
Excellence 
in 
Science- 
$9.6 
millions 
($1 
1.7 
million 
in 
phase 
2) 
Advanced Human Capital- 
$4.7 
millions 
($6.1 
million 
in 
phase 
2) 
State 
of 
the 
Art 
Equipment 
for 
Science and 
Technology- 
$5.2 
millions 
($4.2 
million 
in 
phase 
2) 
Chile’s 
science 
base 
comprises 
its 
scientific 
and  technological 
research  personnel, 
its 
research 
infrastructure, and 
its 
capacity 
to 
access 
in 
a 
timely 
fashion the knowledge 
generated 
in 
other 
countries. 
This 
component 
will 
increase 
the 
capacity 
of 
the 
science 
base 
and 
accordingly 
contribute  to Chile’s 
social 
and 
economic development 
as 
the 
country 
moves 
toward 
a knowledge economy. 
The 
component 
comprises three 
competitive  programs 
for: 
(a) 
research 
institutions 
of 
excellence 
and team grants 
for 
advanced  research 
(b) 
doctoral 
scholarships, 
and 
(c) 
acquisition, 
maintenance 
and 
efficient 
use 
of 
state-of-the-art 
equipment. 
All 
funded 
research 
institutions 
and 
teams 
will 
be 
required 
to 
engage 
in 
outreach activities targeting 
S&T 
awareness 
in 
the general 
population 
and 
cooperation 
with 
the 
business 
community. 
The component 
will 
finance 
grants, scholarships and the establishment 
of 
a 
program 
for 
major scientific 
equipment. 
Sub-component 
1 
- 
Excellence 
in 
Science 
This 
sub-component 
will 
provide 
funding 
through 
grants to: 
(i) 
Institutes 
and Centers 
of 
Excellence 
( 
Institute 
grants) 
through 
open 
competitive 
selection rounds 
to 
choose 
and 
fund 
the 
start-up 
of 
several 
new institutions.  The 
support 
for 
each 
institution 
will 
be 
programmed 
for 
five 
(5) 
to 
ten 
(IO) 
years 
with 
an 
initial 
funding 
period 
of 
five 
years; 
and 
(ii) 
Advanced 
Research 
Teams 
(Team 
Grants) 
who 
will 
work 
together 
to 
address 
large-scale research 
themes. 
This 
element 
builds 
on 
lessons 
learned 
from both 
MSI 
“Grants 
to 
Nuclei” 
and 
FONDECYT 
“Complementary 
Research 
Lines 
Grants”. 
The 
competition 
will 
be 
- 
36 
- 
Page  41
conducted through a 
similar 
competitive 
review 
process. 
Eligibility 
for 
competition 
will 
be 
limited 
to 
proposals 
for 
research 
teams 
dedicated 
to 
(1) 
research 
in 
science and 
technology, 
and (2) 
research 
in 
social 
science 
that 
is 
relevant 
to 
enhancing 
the 
contribution 
of 
S&T 
to 
Chile’s  economic 
development. 
This 
sub-component 
finances competitive grants 
to 
Institutes and 
Centers 
of 
Excellence, and 
Advanced 
Research 
Teams. 
Sub-component 
2 
-Advanced 
Human 
Capital 
This 
sub-component 
will 
provide 
funding 
through 
open 
competition 
for 
scholarships 
to 
doctoral 
students 
undertaking 
their 
studies 
in 
high 
quality 
research 
environments. 
Eligibility 
will 
be 
limited 
to 
students 
undertaking 
studies 
in 
S&T 
or 
in 
social 
science 
that 
is 
relevant 
to 
enhancing 
the 
contribution 
of 
S&T 
to 
Chile’s 
economic development. 
This 
sub-component 
finances scholarships. 
Sub-component 
3 
- 
State-ofthe-Art  Equipment 
for 
Science 
and Technology 
This 
sub-component 
will 
establish a program 
for 
the 
acquisition, 
maintenance and 
efficient 
use 
of 
state-of-the-art 
equipment by: 
(i) 
establishing a 
database 
of 
major 
research equipment; 
and 
(ii) 
providing 
competitive 
grants 
for 
acquisition 
of 
major 
equipment. 
Priority 
will 
be 
given 
to 
equipment related 
to 
areas 
considered essential 
to 
Chile’s 
economic 
development and 
to 
equipment 
that, 
through cooperative 
agreements 
between 
institutions, 
can 
achieve 
economies 
of 
scale 
and 
avoid duplication 
of 
large 
capital 
investments 
for 
scientific 
research. 
This 
sub-component 
will 
finance 
the 
development 
of 
a 
database 
and 
competitive 
grants 
for 
the 
acquisition 
of 
major equipment. 
Project 
Component 
3 
- 
US$22.60 
million 
(Phase 2: $24.7 
million) 
Enhancing 
Public-Private 
Linkages. 
This 
component consists 
of 
three 
sub-components: 
0 
0 
0 
Cooperative 
Research 
- 
$1 
1.7 
millions 
($15.7 
million 
in 
phase 
2) 
Researchers 
in 
Industry 
- 
$6.9 
millions 
($6.4 
million 
in 
phase 
2) 
International Cooperative 
Research 
- 
$4 
millions 
($2.6 
million 
in 
phase 
2) 
This 
third 
component 
will 
support 
activities 
that 
strengthen 
linkages  between 
the 
Chilean 
scientific 
community,  industry  and 
public 
sector 
users 
of 
research 
findings, 
and 
between 
Chile 
and 
the 
global 
research 
and  business 
communities. 
This 
will 
be  accomplished through 
the 
establishment 
of 
three 
competitive 
programs 
for: 
(i) 
cooperative 
research 
consortia and 
teams; 
(ii) 
researchers 
in 
industry; 
and 
(iii) 
international cooperative 
research. 
The 
component 
will 
finance 
grants, 
full 
and 
partial 
scholarships, 
and 
additional 
costs 
associated 
with 
intemational 
cooperation 
in 
research. 
Sub-component 
1 
- 
Cooperative 
Research 
This 
sub-component 
will 
provide  funding  through 
grants 
to: 
(i) 
large 
groups 
(Cooperative 
Research 
Consortia) 
of 
researchers; 
and 
(ii) 
research 
groups 
of 
more 
modest 
size 
(Cooperative 
Research 
Team) 
from 
universities,  government  laboratories,  and 
private  industry 
or 
regional 
development 
agencies, 
undertaking 
collaborative 
research 
and development, 
and research 
training, 
in 
areas 
of 
importance 
to 
industry 
and 
the 
regions 
of 
Chile. 
The 
private  industry 
and 
public 
sector  agencies 
involved 
in 
the 
consortia 
and 
teams 
will 
be 
required 
to 
make substantial 
in-kind 
and 
cash 
commitments 
to 
support the 
cooperative activities.  Consortia  and 
teams 
receiving 
grants 
will 
be 
required 
to 
allocate 
funds 
for 
outreach 
activities 
as 
described 
in 
Component 
2. 
This 
sub-component 
will 
finance 
grants 
to 
Cooperative 
Research 
Consortia and 
Teams. 
Sub-component 
2 
- 
Researchers 
in 
Industry 
This 
sub-component 
will 
expand 
the 
stock 
of 
high-quality 
research personnel 
in 
Chilean 
industry 
by 
- 
37 
- 
Page  42
awarding on a competitive 
basis: 
(i) 
Scholarships 
to 
doctoral 
students 
who 
undertake a 
substantial 
part 
of 
their 
thesis 
work 
in 
industry. 
A 
staff 
member 
of 
the 
company 
will 
be 
an 
Associate Supervisor 
of 
the 
student and 
the company 
will 
be 
required to 
contribute 
to a 
small 
supplement 
to 
the 
scholarship; 
and 
(ii) 
partial 
scholarships 
to 
post-doctoral 
or 
other 
early 
career  researchers 
who 
will 
undertake 
research 
in 
industry. 
These 
latter 
scholarships 
will 
be temporary 
and 
decline 
in 
value 
of 
time, 
with 
the company 
taking 
an 
increasing 
share 
of 
the 
researchers 
salary. 
This 
sub-component 
will 
provide 
full 
and 
partial 
scholarships. 
Sub-component 
3 
- 
International Cooperative 
Research 
This 
sub-component 
will 
aid 
the 
Chilean innovation 
system 
by 
enhancing 
international 
collaboration 
of 
some 
of 
the 
best 
national 
research 
groups and 
industry. 
Moreover, recent 
international 
agreements 
with 
the 
EU 
and the 
US 
will 
likely 
stimulate 
demand 
for 
local 
research 
capacity. 
In 
the 
competitive 
selection 
process, 
strong preference 
will 
be 
given 
to 
research 
that 
will 
involve 
participation 
by 
Chilean industry. 
This 
sub-component 
will 
finance 
the 
additional 
costs 
associated 
with 
participation 
by 
Chilean 
researchers and 
industry 
in intemational 
research 
projects. 
- 
38 
- 
Page  43
Annex 
2(B): 
Monitoring and Evaluation Framework 
CHILE: 
Science 
for 
the Knowledge Economy 
This 
annex  establishes 
a 
framework 
for 
monitoring 
and 
evaluating 
the  Science 
for 
the 
Knowledge 
Economy project 
in 
Chile 
with 
a 
view to improving 
implementation 
and 
impact. The objective 
is 
to 
(i) 
measure 
the 
outcome; 
(ii) 
assess 
the 
need 
for 
corrective 
action; 
and 
(iii) 
collect 
lessons learned. 
Measuring 
the 
impact 
of 
project 
components 
on Chile’s 
national innovation 
system 
and 
stock 
of 
human 
capital 
is 
a  complex 
matter. 
No 
measure 
alone  captures 
all 
aspects. 
Consequently, 
a  number 
of 
quantitative 
and 
qualitative 
performance indicators 
will 
be 
used 
in 
combination 
to 
monitor 
and evaluate 
implementation and impact. 
In 
line with 
the 
evaluation practice 
installed 
by 
the 
MSI-project, qualitative 
evaluations 
of 
Institutions 
of 
Excellence 
and 
the 
project 
in 
general 
will 
be carried out 
by 
a panel 
of 
independent expert scientists. 
Experts 
will 
conduct site-visits on 
an 
annual 
basis 
and subsequently 
submit a 
written 
evaluation report. 
To 
get 
a 
full 
impact 
assessment 
by 
key 
stakeholders, 
annual 
surveys 
of 
Chilean 
firms 
and researchers 
will 
be 
conducted. 
The 
survey 
of 
researchers 
will 
aim 
at 
evaluating the function, 
regulation 
and 
financing 
of 
science 
in 
Chile.  Likewise, 
the 
survey 
of 
firms 
will 
measure 
the 
perceived 
relevance 
of 
conducted 
research and 
obstacles 
to 
increased 
co-operation 
with 
science 
institutions.  Preferably, 
the 
firm 
survey 
will 
be attached 
to 
an 
existing 
survey 
of 
Chilean 
firms. 
For both 
surveys, 
the 
application 
of 
recognized 
sampling 
techniques 
will 
ensure 
collection 
of 
a representative 
sample, 
which 
in 
turn 
will 
allow 
general 
conclusions 
to 
be drawn. 
A 
number 
of 
quantitative performance indicators 
will 
equally 
be 
utilized 
to 
assess 
impact. Rather than 
focusing 
on 
inputs, indicators 
are 
developed 
to 
measure 
outputs 
and 
societal effects 
that 
stem 
from 
the 
implementation 
of 
the 
project and 
its 
components. Emphasis 
is 
given 
to 
create 
clear, 
balanced, 
relevant 
and 
measurable 
indicators 
for 
each 
component 
in 
the 
project 
with 
a 
view 
to 
(i) 
aligning 
goal achievement 
and 
incentives; 
(ii) 
ensuring 
accountability; 
and 
(iii) 
improving 
the 
basis 
for 
management 
decisions 
and 
policy 
making. 
This 
annex 
provides a 
gross 
list 
of 
possible indicators, 
which 
may 
form 
the 
backbone 
of 
the 
indicator 
selection 
process. 
The 
final 
M&E 
framework 
including 
baselines 
and targets 
for 
indicators 
will 
be 
established 
in 
the 
Operational 
Manual 
prior 
to 
effectiveness. 
Data collection 
may 
rely 
on 
information 
collected 
and 
consolidated 
by 
the 
project coordination 
team 
as 
well 
as 
reliable 
secondary 
sources. 
Illustration 
2B-1 
provides 
an 
overview 
of 
how 
outcomes and 
objectives 
are 
believed 
to 
be 
linked 
(the 
use 
of 
strategy 
maps 
is 
inspired 
by 
Kaplan, 
2001). 
Each 
arrow 
represents 
an 
alleged 
causal 
relationship. 
For 
reasons 
of 
simplicity, 
the 
illustration 
predominantly depicts 
direct 
relationships and 
hence 
does 
not 
give 
full 
justice 
to  how 
outcomes  are 
interrelated 
and 
mutual 
supportive. 
Particularly, 
the 
impact 
of 
component 
3 
is 
expected 
to 
rest 
on 
the 
successful 
implementation 
of 
component 
1 
and 
2.

Annex 
2(C): 
Environmental Guidelines 
for Laboratory Research 
CHILE: 
Science 
for the Knowledge 
Economy 
This 
annex 
discusses 
the 
environmental 
aspects 
of 
the 
project 
with 
a view 
to 
ensuring 
adherence 
to 
sound 
environment guidelines 
for 
laboratory 
research. 
Under 
component 
2 
and 
3, 
the 
project 
would 
support 
a number 
of 
competitive 
funds 
for 
research. 
Research 
grants 
will 
be  awarded 
to 
Institutions 
of 
Excellence, 
Research 
Teams 
and  Cooperative 
Research 
Consortia. 
In 
addition, 
private 
sector 
R&D 
will 
be 
supported 
through 
the 
insertion 
of 
young 
researchers 
into 
industry. 
Part 
of 
the 
research 
could involve 
laboratory activities, 
in 
which 
potentially 
environmental 
hazardous 
materials 
are 
applied. 
For 
this 
reason 
the 
project 
may 
involve 
a 
marginal 
environmental 
risk. 
Against this 
background, the 
project 
team 
has 
requested 
environmental guidelines 
from CONICYT, 
the 
National 
Commission 
for 
Science 
and Technology. 
As 
part 
of 
project 
implementation, 
CONICYT 
will 
be 
responsible 
for 
ensuring compliance 
with 
environmental guidelines 
for 
laboratory 
research. 
In 
regard 
to 
support 
of 
R&D 
activities, 
CONICYT 
are 
constraining 
researchers 
and 
institutions 
to 
comply 
with 
national  and 
international 
regulations 
in 
every 
aspect. 
CONICYT 
and 
the 
funded 
research 
institutions 
operate 
under 
the 
law 
and guidelines 
issued 
from 
Comisidn 
Nacional 
del 
Medio 
Ambiente, 
CONAMA, 
which 
regulates, 
monitors 
and evaluates 
the 
disposal 
of 
material 
potentially 
damaging 
for 
the 
environment. 
In 
particular, 
CONICYT 
is 
adhering 
to 
Decreto 
351 
and 
Decreto 
609 
that  regulate 
neutralization 
and 
purification 
of 
chemical residuals. Support 
will 
only 
be 
provided 
to 
programs that 
are 
environmentally 
sound 
with 
respect 
to 
their 
societal 
purpose 
and 
operation. Consequently, the 
project 
will 
not 
support 
activities 
that 
could 
harm 
the environment. 
Research 
projects 
will 
be 
carried 
out  under  reinforcement 
of 
Chilean 
rules 
for 
bio-safety  and 
environmental 
protection 
acts. 
National 
guidelines 
for 
laboratory 
safety, 
disposal 
of 
reagents 
and other 
hazardous 
material, bio-ethics 
and 
bio-safety 
have 
been 
reviewed 
by 
the 
project 
team and 
have been 
found 
to 
be 
accordance 
with 
international 
best 
practice. 
The 
relevant 
Chilean regulation 
is 
available 
in 
the project 
file. 
CONICYT 
will 
continually monitor 
and, 
if 
necessary, 
enforce the compliance 
of 
environmental 
grantees. 
Moreover 
the 
project 
team 
will, 
as 
a natural 
part 
of 
supervision, 
follow-up 
as 
regards 
adherence 
to 
national 
environmental guidelines 
for 
laboratory 
research. 
Although, 
the 
financed 
research 
may 
involve 
the 
use 
of 
environmental 
hazardous 
material, 
the 
area 
of 
research 
includes 
improved 
environmental 
management. 
Experience 
from 
the 
Millennium 
Science 
Initiative 
shows 
that 
support 
of 
research  centers 
and 
science 
nuclei 
within 
bio-diversity 
and 
bio-technology 
improved 
the 
Chilean knowledge 
base 
and 
involved training 
of 
advanced 
students 
in 
areas 
of 
high 
relevance 
for 
better  understanding 
of 
the 
environment. 
This 
is 
economically 
and 
environmentally important 
knowledge 
for 
Chile 
given 
its 
reliance 
on 
natural 
resource-based 
production. 
The 
experience 
is 
expected 
to 
be 
scaled 
up 
with 
this 
follow-on 
program, 
which 
could 
improve knowledge 
formation 
within 
forestry, 
mining, 
marine-biology 
and 
bio-diversity. 
This 
would 
create 
knowledge 
and 
awareness 
regarding environmental 
management, 
and 
as 
such 
promote 
environmental 
sound 
production. 
The 
aspects 
are 
therefore 
expected 
to 
improve 
the 
basis 
for 
sustainable development 
in 
Chile.

Annex 
4 
Economic Analysis 
CHILE: 
Science 
for 
the 
Knowledge Economy 
This 
annex 
succinctly 
summarizes 
existing 
economic 
evidence 
of 
the importance 
of 
science, 
technology 
and 
innovation 
(S, 
T 
and 
I) 
for 
development 
of 
economies 
based 
on 
the 
exploitation 
of 
natural 
resources 
like 
that 
of 
Chile. 
Subsequently, 
it 
critically 
reviews 
the 
rational 
for 
public 
investment 
in 
S, 
T 
and 
I. 
This 
leads 
to 
considerations 
of 
the 
rate 
of 
return 
on 
investments 
in 
technological development 
including 
the 
retum 
on investments 
under 
this 
program. 
Further, 
the 
analysis provides 
the 
underlying 
economic 
reasoning 
for 
this 
program’s 
set 
of 
chosen 
investment instruments 
in 
order 
for 
the 
program’s 
limited 
resources 
to 
most 
appropriately stimulate output 
of 
the 
Chilean 
national innovation 
system 
and 
economic 
growth. 
This 
annex 
collects 
economic 
evidence 
from 
various 
sources. 
In 
particular, 
the 
analysis 
benefitted 
tremendously 
from 
economic 
research 
by 
Maloney 
and 
Lederman on 
the 
national innovation 
svstems 
in 
Latin 
America and 
the 
Caribbean. 
This 
work 
is 
forthcoming 
in 
a 
serie 
of 
publications: 
de 
Ferranti, 
Perry, 
Gill, 
Guasch 
and 
Schady: 
Closing 
the 
Gap 
in 
Education 
and 
Technology; 
Lederman 
and 
Maloney: 
Innovation 
in 
Mexico: 
NAFTA 
Is 
Not 
Enough. 
Further, 
the 
annex 
relies on 
research 
by 
Dahlman 
et 
al. 
(forthcoming), 
OECD 
and  the 
project 
team’s 
own 
investigation 
of 
the 
Chilean  innovation 
system: 
Agapitova 
and 
Holm-Nielsen 
(2002), 
Hansen; 
Agapitova, 
Vukmirovic, 
and 
Holm-Nielsen 
(2002), 
and 
Hansen 
and 
Holm-Nielsen 
(2002). 
1. 
The 
ability 
of 
a 
society 
to 
understand, produce, 
adapt, 
diffuse, 
and 
commercialize 
scientific 
and 
technological  knowledge 
is 
critical 
for 
sustained 
economic 
growth 
and 
improved  quality 
of 
life. 
Countries 
that adopted 
science 
and technology-led 
industrialization 
early-and 
where other 
conditions 
such 
as 
market 
incentives 
and 
strong 
institutions 
were  present-now 
have 
economies that 
are 
deeply 
permeated 
by 
science, 
technology, and innovation. 
These are 
also the countries that 
have 
grown 
steadily 
and 
become 
increasingly wealthy. 
This 
is 
the 
underlying 
reason 
for 
Chile 
to 
reinforce 
its 
science 
and 
technology 
sector. 
The 
increased exposure 
to 
fierce global 
competition, 
rapid 
technological 
progress and 
Chile’s continued 
heavy 
dependence 
on 
natural 
resources, 
amplify 
the 
gains 
of 
strengthening the 
Chilean 
national innovation 
system. 
Value 
of 
Science, 
Technology 
and 
Innovation 
for 
Economic 
Growth 
It 
is 
by 
now 
a well-established 
fact 
that a broad 
definition 
of 
technological 
progress 
accounts 
for 
a large 
part 
of 
economic 
growth. 
For 
example, 
Easterly 
and 
Levine 
(2000) 
using 
the 
growth 
regression 
methodology, 
estimate 
that 
up 
to 
80 
percent 
of 
cross-country 
variation 
in 
economic 
growth 
can be 
attributed to technological 
progress. 
The 
findings 
from 
such 
macro-growth 
accounting 
exercises  are 
corroborated 
by 
larger 
historical 
assessments 
based 
on diverse 
methods. 
For 
the 
Latin 
American 
and Caribbean 
region, 
the 
World 
Bank 
report 
“From 
Natural 
Resources 
to 
the Knowledge 
Economy” 
finds 
that 
knowledge 
and 
technology 
is 
a 
critical 
determinant 
for 
growth 
in 
the region, especially 
because 
of 
the countries’ 
dependence 
on 
natural 
resources. 
It 
stresses 
that 
productivity 
growth 
in 
agriculture 
has 
outpaced that 
of 
manufacturing 
in 
both 
developed 
and 
developing countries. 
Productivity 
growth 
in 
agriculture 
in 
Latin 
America 
has 
increased 
significantly 
after 
trade 
reform. 
In 
short, 
natural 
resource-based 
activities 
can 
lead 
to high productivity 
growth,  technical spillovers,  and 
forward 
and 
backward linkages, 
as 
much 
as 
modem manufacturing. 
Such 
activities 
can  become 
knowledge 
industries.  They 
are 
in 
no 
way 
incompatible 
with 
the  new 
knowledge 
economy. 
The 
key 
to 
success 
is 
to 
complement 
natural 
resource 
wealth 
with 
good institutions, 
human 
capital, 
and 
knowledge. 
Developing 
dynamic 
natural 
resource-based 
sectors 
is 
not incompatible, 
either, 
with building 
new comparative 
advantages 
in 
footloose and 
high-tech 
manufacturing. 
The 
report 
- 
48 
- 
Page  53
underscores 
that 
rich 
endowments 
of 
natural 
resources, 
combined 
with 
the  aggressive 
pursuit 
and 
adoption 
of 
new 
technologies, 
is 
a proven 
growth 
recipe. 
Further, 
the 
evidence 
strongly indicates that 
technological development 
does 
not 
preclude the development 
of 
manufacturing 
or 
other 
activities 
in 
the 
“knowledge” 
economy. 
In 
a nutshell, countries that 
have 
“played 
to 
their 
strengths” 
have done 
well. 
Equally, 
what 
is 
important 
is 
not 
so 
much 
what 
is 
produced, 
but 
how 
it 
is 
produced. 
Taking 
advantage 
of 
global 
technological 
progress 
is 
essential 
in 
every 
field, 
and 
it 
cannot be 
done 
cheaply. 
The 
recurrent 
lesson 
of 
the 
successful 
natural 
resource developers, and 
of 
contemporary 
theory, 
is 
the 
necessity 
of 
engendering 
a 
high 
level 
of 
human 
capital  and 
developing 
a  capacity 
for 
“national” 
learning 
and 
innovation, 
de 
Ferranti, 
Perry, 
Lederman 
and 
Maloney. 
(2002). 
2. 
Public 
investments 
in 
S, 
T 
and 
I 
is 
warranted 
on 
the 
basis 
of 
several 
major market failures 
in 
the 
market 
for 
knowledge: 
Rational 
for 
Public Investment 
in 
Science, 
Technology 
and 
Innovation 
Uncertainty 
of 
outcome. 
Research 
is 
by 
definition 
an 
investigation 
into 
the 
unknown,  and 
the 
outcome 
is 
highly 
uncertain, especially 
with 
a 
view 
to 
commercialization 
of 
research. 
For-profit 
agents 
are 
therefore 
likely 
to 
refrain 
from 
fundamental 
research, 
except 
in 
markets where 
knowledge 
intensity 
of 
products borders the knowledge 
frontier, 
which 
is 
only 
the 
case 
in 
segments 
of 
the 
pharmaceutical, 
telecommunication 
and 
computing 
industries. 
Information 
asymmetries. 
The very 
long 
run 
and 
risky 
nature 
of 
R&D 
further 
compounds standard 
problems 
of 
information 
asymmetries. 
A 
firm 
may 
have a hard 
time 
knowing 
if 
a 
researcher 
conducting 
basic 
research 
is 
incompetent, 
or 
whether 
the 
problem 
is 
simply 
extremely 
difficult. 
Appropriability problems 
impede 
innovation 
and 
diffusion. 
Given 
the 
difficulty 
of 
excluding 
others 
from 
ideas, 
innovators 
will 
not appropriate the 
full 
social benefits 
of 
their work. 
This 
has 
given 
rise 
to 
intellectual 
property 
right 
protections, 
such 
as 
patenting 
that 
creates 
a market 
for 
new 
ideas. 
However, 
the 
social 
value 
of 
the 
innovation 
is 
itself 
a 
function 
of 
the 
degree 
and 
speed 
of 
diffusion 
which, 
in 
some 
dimensions 
is 
impeded 
by 
the 
IPR 
system- 
the 
cost 
of 
using 
the 
innovation 
is 
higher 
than 
the 
marginal 
cost 
of 
its 
diffusion. 
Lumpiness 
and 
scale economies 
dictate specialization. 
R&D 
and 
innovation 
are 
characterized 
by 
economies 
of 
scale 
and lumpiness. 
To 
be 
effective, 
resources 
need 
to 
be 
concentrated 
in 
a 
manner 
beyond the capacity 
of 
the 
individual 
firm. 
This, 
combined 
with 
the 
fact that 
even 
patents 
are 
not 
effective 
at 
resolving 
the 
non-appropriability  problem 
in 
“pure 
science” 
provides a 
rationale 
for 
institutions 
dedicated 
to 
R&D 
and 
innovation 
efforts, 
such 
as 
research centers 
and universities. 
This 
also 
speaks 
for 
the 
need 
for 
a 
national 
innovation 
strategy 
grouping 
a (small) nation’s 
competences 
around core 
competitive 
areas 
in 
order 
to 
reach 
critical 
mass 
and 
competitiveness. 
Innovation 
and 
knowledge developed 
by 
those 
institutions tend 
to 
be nonexclusive 
and 
are made 
available 
to 
all 
and 
any 
interested parties, guided 
by 
an 
appropriate 
allocation 
of 
property 
rights. 
The 
issues 
there 
are 
then 
how 
those 
institutions 
are 
developed and 
how 
are 
they financed. 
Even 
more relevant, 
because 
the 
knowledge 
is 
produced 
by 
non-end-users 
and 
hence 
used 
by 
others, 
incentives 
for 
knowledge 
diffusion 
in 
funding 
become 
crucial 
for 
the 
system’s 
performance. 
Innovation, 
diffusion, 
and application 
require 
collaboration among 
many 
institutions and 
firms. 
Although innovation 
is 
sometimes the 
product 
of 
one 
firm 
alone, 
the 
more common pattern 
is 
one 
of 
joint 
efforts, among 
various 
firms, 
or 
among 
firms 
and R&D-related 
institutions. 
Further, 
progress 
does 
not proceed 
linearly 
from 
pure 
science 
to 
applied 
technologies, 
but 
moves 
in 
both 
directions 
(Nelson 
and Rosenberg 
1993); 
and 
feedback 
from 
frontline 
users 
of 
technology 
to 
researchers 
is 
essential 
for 
the 
refinement 
of 
products and 
production 
processes 
(Rosenberg 
2001). 
In 
many 
industrial 
countries, 
these 
issues 
have 
given rise 
to 
national  institutions 
devoted 
to 
fomenting  or 
-49- 
Page  54
Jones 
and 
Williams 
I 
1998 
I 
35 
Social 
returns 
Taking 
into 
account 
the 
impact 
of 
one 
firm’s 
R&D 
on the 
entire 
economy 
through 
knowledge spillovers 
improving 
other 
companies’ 
products 
and 
production 
techniques 
through 
copying, adapting 
and 
learning, 
the 
return 
to 
R&D 
rises 
several 
fold. 
Available 
estimates 
suggest 
a 
social 
return 
around 
70 
percent 
to 
100 
percent. 
These 
estimates 
point 
to 
a massive 
under-investment 
in 
R&D 
up 
till 
five 
to 
ten 
times 
the 
5.0 
- 
50 
- 
Page  55
existing 
level. 
These 
estimates 
should 
be taken 
with 
a grain 
of 
salt 
given 
the 
difficulties 
mapping and 
quantifying 
knowledge spillovers. 
Nevertheless, 
even 
an 
over-estimation 
in 
the magnitude 
of 
two 
implies 
that 
investments 
in 
science 
and 
technology carry returns substantially 
above 
the 
price 
of 
the 
resources 
provided 
through 
this 
loan. 
Further, 
the 
findings testify 
to 
the 
important 
impact 
of 
market 
failure 
on 
the 
market 
for 
knowledge, 
which 
if 
left 
without 
public 
regulation, 
intervention 
and 
financing 
would 
produce 
around 
one 
fourth to 
one 
fifth 
of 
the socially 
optimal 
knowledge 
for 
the 
economy.

level rates 
of 
returns

for 
a  panel

Three findings 
are 
key 
for 
this 
project: 
0 
Social 
Returns 
to 
R&D 
exceed 
by 
far 
the 
retum to 
investments 
in 
physical 
capital 
by 
a factor 
6 
to 
10 
depending 
upon 
the 
initial 
level 
of 
income per 
capita. 
Returns 
to 
RbD 
increase 
with 
the 
intensity 
of 
natural 
resources 
in 
the 
economy. 
Chile, 
and 
other 
countries 
heavily 
relying 
on 
its 
natural 
resources, 
enjoys 
higher 
returns  on 
R&D 
than  a  more 
manufacturing-based 
economies. 
Formation 
of 
advanced human 
capital 
as 
measured 
by 
tertiary 
education 
is 
a 
prime 
drive 
of 
technical 
progress, 
and 
appears 
to 
be more 
important 
than 
basic 
education. 
Nevertheless, 
it 
should 
be 
noted 
that 
quality 
basic 
education 
is 
a 
precondition 
for 
tertiary 
education and 
for 
equitable growth. 
Figure 
1 
plots 
the estimated 
returns 
to 
R&D, 
physical 
capital and 
the 
ratio 
of 
the 
two 
returns. 
Returns 
to 
R&D 
consistently 
exceeds 
gains 
from 
physical 
capital, 
at 
early 
stage 
of 
development 
by 
60 
percent 
point, 
declining 
to 
10 
percentage 
point 
for 
high-income countries. 
Noteworthy, 
the 
ratio 
of 
the 
two 
returns 
remain 
fairly 
constant 
around 
2.5. 
For 
Chile, 
the 
calculations 
suggest 
a social 
return 
to 
R&D 
above 
50 
percent.

The 
above 
returns 
on 
R&D 
in 
Chile 
of 
50 
percent 
is 
obviously 
suggestive 
and indicates 
an 
average 
return 
to 
the 
average 
investment 
in 
R&D 
in 
a country at 
Chile's 
level 
of 
development. 
The 
actual 
return 
to 
the 
resources 
invested 
by 
this 
project 
evidently 
and 
crucially 
depends 
on context specific 
circumstances 
such 
as 
institutional 
arrangements, 
incentive 
structure and choice 
of 
intervention. 
This 
section 
briefly 
presents 
empirical 
results 
pointing to 
the 
critical 
importance 
of 
(i) 
increasing 
Chile's 
science 
base 
and 
(ii) 
its 
linkages 
with 
the 
private 
sector 
in 
order 
for 
the 
country 
to 
accelerated knowledge 
production 
and reap the 
above discussed 
golden benefits 
associated 
with 
R&D 
expenditures. 
Economic 
Rational 
for 
Choice 
of 
Interventions: 
Performance 
of 
national innovation 
systems 
(NIS) 
is 
most frequently 
measured 
by 
number 
of 
patents 
filed 
internationally.  Although, 
this 
indicator 
is 
the 
best 
available, 
it 
remain imperfect, 
since 
it 
merely 
captures 
innovations 
in 
relation 
to 
the 
global 
stock 
of 
commercial 
knowledge  and  neither 
reflects 
adaptations 
and 
transfer 
of 
knowledge 
from 
the 
global 
pool 
to 
the 
country 
nor 
transfers among 
the 
system's  elements. 
Alternatively, 
experts 
measure 
internationally 
published 
scientific 
articles 
as 
an 
indicator 
of 
scientific 
production. 
Lederman 
and 
Maloney  (forthcoming) 
have  assembled 
and  analyzed  a 
world 
wide 
database 
of 
quantitative 
information 
regarding 
national 
innovation 
systems.

3 
synthesizes 
the 
main 
determinants 
of 
innovation:

The 
findings 
shed 
light 
on 
the importance 
of 
a 
solid 
science 
base 
with 
a 
critical 
mass 
of 
S&T 
personal 
and 
adequate 
funding 
for 
an 
effective 
national 
innovation 
system: 
Impact 
of 
the 
Science base: 
Amount 
of 
R&D 
expenditure 
matters 
greatly 
for 
commercial knowledge 
production. 
This 
is 
a rather 
evident 
result 
and 
corroborates 
findings 
by 
Stem, 
Porter 
and 
Furman 
(2000), 
Quality 
of 
basic 
research 
is 
essential 
for 
patenting. 
Without 
world-class 
research 
firms 
and 
entrepreneurial 
scientists are 
incapable 
of 
bringing 
new 
science 
based 
products 
to 
the 
global 
market 
place. 
Similarly,  empirical 
evidence-see 
figure 
2-suggests 
that 
quality 
of 
science 
equally 
is 
a 
precondition 
for 
interaction 
with 
the  private. 
Once 
again, 
it 
is 
believed 
that 
below 
world 
class 
scientific 
and 
technological  solutions 
are 
uninteresting 
for 
companies 
competing 
on 
the 
world 
market. 
Advanced 
Human 
Capital 
stimulates 
innovation. 
The 
regression 
clearly 
suggest 
that 
higher 
enrolment 
into 
tertiary 
education 
leads 
to 
larger 
output 
of 
the 
innovation 
system. 
Impact 
of 
Private-Public 
Collaboration: 
Collaboration 
between 
private 
and 
public 
sector 
seems 
to 
influence 
innovative productivity. 
Despite, 
the 
collaboration  variable 
turns 
statistically 
insignificant 
when 
equally 
controlling 
for 
quality 
of 
science 
and 
ICT-penetration, 
it 
is 
firmly 
believed 
that 
lack 
of 
collaboration 
plays a key 
role 
for 
Chile's 
failure 
to 
capitalize 
adequately 
from 
its 
S&T 
investments. 
Although, 
the 
lack 
of 
collaboration 
partially 
stems 
from low 
private 
demand 
due 
to 
inadequate 
quality 
of 
science 
- 
as 
discussed 
above 
- 
private 
sector 
representatives argue 
that 
lack 
of 
collaboration  equally 
derives 
from 
a 
culture 
of 
isolation 
within 
the 
scientific 
community, 
lack 
of 
incentives 
for 
outreach 
to 
the 
private 
sector and 
inadequate 
private 
sector 
R&D 
capacity.

Impact 
of 
context variables: 
e 
Educational  attainment,  other 
regional factors, 
and 
ICT-penetration  are 
not 
directly  linked 
to 
innovative 
activity. 
Whilst 
being 
a 
precondition 
to 
tertiary education, 
secondary 
education 
seems 
not 
to 
increase 
patenting.  The 
Latin 
American 
shortfall 
in 
innovation 
is 
explained 
for 
by 
the above 
three 
factors accounting 
for 
the 
quality 
of 
the 
science 
base. 
Similarly, 
ICT 
penetration 
seems 
unrelated 
to 
patenting 
activity. 
However, 
as 
cautioned 
above, 
the 
regressions 
only 
express one 
role 
of 
the 
national 
innovation 
system, 
the 
propensity 
to 
produce new 
world 
commercial innovation. 
It 
does 
not 
take 
into 
account 
the 
innovation 
system's 
role 
in 
augmenting 
existing 
process techniques 
and 
catching 
up, 
where 
ICT, 
collaboration, 
and general 
educational attainment 
are 
expected 
to 
play 
an 
economically 
significant role. 
Overall, 
these 
results 
point to 
the 
key 
importance 
of 
a 
sufficiently 
educated, 
well-connected and 
financed 
science 
base 
for 
innovation 
to 
take 
place. 
This 
link 
is 
equally 
confirmed 
when 
looking 
at 
the 
correlation 
between 
scientific 
output-measured 
by 
internationally published 
articles-and 
innovation, 
gauged 
by 
number 
of 
patents, 
Figure 
3. 
-

-

The 
graph 
in 
Figure 
3 
shows that  the 
relationship 
between 
these 
two 
proxies 
of 
innovation 
outcomes have 
a strongly non-linear 
relationship 
with 
the 
level 
of 
development. 
The 
number 
of 
scientific 
publications, 
which 
is 
best 
interpreted 
as 
a 
proxy 
of 
the 
output 
of 
basic research 
(i.e., 
not 
necessarily 
applied 
research) tends 
to 
decline 
initially with 
development, 
but 
rises 
quickly 
after 
a certain 
point. 
The 
close 
relationship 
suggests 
that 
scientific 
development 
goes 
hand 
in 
hand 
with 
development 
of 
the 
innovation 
system. 
Chile 
is 
situated 
right 
around 
the 
“take-off’ 
point 
where 
both 
curves rises steeply.

CONICYT

The

in

CONICYT

to 
the Bank. 
A 
capacity 
assessment 
of 
CONICYTs 
procurement 
unit 
was

based 
on 
the experience 
of 
the

This

with 
a  Bank  financed  project. 
The

of 
the  procurement 
organizational 
structure 
of 
CONICYT 
and 
an 
Action 
Plan 
agreed 
upon 
during 
Negotiations. 
The 
Action 
Plan includes 
the 
following 
aimed 
at 
reinforcing 
the 
implementing 
capacity 
of 
CONICYT 
(i) 
hiring 
a 
procurement consultant knowledgeable 
of 
Bank 
procurement 
policies 
and procedures; 
(ii) 
drafting 
of 
standard 
biddings 
documents 
for 
NCB 
procurement 
of 
goods 
and works; 
(iii)

and

of 
uniform 
annual  procurement  plans 
by 
beneficiary 
research 
institutions 
that

(iv)  developing 
a 
chapter

OM 
for 
procurement 
under 
the 
grants consistent 
with 
the 
procurement

(v)

of 
at 
least 
two 
weeks 
for

procurement staff, 
and 
(vi) 
developing 
and

a project

and

In

to familiarize

The

requirements and 
will

Notice

Negotiations. 
The

in

A. 
The 
following 
principles 
of 
procurement 
shall 
expressly govern 
all 
procurement 
of 
works 
and 
goods 
under 
National 
Competitive 
Bidding: 
1. 
All 
documents 
pertaining 
to 
a 
bid 
shall 
be 
submitted 
at 
the 
same 
time. 
Bidders shall 
not 
be 
required 
to 
submit 
bid 
securities 
or 
other documents 
in 
advance and separately 
from 
their 
bids. 
2. 
Contracts 
shall 
be 
awarded 
to 
the 
bidder  who 
meets 
the  appropriate 
standards 
of 
capability 
and 
resources and whose 
bid 
has 
been 
determined 
(i) 
to 
be 
substantially responsive 
to 
the 
bidding 
documents 
and 
(ii) 
to offer 
the 
lowest evaluated 
cost. 
3. 
Foreign contractors 
or 
suppliers shall 
not 
be 
required 
to 
have a representative 
in 
Chile 
as 
a 
condition 
for 
bidding. 
B. 
The 
following

of 
procurement 
shall 
expressly govern the selection 
of

services: 
1. 
Foreign  consultants

required 
to

a  representative 
in 
Chile 
as

condition 
of

Annex 
6(B): 
Financial Management and Disbursement 
Arrangements 
CHILE: Science for 
the 
Knowledge 
Economy 
Financial 
Management 
1. 
Summary 
of 
the 
Financial 
Management 
Assessment 
CONICYT’s 
long 
experience 
in 
implementing 
programs 
in 
the 
science and 
technology 
field, 
including 
development 
projects 
(IDB 
financing) 
together 
with 
a 
cadre 
of 
high 
level 
professional  staff  warrant 
satisfactory 
arrangements 
for 
the 
financial 
management 
concerns 
of 
this new project. 
CONICYT’s 
present 
organization structure 
responds 
to 
the 
multiple 
activities 
it 
carries out 
in 
the 
fields 
of 
science 
and 
technology. 
CONICYT 
has 
not 
had 
direct 
experience 
with 
Bank 
financed  projects. 
Nevertheless, 
during 
project preparation 
and 
at 
appraisal, 
staff 
assigned 
to 
the 
financial 
management 
area 
of 
the 
new 
project 
quickly 
grasped 
the 
essentials 
on the 
subject, 
thus 
anticipating 
an 
effective 
and 
efficient 
operation 
in 
this 
regard 
during 
project implementation. 
CONICYT 
needs 
to 
review 
its 
level 
of 
staffing 
to 
address 
the increased 
workload 
stemming 
from 
the 
new 
project  activities.  Discussion 
held 
with 
upper 
management 
indicate 
the 
entity 
will 
carry  out  a 
comprehensive 
review 
of 
their 
financial 
management 
functions 
and 
responsibilities 
to 
determine 
the 
real 
need 
of 
additional staff 
to 
handle 
the new 
operation’s 
administrative 
and 
financial 
activities. 
During 
the 
FM 
Assessment, 
agreement 
was 
reached 
on the 
format 
for 
the 
FMRs, 
which 
will 
be 
submitted 
to 
the 
Bank 
on 
a  quarterly 
basis. 
FMRs 
will 
be 
prepared 
for 
information 
purposes 
only, 
not  for 
disbursements, 
at 
least 
in 
the 
early 
stages 
of 
project 
implementation. 
Adequacy 
of 
the 
Financial 
Management and Disbursement Arrangements 
The 
Financial 
Management 
Department 
of 
CONICYT 
(DAF) 
will 
have 
a 
leading role 
in 
all 
fiduciary 
responsibilities 
pertaining 
to 
the 
Project. 
DAF 
will 
be 
the 
sole 
link 
between 
the 
Government 
and 
Beneficiaries 
in 
connection 
with 
fund 
transfers. 
DAF 
will 
account 
for 
both 
funds 
received 
from 
the 
Government and 
funds transferred 
to 
Beneficiaries. 
A 
Project Coordination 
Team, 
composed 
by 
a 
Coordinator 
General 
and three 
Deputy 
Coordinators 
(one 
for 
each 
component) 
will 
perform 
the 
day-to-day 
tasks 
of 
implementing 
the 
Project. Consideration 
is 
also 
been 
given 
to 
the 
inclusion 
of 
a 
fourth  Deputy 
Coordinator,  responsible 
for 
the 
overall 
financial 
management 
concerns 
of 
the 
immediate project. 
This 
Group 
will 
also 
maintain 
the detailed accounting 
records 
from 
which 
the 
FMRs 
will 
be prepared 
quarterly. Balances 
of 
records 
maintained 
by 
the 
Project 
Coordination Team 
will 
be 
reconciled 
with 
those 
maintained 
by 
DAF 
on a 
monthly 
basis. 
The 
positions 
for 
these 
three/four 
Deputy 
Coordinators have 
been 
identified 
but 
as 
of 
the 
date 
of 
drafting 
of 
this 
report 
they had 
not 
been 
engaged. 
CONICYT 
maintains appropriate accounting 
systems, 
prepares 
timely 
financial 
statements 
and 
provides 
relevant 
financial  information 
to 
its 
higher 
management. 
A 
core 
of 
well 
trained  professionals 
in 
the 
administrative 
and 
financial 
area 
warrant satisfactory 
arrangements 
for 
the 
forthcoming Bank 
financed 
project. 
A 
chart 
of 
accounts 
discriminating 
expenditures 
by 
Component 
and 
major 
activities 
was 
prepared 
to 
record 
and 
serve 
as 
basis 
for 
the 
preparation 
of 
project financial 
statements. 
Disbursements 
will 
be 
prepared 
by 
the Finance and 
Administration 
Department, 
following 
Bank policies 
and 
procedures. 
An 
experienced 
officer 
working 
at 
another 
Bank 
finance 
project 
at 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Education 
will 
provide 
support and guidance 
on 
disbursement 
and 
procurement 
matters 
at 
the 
initial 
period 
of 
project 
implementation. 
Project financial 
management 
and disbursement 
and procurement 
arrangements 
will 
be 
included 
in 
the 
Operational Manual.

details on

please 
refer 
to

Results 
of 
the 
assessments 
of 
inherent 
risks 
and 
control 
risks 
suggest 
a comfortable 
level 
at 
CONICYT, 
primarily 
due 
to 
its 
long 
standing operational record 
and 
wide recognition 
as 
a 
competent, 
efficient 
and 
effective entity, 
leader 
in 
the 
promotion 
and 
dissemination 
of 
best 
practices 
in 
the context 
of 
Science 
and 
Technology. The 
financial 
management 
action 
plan 
presents 
the 
areas 
relating 
to 
the 
project financial 
management 
arrangements 
requiring 
further improvements 
to 
be 
in 
compliance 
with 
Bank 
standards. 
Significant 
risks 
The 
additional workload 
stemming 
from 
the new operation 
may 
cause 
delays 
in 
the 
administrative 
and 
financial 
processes. 
As 
a  part 
of 
CONICYT’s 
continuous 
efforts 
to 
rationalize 
the 
organizational 
structure 
and 
in 
agreement 
reached 
with 
the 
Bank 
during 
negotiations, the 
management 
of 
CONICYT 
is 
reviewing 
the 
function 
of 
the  Finance  and 
Administration 
Department 
with 
a 
view  to 
increasing 
efficiency, 
assuring 
adequate 
staffing 
and 
improving 
the 
integration 
of 
the  Department 
into 
the 
organization. 
These 
efforts 
will 
be 
closely 
monitored 
by 
the 
Bank 
team. 
Control 
environment 
and 
procedures 
The 
control 
environment: 
CONICYT 
is 
an 
Agency 
that 
operates 
under the 
auspices 
of 
the 
Ministry 
of 
Education. 
As 
such, 
it 
is 
required 
to 
observe 
the 
Chilean 
budget 
law 
and  related 
procedures and 
regulations. 
There 
is 
little 
if 
any 
room  for 
deviation 
from 
these 
standards. 
Central 
Government 
surveillance 
in 
this 
regard 
is 
efficient 
and 
effective.  The 
philosophy 
and operating  style 
of 
project 
management 
is 
appropriate 
for 
the 
type 
of 
activities carried 
out 
by 
this 
entity. 
A 
group 
of 
prominent 
personalities 
in 
the 
areas 
of 
science, 
academia and 
public 
service 
will 
constitute a Supervising Board, 
providing 
guidance 
on operational 
policies 
and 
major project  activities.  Project entity’s 
methods 
of 
assigning 
authority 
and 
responsibility 
have been 
proven 
during 
the 
long 
life 
of 
this 
organization (over 
30 
years). 
The 
control 
procedures: 
Management 
has 
established 
appropriate 
reporting 
standards 
although 
not 
uniformly 
applied 
in 
all 
of 
the 
entities activities. 
For 
the 
Bank 
financed 
project 
FMRs 
will 
be 
prepared 
quarterly. 
In 
addition, 
the 
Finance 
and 
Administration 
Department 
will 
prepare 
monthly 
financial 
reports 
to 
review 
project financial 
activity 
in 
preparation 
for 
the 
analytical 
work 
required 
in 
the 
preparation 
of 
FMRs. There 
is 
appropriate 
segregation 
of 
duties 
among 
financial 
staff 
and 
access 
is 
limited 
to 
records 
and 
selected 
financial 
reports. 
Management 
is 
regularly 
provided 
with 
statements 
on 
the basis 
of 
which 
comparison 
and analysis 
of 
financial 
progress 
with 
budgeted 
amounts 
is 
carried 
out. 
Risk 
assessment 
Inherent 
risks 
at 
this 
new operation 
are 
minimal 
if 
not 
nil. 
Good 
Govemance 
prevails 
throughout 
the 
public 
sector 
in 
Chile. 
Although 
lately 
there  have 
been 
indications 
of 
irregular 
practices 
in 
certain 
agencies, 
the 
legal 
system 
has 
reacted 
energetically 
and 
appropriate actions 
are 
being 
taken. 
Funds 
flow 
procedures 
in 
the 
government 
are 
adequately 
established  and  appropriate  norms 
safeguard 
these 
operations. 
The 
salary 
structure 
of 
public 
servants, 
although 
significantly below 
those 
of 
private 
sector, 
still 
retains workers 
for 
long 
tenure 
in 
their 
positions. 
Technical 
management 
is 
normally 
independent 
from 
politics. 
The 
accounting profession 
observes 
international 
standards. 
Control 
risks: 
CONICYT 
is 
an 
organization 
with 
a 
proven 
efficient 
and 
effective 
operational 
record. 
During 
the 
over 
thirty 
years 
since 
initiating 
operations 
in 
the 
area 
of 
science 
and  technology, 
management 
has 
maintained 
high 
standards 
of 
transparence 
and execution 
of 
programs 
to 
convey 
the 
image 
necessary 
to 
function 
in 
this 
environment. 
The 
entity 
also 
operates 
programs financed 
by 
the 
Inter 
American Development 
Bank 
and other 
Donors. 
Hence, 
the requirements 
of 
international 
organizations 
are 
known 
to 
management 
through 
direct 
experience.

As

of 
financing. 
Requests

will

to

Bank 
of 
the 
basis

specifically

of 
Expenditure 
(SOE)/modified 
FMR 
for 
the 
Grants component,

and attached 
to

and

(a)
