Additional 
Annex 
11 
: 
Science, Technology and Innovation in Chile 
CHILE: 
Science 
for 
the Knowledge Economy 
(This 
summary 
is 
based 
on 
Agapitova, 
Natalia 
& 
Lauritz 
Holm-Nielsen, 
2002. 
Science, Technology 
and 
Innovation 
in 
Chile. 
Washington, 
DC: 
World 
Bank) 
The 
most recent 
volume 
of 
the 
Latin 
American 
Competitiveness 
Report 
makes 
the 
following 
observation: 
“The main 
competitive 
weakness 
of 
the 
Chilean 
economy 
is 
the 
country’s lack 
of 
ability 
to 
innovate 
and 
adopt 
technological 
change” 
(Vial 
& 
Cornelius, 
2002: 
176). 
At 
the 
same 
time, 
value-added 
in 
manufacturing 
is 
almost 
eight 
times 
lower 
in 
Chile 
than 
in 
most 
developed countries 
and 
export 
of 
high-technology products 
represents 
only 
5% 
of 
total 
exports; 
a 
share 
which 
is 
significantly below 
the 
levels 
of 
other 
Latin 
American 
economies 
and 
most 
OECD 
countries. 
In 
consequence, 
the development 
path 
followed 
in 
Chile 
has 
diverged substantially 
from 
that 
of 
more 
developed 
countries: 
The 
share 
of 
agricultural production 
has 
increased 
steadily; 
whereas 
manufacturing 
and services 
have 
been 
in 
decline. 
This 
paper 
takes 
the 
position 
that  a 
weak 
capacity 
for 
science, 
technology 
and 
innovation, 
is 
one 
of 
the 
key 
reasons 
why 
Chile 
lags 
the 
more developed countries 
in 
terms 
of 
productivity 
and 
technological 
development. 
Hence, 
the 
purpose 
of 
the 
current 
paper 
is 
to 
benchmarks 
Chile’s  capacity 
for 
science, 
technology 
and 
innovation 
and, 
subsequently, 
offer 
a 
number 
of 
explanations why 
Chile 
has 
been 
relatively 
unsuccessful 
in 
developing 
its 
capacity 
for 
science, 
technology 
and 
innovation. 
Seven 
areas 
are 
examined: 
(i) 
Political 
organization; 
(ii) 
expenditure 
level 
and 
composition; 
(iii) 
public 
policies; 
(v) 
intellectual 
property protection; 
(vi) 
foreign 
investments; 
and, 
finally 
(vii) 
access 
to 
venture capital. 
Benchmarking 
Chile: 
Science, 
Technology 
and 
Innovation 
Scientific 
Publications 
and 
Research 
Personnel 
The 
number 
of 
scientific  publications 
increased 
steadily 
in 
1990-1998, 
particularly 
in 
specialized 
scientific 
databases 
like 
biological 
sciences, 
which 
in 
tum 
reflects 
the 
existence 
of 
knowledge intensive 
fruit 
and 
wine  industries 
in 
Chile.  However,  Chile’s 
scientific 
contribution 
is 
still 
limited: 
In 
1998 
Chilean 
researchers 
were 
mentioned 
only 
in 
0.19% 
of 
SCI-Search 
citations 
(Science 
Citation Index 
Database) and 
0.17% 
of 
citations 
in 
the French 
scientific 
database 
PASCAL. 
In 
the 
same 
year, 
Brazil’s 
contribution 
to 
the 
same 
databases 
amounted 
to 
1.06% 
and 
1.04% 
respectively, 
while 
Mexico 
and 
Argentina 
were 
cited 
in 
around 
0.42% 
of 
publications 
in 
both 
databases. 
Still, 
Chile’s 
performance 
looks 
better 
in 
relative 
terms 
(publications 
for 
every 
100,000 
people), 
where 
the 
country 
is 
at 
the 
top 
of 
Latin 
America 
with 
12.4 
publications 
in 
SCI-Search, 
and 
the 
third 
best 
performer 
in 
Latin 
America 
for 
PASCAL 
with 
3.8 
citations 
in 
1998. 
Technology: 
Patents 
and 
Technological 
Transfer 
Patent-based 
statistics 
are 
the 
most 
widely 
used 
indicator 
for 
the 
output 
of 
innovation activity 
because 
of 
their 
importance 
for 
the 
commercialization 
of 
new products. Based 
on 
an 
examination 
of 
trends 
in 
solicited 
patents 
in 
Chile 
in 
1974-2000, 
the 
most 
obvious 
conclusion 
is 
that the 
innovative 
capacity 
of 
Chile 
seems 
to 
be 
less 
developed, 
and, 
especially, 
less 
dynamic than 
the 
scientific 
one. 
First 
of 
all, 
no 
progress 
has 
been made 
until 
the recent 
past 
when 
Chile 
opened 
its 
market 
to 
FDI: 
The 
number 
of 
solicited 
patents 
stagnated at 
around 250 per 
year 
for 
residents 
and 
500 
for 
non-residents. Since 
1991, 
the 
number 
of 
solicited 
patents 
has 
been 
rapidly 
growing, 
but 
only 
as 
a result 
of 
applications registered 
by 
non-residents. 
Therefore, 
the 
auto 
sufficiency 
rate 
of 
Chile 
is 
decreasing, 
emphasizing 
its 
growing 
dependence 
on 
foreign 
technology transfers. 
Another 
key 
characteristic 
is 
the 
ratio 
of 
granted 
to 
solicited 
patents, 
which 
is 
very 
low 
in 
Chile: 
Since 
1991, 
17% 
of 
non-resident 
solicited 
patents were 
granted 
and 
only 
9% 
for 
residents. 
As 
a 
result, 
Chile 
is 
(iv) 
cooperation 
and 
knowledge 
diffusion; 
-78- 
Page  83
a 
regional 
outsider 
in 
terms 
of 
absolute number 
of 
patents: 
Less than 
3,600 
patents were 
granted 
in 
Chile 
in 
1992-2000, compared 
to, 
approximately, 
15,000 
in 
Argentina, 
20,000 
in 
Brazil, 
and 
32,000 
in 
Mexico 
(RICYT, 
2000). 
Determinants 
for 
Chile’s 
science, 
technology 
and 
innovation 
capacity 
Organization 
The organization 
of 
science, 
technology 
and 
innovation policies 
in 
Chile 
seems 
to offer 
part 
of 
the 
explanation why 
Chile 
has 
not 
yet 
developed 
its 
capacity 
further: 
No 
single 
high-level 
policy 
body 
exists 
to 
examine 
and 
formulate 
science, 
technology 
and 
innovation policies 
in 
Chile. 
Instead, 
a 
patchwork 
of 
policies 
and 
institutions 
deal 
with 
science, 
technology  and innovation,  notably 
CONICYT 
and 
the 
Presidential 
Advisory 
Commission 
on 
Scientific 
Matters. 
CONICYT’s 
(Comisidn 
Nacional 
de 
Investigacidn 
CientljCica 
y 
Tecnoldgica, 
Chilean 
National Council 
for 
Science 
and 
Technology)  main 
role 
is 
to 
advise 
on the 
planning 
of 
scientific 
and 
technological 
development. 
Unfortunately, 
the 
council 
is 
not 
structurally 
organized to 
provide 
advice 
seeing 
that most 
of 
its 
resources go 
into 
the 
day-to 
day 
administration 
of 
financial 
instruments. The Presidential 
Advisory 
Commission on 
Scientific Matters 
also 
has 
advisory responsibilities, 
with 
some 
limited 
functions 
in 
the 
science 
field 
(excluding 
engineering 
and 
technology). 
It 
has 
few 
resources 
for 
a 
secretariat 
function, 
which 
in 
turn 
limits 
its 
capacity to commission 
policy 
studies 
to 
underpin 
its 
discussions. 
Expenditure 
for 
science, 
technology 
and 
innovation 
R&D 
expenditures 
have 
increased 
steadily 
in 
Chile 
throughout the last 
30 
years 
to 
a 
level 
of 
US$ 
450 
millions 
in 
2001, 
which 
in 
turn 
is 
40 
times more 
than 
in 
1975. 
On 
average, 
Chilean 
R&D 
expenditures 
have increased 
by 
17% 
each 
year 
in 
1975-2000. 
Likewise, 
Chilean expenditure per resident grew 
by 
approximately 
15% 
per 
year 
in 
1975-2000, 
which 
has 
implied 
an 
increase 
in 
expenditure 
per 
capita 
from 
US$ 
1.12 
in 
1975 
to 
US$29 
in 
2000. 
Notwithstanding 
the 
surge 
in 
R&D 
spending, 
Chile 
is 
still 
far 
from 
the 
level 
of 
benchmarking 
economies. 
Whereas 
Chile 
spent 
0.55% 
of 
its 
annual 
GDP 
in 
the 
period 
1985-2000, 
USA 
spent 
2.74%. 
The 
public 
sector 
accounts 
for 
the 
bulk 
of 
public 
expenditure. 
In 
1990-1999 
the 
Chilean 
government 
invested 
US$ 
2,265 
millions 
on 
R&D, 
which 
amounts 
to 
two 
thirds 
of 
total 
R&D 
expenditures 
in 
the 
period. Another US$ 
274 
millions 
were 
allocated 
to 
R&D 
purposes 
in 
higher 
education, 
which 
in 
turn 
brings 
total 
public 
expenditure 
to 
almost 
75% 
of 
national 
R&D 
spending 
(RICYT, 
2000). 
By 
contrast, 
private 
sector 
investments 
in 
R&D 
have been 
negligible, especially 
until 
1990. 
After 
1990, 
on 
the 
other 
hand, 
policy 
stability 
and 
improvements 
to 
the 
property 
rights 
regime 
have 
stimulated 
private 
R&D 
expenditure, 
which 
grew 
by 
four 
times 
over the 
1990s. 
Nevertheless, 
it 
still 
represents 
little 
more 
than 
one-fifth 
of 
Chilean 
R&D 
investments, 
which 
is 
lower 
than 
most 
other 
Latin 
American 
countries  and 
significantly  behind 
the 
developed 
OECD 
countries. 
In 
USA, 
for 
example,  business 
expenditures 
now 
represent 
68.5% 
of 
national 
R&D 
spending 
(RICYT, 
2001). 
The Chilean 
public 
authorities 
have become 
aware 
of 
the importance 
of 
private 
investment 
in 
R&D 
activities, 
and 
several 
funds 
such 
as 
FONDEF 
and 
FONTEC, 
now 
require 
a 
financial 
contribution 
from 
the 
private 
sector 
as 
a 
primary 
condition 
for 
project 
approval. Moreover, 
evidence 
indicates that 
some 
elements 
of 
the 
private 
sector 
have 
realized  that 
research 
has 
a 
major 
potential 
for 
the 
long-term 
sustainability 
of 
their 
business. 
In 
addition 
to 
domestic 
sources, 
foreign 
investors 
contribute 
a 
significant 
amount 
(US$ 
238 
millions 
in 
1990-1999) 
to 
national 
R&D 
spending. 
This 
is 
the 
highest 
number 
in 
Latin 
America 
and 
is 
largely 
due 
to 
the 
favorable investment environment 
created 
by 
the 
Chilean 
government. 
A 
breakdown 
of 
public 
R&D 
investment 
by 
type 
of 
research 
shows 
that 
basic research 
is 
clearly 
the 
government’s 
priority, receiving 
56.5% 
of 
total 
allocations 
in 
the 
period 
1979-2000. 
Allocations 
to 
the 
- 
79 
- 
Page  84
applied 
science 
have also increased 
in 
importance 
and 
rose 
from 
24.6% 
to 
31.6% 
in 
2000 (1992 
US$). 
Experimental 
development, 
which 
contributes 
to 
commercialization 
of 
scientific 
discoveries, received 
by 
contrast 
only 
13.1% 
of 
investments 
on 
average 
in 
the 
period 
1979-2000. 
This 
trend constitutes a 
major 
obstacle 
to 
the 
development 
of 
Chile's  innovative 
capacities 
and 
deviates 
clearly 
from 
the 
world's 
leading 
economies: 
While 
Chile 
and 
USA 
allocate 
similar 
shares 
of 
expenditure 
to 
applied 
research 
(30.5% 
in 
Chile 
vs. 
22.7% 
in 
USA 
on 
average 
in 
1990-1999), 
USA 
clearly  privileges 
experimental 
development (60.9% 
vs. 
13.4% 
in 
Chile 
on 
average 
in 
1990-1999) 
at 
the 
expense 
of 
basic 
research 
(16.4% 
in 
USA 
vs. 
56.4% 
in 
Chile 
in 
the 
same 
period). 
Policies 
Chilean 
policies 
have 
typically 
neither protected 
nor privileged 
any 
specific 
sector. 
As 
a result, 
the 
Chilean 
government 
has 
primarily 
focused on crosscutting 
science 
policies 
and 
less 
so 
on technology 
policies, 
leaving 
the 
need 
for 
innovation 
unattended. 
More 
recently, 
the 
Chilean 
government 
has 
become 
more 
aware 
of 
the 
importance 
of 
technological 
progress 
for 
the 
future 
development 
of 
the 
country 
and 
its 
competitiveness 
in 
the 
world 
market. 
This 
has 
resulted 
in 
a variety 
of 
programs, such 
as 
the Technology 
Innovation 
Program, 
the Production 
Promotion 
Program 
(SERCOTEC), 
and, 
finally, 
the 
Millennium 
Science 
Initiative 
Project. 
The 
Millennium 
Science 
Initiative 
Project 
(MSI) 
was 
launched 
in 
1999 
by 
the 
Chilean 
Ministry 
of 
Planning 
and 
the 
World 
Bank 
to 
revitalize 
the Chilean 
S&T 
system 
by 
supporting 
training 
of 
advanced 
human 
capital 
by 
world-class 
scientists 
engaged 
in 
cutting 
edge 
research. 
Based 
on 
the 
2002 
completion 
report, 
the 
project 
has 
succeeded 
in 
diverting 
the Government's attention 
to 
S&T 
issues; 
improving 
the 
productivity 
of 
the 
country's 
best 
researchers; 
and, 
finally, 
increasing 
the 
opportunities 
for 
advanced 
training 
and 
scientific 
collaboration. 
Whereas 
the 
Government 
operates 
a  number 
of 
policy 
programs 
in 
direct 
support 
of 
S&T, 
little 
experience exists 
with 
respect 
to 
indirect 
incentives. 
Still, 
a 
vast 
reservoir 
of 
international 
experience 
suggests 
that 
such 
policies 
have 
a major 
potential 
for 
stimulating 
S&T 
investments 
in 
the 
private 
sector. 
In 
consequence, 
the 
Government 
may 
want 
to 
experiment along 
these 
lines. 
A 
first 
step 
might 
be 
to 
refine 
the 
scheme 
for 
rewarding 
donations 
to 
universities 
by 
providing 
some 
form 
of 
tax 
benefit 
for 
donations 
aimed 
at 
supporting 
R&D. 
Currently, 
the 
only 
government 
incentive 
for 
funding 
research, 
apart 
from 
partnership 
in 
various 
funds, 
is 
the 
50 
percent 
tax 
deduction 
permitted 
for 
donations 
to 
universities. The 
response 
to 
this 
somewhat 
unfocused 
measure 
has 
been 
strong, 
with 
donations 
rising 
by 
a factor 
of 
20 
since 
the 
beginning 
of 
the 
1990s 
to 
a current 
figure 
of 
around 
US$ 
30 
million. 
Cooperation 
and 
knowledge 
diffusion 
It 
has 
become 
widely 
recognized 
in 
recent 
years 
that 
innovation 
processes 
are 
characterized 
by 
a 
considerable 
degree 
of 
interaction 
between 
different 
actors 
involved 
in 
scientific, 
technological 
and 
productive 
activities, 
i.e. 
networking. 
Science related 
policies 
developed 
by 
the 
Chilean 
government 
have 
strengthened the 
country's capacity 
for 
research 
and 
instilled 
a 
culture 
of 
open 
dissemination 
of 
research 
through  publications.  However, 
academic 
interests and  a  narrow 
disciplinary 
approach 
to 
research 
continue 
to 
separate 
discovery 
from 
exploitation 
and 
utilization 
(Mullin 
and 
al., 
2000). 
Moreover, 
coordination 
between 
different 
research 
performers 
such 
as 
the 
technological 
institutes and universities 
is 
insufficient. 
In 
other 
Latin 
American 
countries, 
by 
contrast, serious attempts are 
now 
being 
made 
to 
coordinate  technology-development 
activities 
within 
broadly  defined  "production 
chains" 
(cadenas 
productivas). 
The 
Acuerdos 
Sectoriales 
de 
Competitividad 
in 
Colombia, and 
the 
Agendas 
Industriales, 
Sociales 
y 
Regionales 
of 
CONICYT 
in 
Venezuela 
are 
two 
examples. 
In 
Chile,  cooperation between 
the 
private 
sector 
and 
research 
organizations 
is 
also 
limited. 
Some 
universities 
have 
set 
up 
offices 
to 
assist researchers 
in 
their 
dealings 
with 
clients. 
As 
a result, 
31.8 
% 
of 
firms 
acknowledged 
having 
benefited 
from 
innovations 
from 
universities 
and 
16.2 
% 
from 
public 
research 
institutes. 
Some 
25 
% 
of 
firms 
actually 
signed 
contracts 
with 
universities, and 
14 
% 
signed 
them 
- 
80 
- 
Page  85
with 
public 
research 
institutes. Furthermore, 
60 
% 
of 
firms 
reported 
having 
carried out 
some 
type 
of 
joint 
innovation 
with 
client 
firms. 
However, 
it 
is 
important 
to 
point 
out that external interactions 
were 
not 
assigned 
a 
crucial 
role by 
the 
firms 
themselves. 
Most 
firms 
reported 
that the 
principal 
source 
of 
new 
ideas 
was 
their 
own 
personnel (Melo, 
2001). 
One 
of 
the 
key 
obstacles 
to 
public-private 
cooperation 
seems 
to 
be 
the 
inward 
orientation 
of 
university 
researchers. 
Although 
there 
are 
some 
links 
between 
business 
and 
universities, 
particularly 
at 
the 
human-resources 
level 
(there 
is 
a 
tradition 
of 
part-time professorships 
being 
held by 
businessmen), 
the 
university 
mentality 
is 
generally 
not 
geared 
to 
solving 
problems 
within 
a 
business 
timescale. 
Lack 
of 
knowledge-diffusion 
mechanisms 
is 
another 
reason 
why 
Chile 
has 
been 
unsuccessful 
in 
developing a strong network. Even though 
the government 
has 
emphasized the strategic 
importance 
of 
knowledge, 
little 
has 
been 
done 
to 
diffuse 
it. 
IPR-protection 
Technology 
development 
in 
the 
industrial 
sector 
requires a 
patent 
rights 
system 
developed to 
motivate 
investment 
and 
provide 
access 
to 
the 
vast 
reservoir 
of 
knowledge 
globally 
available. 
Chile 
currently 
features 
a 
full 
legal framework 
with 
legal 
bodies 
to 
protect 
R&D 
investments 
(Lorenzini, 
1997). 
The regime 
has 
been amended 
several 
times 
in 
recent 
years. 
First, 
a new 
industrial 
property law, 
enacted 
in 
1991, 
has 
fixed 
a 
basic and 
common proceeding 
for 
the granting 
of 
patents, 
trademarks, 
utility 
models 
and 
industrial 
designs 
in 
order 
to 
streamline bureaucratic barriers. 
The 
same 
law 
created 
a special 
Court 
of 
Appeal 
for 
industrial 
property 
matters, 
which 
reviews,  revokes 
or  confirms 
any 
decision 
by 
the 
administrative authority. 
The 
next 
step 
forward 
was 
the 
copyright 
amendment 
law 
of 
1992, 
which 
inter 
alia 
provides 
for 
the 
establishment 
of 
a new 
private 
management system 
for collecting 
copyright 
fees 
from 
authors. 
Finally, 
in 
1999 
the 
GoC introduced 
a new 
IPR 
legislation draft 
with 
the 
intention 
of 
bringing 
Chile's 
intellectual 
property regime 
into 
agreement 
with 
the 
World 
Trade Organization 
(WTO) 
Agreement 
on 
Trade-Related Aspects 
of 
Intellectual 
Property Rights 
(TRIPS). 
Nevertheless, 
this 
legislation 
has 
still 
not 
been 
enacted and 
is 
reportedly 
not 
TFWS-consistent, 
even 
in 
its 
draft 
form 
(Inter-Agency Committee on 
Intellectual 
Property 
Rights, 
2001). 
Among 
other 
issues, 
the 
draft 
law 
does 
not 
provide 
adequate 
protection 
for 
confidential 
test  data. 
Inadequate 
enforcement 
against 
piracy 
and 
counterfeiting 
also 
remain 
serious 
problems, 
as 
does 
the 
large 
backlog 
of 
pending 
patent applications. Furthermore, 
Chilean 
Courts 
of 
Appeal 
have been 
supportive 
in 
cases 
where 
the trademark 
has 
been 
"stockpiled" 
but 
gone 
unused, 
but 
less 
so 
in 
cases 
where 
investments 
were 
made 
in 
use 
of 
the  trademark. 
In 
either 
case, 
proceedings 
can 
be 
lengthy (2-4 
years) 
and 
expensive ($5.000-$10.000) 
with 
a 
low 
success 
rate 
(Chile 
Country Commercial 
Guide, 
2002). 
In 
consequence, 
the 
current 
IPR 
systems 
needs 
to 
be 
further 
revised 
in 
order 
to 
stimulate investments 
in 
innovation 
and 
technology development. 
Foreign 
Direct 
Investment 
Foreign direct 
investment 
is 
one 
of 
the 
chief 
ways 
in 
which 
to 
participate 
in 
the knowledge 
revolution 
and 
facilitate 
transfer 
of 
technologies 
from 
the 
most 
developed 
economies. 
Chile, 
unlike 
most 
other 
Latin 
American 
countries, 
has 
a 
transparent 
and 
stable 
legal 
and economic environment 
in 
place 
to 
attract 
foreign 
investment. 
While 
many 
other  nations 
have 
placed 
significant 
restrictions  on 
foreign direct 
investment 
(FDI), 
Chile 
realized that 
providing 
foreign 
firms 
with 
fair, 
non-discriminatory 
rules 
was 
the 
best 
method 
to 
encourage 
investment. 
In 
fact, 
Chile 
was 
the 
first 
Latin 
American 
country 
to 
receive 
an 
investment-grade 
credit 
rating 
from 
international 
rating 
agencies, 
including 
Standard 
& 
Poor's 
and 
Duffand Phelps. 
More 
importantly, 
it 
has 
been able 
to 
maintain 
this 
rating 
throughout 
the 
recent 
financial 
crisis affecting 
all 
emerging 
economies, 
and 
currently holds 
the 
lowest 
risk 
rating 
in 
Latin 
America. 
-81 
- 
Page  86
From 
1990 
to 
1992, 
annual 
foreign 
investments 
averaged 
US$ 
1.7 
billion, 
but 
from 
1993 
flows 
increased 
dramatically, reaching a record 
US$ 
10.3 
billion 
in 
1999 
(which 
represented 
an 
increase 
of 
45% over 
1998). 
In 
2000, 
foreign 
investment registered a drop, 
but 
soon 
recovered 
and reached 
US$ 
8.1 
billion 
in 
2001. 
Between 
1985 
and  2001,  investments 
in 
natural 
resource 
industries 
(Mining, 
Forestry,  Fishing, 
Aquaculture 
and 
Agriculture) 
represented 
34.6% 
of 
total 
materialized 
FDI, 
while 
service 
industries 
accounted 
for 
an 
additional 
22.9%, 
followed by 
the 
Electricity, 
Gas 
and 
Water industries 
(18.0%) 
and 
Manufacturing  (1 
3.4%) 
(Foreign Investment Committee). 
Thus, 
technology transfer 
via 
FDI 
is 
very 
limited, 
seeing 
that 
it 
is 
mainly 
oriented towards 
natural 
resources 
sectors, 
which 
are 
not 
very 
dynamic 
on the technology 
front 
and 
generate 
fewer spillovers than high-technology industries. 
Venture 
capital 
Despite a favorable 
legal 
framework 
and 
relatively 
healthy 
public 
finances, 
Chile 
has 
been 
unable 
to 
attract significant 
amounts 
of 
venture 
capital. Venture 
capital 
refers 
to 
equity 
investment 
in 
new 
firms. 
It 
is 
a 
major 
source 
of 
funding for 
new 
technology-based 
firms 
in 
countries 
like 
the 
United 
States 
and 
it 
is 
pivotal 
in 
promoting  radical 
innovations  often 
carried 
out 
by 
new 
firms. 
In 
consequence, 
the 
development 
of 
a mature 
venture 
capital market 
is 
one 
of 
the 
key 
challenges 
for 
Chile. 
Venture 
capital 
means 
risk, 
which 
doesn't 
go 
well 
with 
the 
Chilean 
business 
culture 
according 
to 
various 
accounts. 
According 
to 
Kathleen Barclay, 
the 
Senior 
Country 
Officer for 
Chase 
Manhattan 
Bank 
and 
vice 
president 
of 
AMCHAM. 
Chilean 
entrepreneurs 
usually 
start 
by 
using capital 
from 
friends 
and 
family 
and, 
once 
they 
are 
big 
enough 
and 
operate 
at 
relatively 
low 
risk, 
they 
switch 
to 
private equity 
financing, 
without 
passing through 
the 
intermediate 
financing 
steps, 
namely 
"seed 
capital",  "angel 
investors", 
Angel 
investors 
are 
successful entrepreneurs 
who 
invest 
in 
companies 
in 
the 
same 
line 
of 
business. 
This 
type 
of 
investment 
is 
very common 
in 
the 
United 
States, 
but 
rare 
in 
Chile, 
which 
is 
partly 
due 
to 
a small 
domestic 
market 
that doesn't 
allow sufficient 
risk 
diversification. 
and 
"venture 
capital". 
Still, 
recent developments 
call for 
optimism. 
Initiatives 
from 
organizations 
like 
Fundacion 
Chile 
and 
COW0 
(Chile's 
Economic 
Development  Agency) 
are 
only 
two 
indications 
that 
Chile 
is 
slowly 
developing a 
tradition 
for 
seed 
capital. CORFO, 
for 
example, 
is 
taking 
measures 
to 
improve 
the 
financial 
situation 
for 
entrepreneurs and support 
innovative 
ideas 
without 
finance. 
It 
has 
a program 
to 
match 
venture 
capital investments 
of 
up to 
approximately 
US$ 
54,000 
so 
that 
risks 
and 
benefits 
are 
shared. 
These 
projects 
are 
required 
to 
have 
a 
significant innovation 
angle and healthy 
growth 
perspectives. 
A 
number 
of 
funds 
dedicated 
to 
investing 
in 
small-  and medium-sized 
companies 
(SME) 
have 
also 
emerged 
in 
Chile. 
The 
funds, 
totaling 
about 
$100 
million 
in 
capital 
altogether, 
have 
all 
been 
publicly 
traded, and 
are, 
in 
part, designed 
to 
be a 
conduit 
for 
Chilean 
pension funds 
to 
invest 
in 
small 
business 
(Pitch, 
1995). 
One 
example 
is 
ChileTech 
Risk 
Capital 
Fund, 
which primarily 
invests 
in 
agribusiness, 
forestry 
and 
marine 
resources 
businesses. 
Its 
goal 
is 
to 
invest 
in 
highly 
innovative 
enterprises, 
which 
seek 
to 
be 
competitors 
in 
the 
global 
marketplace and 
those 
that 
face 
barriers 
to 
entry beyond 
financial 
resources. 
The 
strategic 
importance 
of 
venture 
capital 
is 
also 
acknowledged 
by 
President 
Lagos, 
who 
in 
his 
presidential 
address 
committed 
his 
administration 
to 
the development 
of 
venture 
capital 
markets. 
Still 
these 
developments 
do 
not 
change 
the 
shodmedium 
term 
assessment 
of 
Chile: 
Lack 
of 
risk 
capital 
seems 
to 
be 
a 
major 
obstacle 
to 
innovation 
and 
technological development. 
- 
82 
- 
Page  87
Conclusions 
and recommendations 
Liberal 
policies, 
based 
on 
solid 
and 
reliable  institutions, 
have  succeeded 
in 
creating  a  favorable 
macroeconomic 
environment 
for 
science, 
technology and innovations 
in 
Chile. However, 
while 
scientific 
and, 
to 
a certain 
extent, 
technological development 
has 
made progress 
over 
the 
last 
decade, 
it 
remains 
disconnected 
from 
the 
economy and 
as 
such 
do not lead 
to 
continued product 
innovation 
and 
higher 
living 
standards. 
A 
number 
of 
factors 
help 
explain 
this 
situation, 
several 
of 
which 
should 
be 
addressed 
in 
the 
following 
years 
if 
Chile 
wants 
to 
significantly 
develop 
its 
capacity 
for 
science, 
technology 
and 
innovation: 
Realign the 
institutional 
set-up 
and 
increase 
S&T 
expenditures. 
A 
high-level 
political 
body 
should be 
vested 
with 
the powers and 
means 
to 
set 
the 
agenda 
for 
science, 
technology 
and 
innovation 
in 
Chile. 
S&T 
expenditures should, 
at 
the 
same 
time, 
be 
increased, 
which 
in 
turn calls 
for 
increased 
private 
sector 
participation 
in 
addition 
to 
an 
increase 
in 
public 
sector spending. 
Promote innovation 
in 
small and 
medium enterprises. 
Target 
public 
S&T 
policies 
towards 
the 
needs 
of 
SME’s 
as 
well 
as 
the 
promotion 
of 
linkages between 
national 
R&D 
institutions 
and industry. 
For 
this 
end, 
a 
set 
of 
indirect 
incentives 
- 
similar 
to 
those 
found internationally 
- 
could 
be 
developed 
to 
reduce 
private 
costs 
of 
STI 
related activities. 
Strengthen 
cooperation 
and  knowledge  diffusion 
mechanisms. 
Attention could 
inter 
alia 
be 
given 
to 
incentive 
structures 
for 
researchers, 
and 
the 
need 
to 
stimulate 
mobility 
of 
personnel 
within 
the 
science 
system 
and  between 
science 
and 
industry.  The  other  important 
policy  tool  for 
the 
promotion 
of 
private-public scientific cooperation 
is 
the 
establishment 
of 
centres 
of 
excellence. 
These 
help 
create 
and 
diffuse 
knowledge 
and can 
act 
as 
the 
core 
of 
innovation 
networks. 
Stimulate 
risk 
capital. 
Risk 
capital 
can 
be 
accessed 
either 
via 
FDI 
of 
domestic 
sources. 
The 
current 
supply 
of 
investments, 
foreign 
and domestic, 
is 
insufficient. 
FDI 
flows 
do 
not 
target 
technology-intensive 
sectors 
and domestic venture 
capital markets 
are 
still 
in 
their 
infancy. The question 
is, 
however, 
how 
to 
nurture 
risk 
capital 
in 
Chile? 
As 
already 
pointed 
out, 
some 
of 
the fundamentals, 
such 
as 
a 
solid 
legal 
framework 
and 
an 
open 
investment regime 
are 
already 
in 
place. 
In 
consequence, 
one 
of 
the 
key 
obstacles 
could 
be 
lack 
of 
demand 
for 
seed 
capital, 
which 
in 
turn 
reflects 
a risk-averse 
business 
community. 
-83- 
Page  88
Additional 
Annex 
12: 
Human 
Resources 
for 
the 
Knowledge 
for 
Economy 
CHILE: 
Science 
for 
the Knowledge Economy 
(This 
summary 
is 
based 
on 
Thomas 
Nikolaj 
Hansen, 
Lauritz 
Holm-Nielsen 
& 
Patricia 
Garcia 
Zliiiiga, 
2002. 
Human 
Resources 
for 
the 
Knowledge 
Economy. 
Washington, 
DC: 
World 
Bank) 
This 
paper takes 
stock 
at 
the 
Chilean education 
system 
and 
the 
country’s 
endowment 
of 
human 
capital. 
The 
objective 
is 
to 
assess 
whether 
Chile 
is 
adequately 
equipped 
in 
terms 
of 
human capital 
to 
develop 
into 
a 
knowledge-based economy.  The  paper 
opens 
by 
examining 
the 
key 
characteristics 
of 
the 
Chilean 
educational 
system, 
such 
as 
political 
organization, expenditure, enrollment,  attainment 
and 
quality 
of 
education. 
Against 
this 
backdrop, 
the 
paper 
investigates the 
skills 
profile 
of 
the 
Chilean population 
in 
order 
to 
assess 
whether 
Chile 
is 
in 
a 
position 
to 
develop 
into 
a knowledge-based 
economy. 
The 
Chilean Education 
System 
Organization 
The 
political 
organization 
of 
the 
Chilean education 
sector 
was 
profoundly 
changed 
following 
reforms 
in 
the 
early 
1980’s: 
Responsibility 
for 
basic 
public 
education 
was 
delegated 
from 
the 
Ministry 
for 
Education 
(MINEDUC) 
to 
municipalities 
and 
education 
institutions, 
and 
at 
the 
same 
time, 
a 
vast 
number 
of 
private 
and 
semi-private 
schools 
came 
into 
existence. 
As 
pointed 
out 
by 
Vegas 
(2002), 
decentralization 
led 
to 
an 
increase 
in 
educational 
achievement 
at 
the 
primary 
level 
in 
schools 
which 
at 
the 
same 
time 
enjoyed extensive 
teacher 
autonomy. 
Nevertheless, many 
education 
institutions 
have 
not 
been 
sufficiently 
capacitated 
to 
lift 
this 
task. 
This 
problem 
has 
been 
further 
aggravated 
by 
the 
fact 
that 
transfer 
of 
responsibilities 
has 
not 
been 
met 
by 
a corresponding 
rise 
in 
funding 
(Delannoy, 
2000). 
Like 
the basic 
education 
system, 
the 
tertiary 
level 
was 
reformed 
and 
decentralized 
profoundly 
in 
the 
early 
1980s 
following 
structural 
and 
financial 
reforms. 
Hence, 
the 
range 
of 
higher 
education 
institutions 
grew 
from 
originally 
eight universities 
into 
a 
total 
of 
257 
tertiary  institutions 
encompassing 
68 
universities 
(public 
and 
private), 
70 
professional institutes  (private) 
and 
1 
19 
technical 
training 
centers 
(private). 
Decentralization 
has 
had 
implications 
somewhat 
similar 
to 
those 
of 
the basic education 
system. 
First, 
the 
extensive 
autonomy 
means 
that 
MINEDUC 
and 
its 
division for 
higher 
education, 
DIVESUP, 
has 
limited 
possibilities 
to  control 
the 
course 
of 
the  higher  education 
sector. 
Second, 
not 
all 
of 
the 
tertiary 
institutions 
have 
been 
sufficiently 
capacitated 
for 
self-governance 
(World 
Bank, 
1998, 
p.4). 
This 
has, 
as 
the 
following 
sections 
will 
show, 
led 
to 
the 
establishment 
of 
a very 
heterogeneous 
tertiary 
sector. 
Finance 
for 
Education 
The 
overall 
level 
of 
public 
education expenditure 
decreased 
throughout the 
1980s 
as 
a 
consequence 
of 
cost-efficiency  reforms. Over 
the 
1990s, 
by 
contrast, 
public 
expenditure gained 
momentum, 
which 
to 
some 
extent 
can 
be 
seen 
as 
a reaction 
to 
the reforms 
of 
the 
1980s. 
Still, 
the 
current 
level 
of 
public 
expenditure constitutes 
only 
3.7% 
of 
GDP 
(1998), 
which 
is 
significantly below 
the 
average 
level 
in 
low 
and 
middle-income countries 
(4.2%) 
and high-income countries 
(5.6%). 
The 
relatively 
low 
share 
of 
public 
expenditure 
relative 
to 
GDP 
is 
compensated 
by 
a very 
high 
level 
of 
private 
sector 
funding 
amounting 
to 
approximately 
2.7% 
of 
GDP. The 
role 
of 
the 
private 
sector 
is 
especially dominant 
with 
regard 
to 
higher education. 
Hence, 
government 
funding 
as 
share 
of 
total 
tertiary funding 
dropped 
from 
a 
level 
of 
100% 
prior 
to 
the reforms 
of 
the 
early 
1980s 
to 
a 
level 
of 
only 
27% 
of by 
1990. 
The 
rules 
guiding 
the 
allocation 
of 
public 
expenditures 
have been changed 
profoundly 
over 
the 
last 
- 
84 
- 
Page  89
decades. 
Hence, 
one 
of 
the most significant traits 
of 
the 
reforms 
in 
the 
1980s 
was 
the 
introduction 
of 
the 
capitation 
system 
linking 
resources 
for 
basic 
education 
allocation 
to 
student 
attendance. 
This, 
in 
turn, 
has 
ensured 
a better 
fit 
with 
the 
needs 
of 
the 
system. 
The 
allocation 
rules 
guiding public 
expenditures 
for 
tertiary 
education 
have 
also 
been 
reformed 
in 
the 
past decades. 
Prior to 
the 
launch 
of 
the 
Government’s 
Higher  Education 
Improvement  Project 
in 
1998, 
50% 
of 
public 
funding 
for 
tertiary  education 
was 
allocated exclusively 
to 
25 
universities 
affiliated 
with 
the 
Consejo 
de 
Rectores 
(MINEDUC, 
1999). 
In 
consequence, 
this 
system 
was 
highly 
discriminatory to 
tertiary institutions not 
affiliated 
with 
the 
Consejo 
de  Rectores. 
Another 
significant 
share 
of 
public funding 
(9%) 
was 
allocated through the 
Institutional 
Development 
Fund 
(Fondo 
de 
Desarollo 
Znstitucional, 
FDI), 
which 
in 
turn 
consisted 
of 
four different 
competitive 
funds. 
FDI 
was 
heavily 
criticized for letting 
political 
criteria 
dominate 
the 
allocation 
of 
funds 
The 
higher education improvement 
project 
was 
launched 
in 
1998 
in 
order 
to 
level 
the 
playing 
field, 
by 
giving 
all 
tertiary 
education 
institutions 
equal 
access 
to 
direct 
subsidies 
and 
creating a US$225 
million 
competitive 
fund to 
replace the FDI-system 
(World 
Bank, 
1998, 
World 
Bank, 
2002f 
and 
CENDA, 
2001). 
Enrollment 
and 
attainment 
Chile 
has 
attained 
virtually 
universal 
coverage 
at 
the 
primary 
level. 
Chile 
has 
also 
seen 
a 
significant 
increase 
in 
secondary 
enrollment 
from 
approximately 
50% 
in 
1980 
to 
more 
than 
80% 
in 
1998. 
Finally, 
Chile 
has 
seen 
a notable 
increase 
in 
tertiary 
enrollment 
by 
regional 
standards. Hence,  whereas 
LAC 
tertiary enrollment 
rose 
from 
14 
to 
20% 
in 
1980-1998, 
the 
Chilean 
number 
went 
up 
from 
12% 
to 
34%. 
However, Chilean 
tertiary 
enrollment 
is 
still 
behind 
the 
OECD 
average 
of 
51%. 
The 
growth 
in 
tertiary 
enrollment 
has 
been 
partly 
facilitated 
by 
the 
increase 
in 
tertiary 
education institutions, 
notably private 
universities 
and 
technical institutes 
(Teixeira 
& 
Amaral, 
2001, 
p.13ff). 
Moreover,  Beyer 
et 
al. (1999) 
points out that 
the 
private 
returns 
to 
tertiary 
education have 
been 
increasing 
relative 
to 
primary 
and 
secondary 
over 
the 
past 
three 
decades, 
which 
in 
turn 
can 
explain 
the increase 
in 
tertiary 
enrollment 
from 
1980 
to 
the 
mid 
1990s. 
A 
simple correlation 
between 
educational attainment 
and 
average 
labor income 
suggests 
that 
the 
earnings 
of 
tertiary 
graduates 
went 
up 
from 
2.4 
times 
the 
earning 
of 
secondary graduates 
in 
1980 
to 
3.3 
times 
by 
1996. 
More 
sophisticated regression analysis 
likewise 
documents that the 
skill 
premium 
has 
been 
increasing over 
the 
last 
decades, 
although 
there 
has 
been 
a 
slight 
decrease 
in 
the 
1990s 
(SAnchez-Pkamo 
& 
Schady,  2002). 
As 
pointed 
out 
by 
Beyer 
et 
al. 
(2001, 
p.121f.) 
this 
reversion may 
reflect 
the 
increase 
in 
supply 
of 
tertiary 
graduates, 
which 
is 
expected 
to 
drive 
down 
the 
price 
on 
highly 
skilled 
labor. 
Equity 
The 
gains 
in 
enrollment 
and 
educational attainment have 
not 
been 
equally 
distributed. 
Inequity 
cross 
income 
quintiles 
is 
especially  dominant 
with 
regard 
to 
tertiary 
education;  a 
trend, 
which 
has 
been 
increasing 
in 
recent 
years: 
Tertiary 
enrollment 
rose 
by 
almost 50% 
for 
the richest income 
quintile, 
but 
was 
stagnant 
for 
the 
lowest 
income 
quintile 
(Araneda 
& 
Marin, 
2002). 
As 
pointed 
out 
by 
Teixeira 
and 
Imaral 
(2001, 
p.15) 
this 
follows 
from 
the 
fact 
that 
the 
poorest 
income 
quintiles 
cannot 
afford 
to enroll 
in 
the 
new 
private 
universities 
and 
find 
it 
difficult 
to 
be accepted 
to public 
universities, 
which 
are 
highly 
elitist 
in 
terms 
of 
academic 
admission criteria. 
In 
the 
legal 
framework 
of 
the 
institutions, 
there 
is 
no 
generalized social 
or 
ethnic 
discrimination 
in 
access 
to 
higher 
education and 
hence 
entry 
into 
the 
S&T 
sector 
as 
a 
researcher 
in 
Chile. 
Furthermore, 
the 
government 
has 
established a specific  scholarship 
program 
for 
indiginous 
people 
to 
access 
higher 
education.  Nevertheless 
the 
poor 
and 
people 
from 
minority 
backgrounds continue 
to 
be underrepresented 
among 
students 
and 
researchers, 
(Repetto 
and 
Holm-Nielsen, 
2003). 
High 
enrollment 
rates 
in 
Chile 
have  translated 
into 
increasing  educational 
attainment. Hence, 
Chile’s  attainment 
is 
according 
to 
Barro 
and 
Lee 
(2000) 
one 
of 
the highest 
in 
Latin 
America 
with 
7.9 
years 
of 
formal 
education 
almost 
equally  distributed 
between 
males 
and females. 
Chile’s domestic figures published 
by 
MINEDUC 
in 
the “Compendio  Estadistico 2000” 
present 
a 
10.17 
national 
figure for 
educational  attainment 
(with 
a 
range 
of 
8.43 
to 
1 
1.04 
between 
regions). 
This 
number 
- 
85 
- 
Page  90
is 
only 
exceeded 
in 
Latin 
America 
by 
Argentina 
with 
8.49 years 
and 
is 
well 
above the 
Latin 
American 
average 
of 
5.7 
years 
(Barro 
& 
Lee, 
2000). 
However,  a 
significant 
gap 
still 
exists 
to 
high-income 
countries, 
which 
feature 
9.5 
years 
of 
average 
educational attainment. 
Quality 
of 
Education 
Seen 
in 
a 
Latin 
American context, 
Chile 
has 
been 
in 
the 
avant-garde 
of 
Latin 
American 
countries 
in 
terms 
of 
developing 
and 
participating 
in 
quality 
assessments. 
In 
consequence, 
Chile 
today 
has 
access 
to 
a 
fairly 
wide selection 
of 
data 
on 
student 
performance. 
On 
a 
national 
level, SIMCE, 
Sistema 
de 
Medicidn 
de 
la 
Calidad 
de 
la 
Educacidn 
(System 
for 
the 
Measurement 
of 
Educational 
Quality) 
has 
been 
used 
consistently 
since 
1988 
to 
assess 
Spanish 
and Mathematics 
abilities 
of 
4th 
and 
8th 
graders. 
Following 
a 
negative trend 
in 
the 
198Os, 
the 
Spanish and 
Mathematics 
abilities 
of 
fourth 
graders 
in 
municipal 
schools 
have made 
significant 
progress over the 
last 
years 
(Cox 
& 
Lemaitre, 
1999, 
p.159). 
The 
national 
test 
average 
was 
around 
50 
in 
1988, 
but 
by 
1996 
the 
pupils 
had 
achieved 
a 
score 
of 
72. 
Moreover,  the 
progress applies 
across 
the 
board 
seeing 
that 
the 
gap 
in 
test 
average 
between urban 
and 
rural 
has 
decreased 
from 
30 
percentage 
point 
in 
1992 
to 
roughly 
10 
percentage 
points 
by 
1996 
(Delannoy, 
2000). 
Chile 
has, 
unlike 
most 
Latin 
American  countries, 
taken 
part 
in 
various 
regional 
and 
international 
assessments 
of 
student 
performance, 
notably 
the 
UNESCO/OREALC-assessment, 
and 
the 
international 
TIMSS-test. 
Chile 
emerged 
among  the 
top  four 
countries 
in 
the 
regional 
UNESCO/OREALC 
benchmarking, 
which 
was 
conducted to 
assess 
mathematics 
and 
language 
in 
the 
LAC 
region. 
Unlike 
the 
UNESCO/OREALC 
assessment, 
the 
TIMSS-test 
puts 
the 
performance 
of 
Chilean 
students 
into 
a 
truly 
global 
perspective. 
Hence, 
Chile 
participated 
as 
the 
only 
representative 
from 
the 
LAC 
region 
in 
the 1999 
TIMSS-test 
together 
with 
37 other countries 
mainly 
from 
North 
America, 
Europe 
and 
the 
Far 
East. 
The 
test 
was 
designed 
to 
benchmark 
the 
mathematics and 
science 
ability 
of 
students 
at 
the 
eighth 
grade 
level. The top performers 
in 
the 
sample 
were 
mainly 
Asian 
countries 
like 
Taiwan, 
Singapore and 
Korea. 
Chile 
on 
the 
on 
the 
other 
hand 
was 
rated 
below 
the 
mean score 
and 
notably behind 
several 
Central 
and 
East 
European countries 
like 
Hungary, the 
Czech and 
Slovak 
Republic 
and 
the 
Russian Federation. 
In 
conclusion, 
the 
quality 
of 
Chilean education 
is 
among the 
highest 
in 
Latin 
America, 
but 
significantly 
behind 
East 
Asian 
countries,  most 
East- and 
Central 
European countries 
as 
well 
as 
the 
majority 
of 
OECD-countries. 
Prevalence 
of 
outdated 
curricula 
and 
low 
priority 
of 
science 
education 
at 
the 
basic 
level 
helps 
explain 
why 
Chile 
is 
lagging 
behind. 
As 
pointed 
out 
by 
Arellano 
(2002), 
the 
Chilean 
science 
curriculum 
has 
been 
relatively 
limited 
in 
Chile 
compared 
to 
many other 
countries, 
notably 
the 
Far 
Eastern countries 
which 
have 
put 
extensive 
focus 
on 
the 
exact 
sciences. 
Likewise, 
Mullin 
et 
al. (2000) 
point 
out that 
science 
teaching 
in 
Chile 
has 
suffered 
from 
serious 
deficiencies. 
- 
86 
- 
Page  91
Quality 
in 
higher 
education 
Compared 
to 
the 
primary 
and 
secondary 
level, very 
few indicators 
exist 
to 
track the 
level 
of 
quality 
at 
the 
tertiary level. 
Still, 
the 
educational 
level 
of 
faculty 
staff 
can 
give 
a 
rough 
indication 
of 
the 
quality 
level. 
1997 
data 
on 
faculty  staff 
in 
public 
universities  reveal that 
only 
12% 
of 
faculty  staff 
in 
Chile 
have 
PhD-degrees. 
Compared 
to 
most 
high-income 
OECD 
countries 
this 
numbers 
is 
very 
low, 
and 
indicate 
in 
turn 
that 
quality 
of 
faculty staff 
in 
Chilean universities 
is 
low 
(Garcia 
Guadilla 
in 
World 
Bank, 
1999b, 
p.36). 
A 
1998 
World 
Bank 
assessment 
further 
concludes 
that 
quality 
is 
very 
heterogeneous 
in 
the 
tertiary 
education 
sector. 
The 
public 
higher education 
sector 
is 
generally considered 
to 
be 
high 
quality, 
whereas 
quality 
levels tend 
to 
be 
lower 
in 
private 
sector 
institutions, 
notably 
IP’s 
(Professional Institutes) 
and 
CTF’s (Technical 
Training 
Centers). 
This picture 
of 
heterogeneity 
is 
arguably a result 
of 
a 
flawed 
quality 
framework. 
The 
absence 
of 
systematic 
quality 
assessments 
naturally 
prevents  policymakers 
from 
identifying 
the 
strengths 
and 
weaknesses 
of 
the tertiary 
system. 
Yet, 
progress 
has 
been 
made 
with 
the 
decision 
to 
make 
the 
Higher  Council 
for 
Education  responsible 
for 
developing 
a 
coherent  and 
all-encompassing 
quality 
system. 
These 
efforts 
are 
supported 
by 
the 
Higher 
Education Improvement 
project (MECESUP), which, 
inter 
alia, 
promotes 
the 
adoption 
of 
a 
flexible 
accreditation 
system, 
which 
will 
authorities (and 
students!) 
to 
monitor 
and benchmark 
higher education 
institutions 
more 
closely. 
So 
far 
the 
results 
are 
encouraging. 
According 
to 
a 
recent 
Bank mission (20020, 
the 
changes have 
stimulated 
a 
sense 
of 
competitiveness between 
the 
different 
higher education 
institutions, 
which 
in 
turn 
gives 
them 
an 
incentive 
to 
improve 
the 
quality 
and 
relevance 
of 
their programs. 
Diversification 
is 
a 
keyword for  improving 
the 
quality 
of 
educational output 
in 
the 
higher education 
sector. 
The 
strategy 
is 
to allow 
institutions 
to 
capitalize 
on 
their 
comparative 
advantages 
with 
a 
view 
to 
provide 
specialized 
high-quality  institutions 
rather  than 
“department-stores” 
with 
a 
full 
stock 
of 
educational 
programs. 
At 
the 
same 
time diversification 
strategies 
try 
to 
create a market, 
where 
public 
and 
private education suppliers 
compete, 
which 
in 
turn 
should induce 
them 
to 
cater 
to 
the 
needs 
of 
the 
students. 
As 
such 
the 
introduction 
of 
the 
1981 
reform 
of 
the 
higher education 
sector can 
be 
seen 
as 
a 
step 
ahead 
by 
allowing for 
a 
differentiation 
of 
higher  education 
institutions. 
Still, 
a 
1998 
World 
Bank 
evaluation 
pointed 
out that the 
diversification 
had 
led 
to 
widespread 
duplication 
leaving 
universities 
and 
CFTs competing 
for 
the 
same 
students 
rather 
than 
develop complementary  programs 
(World 
Bank, 
1998). 
Moreover, 
the 
enrollment 
figures 
indicate a 
lack 
of 
success 
among 
many 
of 
the new institutions. 
Whereas 
university 
enrollment 
has 
increased 
markedly, enrollment 
in 
technical 
training 
centers 
dropped 
17% 
in 
a 
three-year 
period. 
This 
is 
arguably 
due 
to 
lack 
of 
quality 
in 
these 
institutions. 
Moreover, 
the 
vertical 
integration  between 
secondary  and 
tertiary  technical  programs 
is 
very  poor. 
The 
lack 
of 
quality 
assessment at 
the 
tertiary 
level 
means 
that 
tertiary 
technical 
institutions 
operate 
with 
outdated curricula, 
which 
neglect 
the 
needs 
of 
the economy. 
In 
conclusion, there 
is 
no 
educational 
or 
economic incentive 
for 
secondary graduates 
to enroll 
in 
tertiary 
technical 
institutions. 
The 
Skills 
Profile 
The 
skill 
premium 
for 
tertiary 
graduates 
(relative 
to 
secondary 
and 
primary 
graduates) 
has 
been 
rising 
significantly 
over the last 
decades 
in 
Chile 
(Beyer 
at al., 
1999). 
This 
in 
turn 
indicates 
that 
knowledge 
and 
high 
skills 
are 
in 
strong 
demand. 
Still, 
this 
does 
not reveal 
which 
specific 
skills 
are 
in 
demand. 
Higher order 
skills 
Higher 
order 
skills 
encompass 
the 
ability 
to 
perform 
independent  analysis,  adapt 
quickly 
to  new 
situations and interact 
with 
other 
persons 
on a professional 
level 
and 
show 
tolerance 
and 
solidarity. 
Globally 
these 
skills 
are 
in 
increasing 
demand. 
This 
also applies 
to 
Chile, 
which 
due 
to 
its 
extensive 
economic 
openness 
is 
forced 
to 
react 
to 
a constantly changing 
international 
context. 
The 
1998 
International 
Adult 
Literacy 
Survey 
(IALS) 
can be 
used 
to 
approximate 
proliferation 
of 
higher 
- 
a7 
- 
Page  92
order 
skills 
in 
Chile. The 
survey 
targeted, 
inter 
alia, 
the 
ability 
to 
understand and 
use 
information 
from 
various 
text 
materials, the 
ability 
to locate 
and 
apply 
information 
from 
formats 
such 
as 
job 
applications, 
itineraries, 
tables 
and charts, 
and, 
finally, 
the 
ability to 
do arithmetic operations (OECD, 
2000). 
Clearly, 
these 
definitions 
do 
not 
encompass 
the 
full 
meaning 
of 
higher order 
skills, 
but 
they 
do 
capture, 
however, 
the 
ability 
of 
individuals 
to 
locate relevant 
printed 
information 
and 
make 
effectively 
use 
thereof. 
Chile 
was 
the 
only 
Latin 
America 
country 
to 
take 
part 
in 
IALS 
assessment, 
which 
in 
addition 
to Chile 
consists 
of 
18 
OECD-countries 
and Slovenia. 
Chile 
was 
located 
at 
the 
very 
bottom 
across 
all 
types 
of 
literacy. 
These 
results 
imply 
that 
only 
20% 
of 
the 
Chilean 
population 
have 
a reading comprehension 
sufficient 
to 
deal 
with 
the 
“the 
demands 
of 
everyday 
life 
and 
work 
in 
a complex 
advanced 
society” 
(Arellano, 
2002, 
p.680. 
A 
further 
breakdown 
of 
the 
results 
shows 
that 
the 
low 
literacy 
levels 
are 
especially prevalent among 
the 
older 
age 
cohorts, 
which 
in 
turn 
makes 
a 
case 
for intensifying training 
efforts 
targeting 
the 
older 
part 
of 
the 
workforce 
(see 
section 
on 
lifelong 
learning below) (OECD, 
2000, 
p.34). 
In 
consequence, 
Chileans 
still 
have 
a 
long 
way 
to 
go 
in 
developing higher order 
skills, 
and 
the 
economy 
will, by 
implication, 
remain vulnerable 
to 
external 
shocks. 
Hard 
skills 
Chilean high-technology products 
amount 
to only 
5% 
of 
total 
exports, 
a 
number 
which 
is 
significantly 
below 
the 
levels 
in 
other 
LAC 
countries 
such 
as 
Mexico 
(60%), 
Brazil 
(32%)  and 
Argentina 
(19%) 
(UNDP, 
2001). 
In 
sum, 
current 
demand 
for 
hard 
skills 
(scientists, 
engineers, 
technological workers 
etc.) 
seems 
to 
be 
lower 
in 
Chile 
than 
in 
most 
advanced 
OECD 
countries. 
This 
in 
turn 
is 
a 
serious 
obstacle 
for 
generating 
rapid 
technological growth. 
Enrollment 
in 
technical-vocational 
secondary 
programs 
has 
increased 
consistently  over the  last 
two 
decades, 
while 
enrollment 
in 
tertiary 
technical 
training 
centers 
has 
been decreasing 
(Araneda 
& 
Marin, 
2002). 
As 
pointed 
out earlier, 
this 
is, 
inter 
alia, 
due 
to 
lack 
of 
integration 
between technical programs at 
the 
secondary 
and 
tertiary level. 
Moreover, the expansion 
of 
private universities 
has 
favored soft 
skills 
at 
the 
expense 
of 
hard 
skills 
programs. 
Whereas 
basic science 
programs 
represent 12% 
of 
careers 
offered 
in 
traditional 
universities, 
it 
only 
takes 
up 
0.5% 
in 
the 
private 
universities. 
Social 
sciences, 
by 
contrast, 
represent 
27% 
of 
careers 
in 
the 
private 
sector 
and 
only 7% 
in 
the 
traditional 
university 
sector. 
This 
arguably 
reflects  a 
lack 
of 
willingness  among 
privately  funded  institutions 
to 
engage 
in 
expensive 
programs; 
a 
low-risk 
behavior 
identified 
in 
most 
countries according 
to 
Teixeira 
& 
Amaral(2001, 
p.21). 
The hard 
skills 
profile 
of 
Chile 
is 
further 
weakened 
by 
a 
low 
turnover 
of 
PhD’s 
in 
hard 
skills 
fields 
such 
as 
natural and 
exact 
sciences, 
medical 
sciences 
and 
engineering 
and 
technology 
(RICYT, 
2001). One 
possible explanation 
for 
the 
low 
turnover 
of 
researchers 
could 
be the 
fact 
that 
potential 
researchers 
perceive that 
they 
exclude 
themselves 
from 
careers 
in 
the 
private 
sector 
by 
enrolling 
in 
PhD 
programs. 
Thus, 
research 
programs 
are 
chiefly 
associated 
with 
careers 
in 
universities 
and 
research 
institutions 
(Mullin 
et 
al., 
2000, 
p.86ff). 
In 
sum, 
Chile 
faces 
an 
important 
challenge 
in 
making 
PhD 
programs more 
attractive. 
This 
can 
be 
facilitated by bringing 
research 
programs 
in 
better 
agreement 
with 
the 
needs 
of 
the 
economy. 
This 
in 
turn 
would 
make 
PhD 
candidates 
more 
attractive 
to 
the enterprise 
sector 
and 
boost 
the 
skill 
premium 
to 
PhD 
doctorates. 
ICT-literacy 
ICT-skills 
appear 
to 
be 
in 
stronger 
demand 
in 
Chile 
than 
hard 
skills 
generally. 
Hence, 
Chile 
features 
a 
very 
high 
penetration 
of 
ICT-technologies, 
but 
is 
still 
behind 
the 
levels 
of 
most 
OECD 
countries. 
Still, 
as 
Agapitova 
& 
Holm-Nielsen 
(2002. 
p.6) 
point 
out, 
knowledge 
intensive 
services 
such 
as 
computing 
have 
experienced very 
rapid 
growth 
in 
Chile. 
Telecommunications 
is 
likewise 
a very dynamic 
sector, 
and 
has 
in 
the 
wake 
of 
privatization 
made 
impressive 
progress 
in 
terms 
of 
equipping 
the 
nation 
with 
fast 
and 
reliable 
means 
of 
communication. Currently, 
the sector 
thrives 
due 
to 
large 
investments 
in 
long-distance 
telephony 
and 
cellular 
and 
wireless communication. 
- 
88 
- 
Page  93
As 
pointed 
out 
in 
a 
recent study 
by 
a 
Chilean 
presidential 
commission, 
the 
Chilean 
endowment 
of 
ICT-skills 
was 
far 
from 
sufficient 
by 
the 
late 
1990s 
(Comision 
Presidencial, 
1999,  p.61). 
Still, 
it 
is 
difficult 
to 
get 
a 
more 
detailed 
picture 
seeing 
that 
very 
little 
data 
is 
available. 
Some 
of 
the 
only 
data 
on 
the 
quality 
of 
Chilean 
ICT-workers 
has 
been 
provided by 
the 
World 
Economic Forum, 
who 
in 
a 
survey 
asked 
employers 
in 
a 
number 
of 
different 
countries 
to 
rate 
the 
quality 
of 
ICT-workers relative 
to 
other 
countries. 
Chilean 
employers 
rated 
the 
domestic  supply 
of 
ICT-workers 
among 
the 
best 
in 
the 
Latin 
America. 
In 
fact, 
Chile 
was, 
together 
with 
Costa 
Rica 
and 
Brazil, 
far 
above 
the 
mean 
value 
for 
Latin 
America 
and 
the 
East 
Asia 
and the 
Pacific. 
High-income 
OCED 
countries, 
on the other 
hand, 
were 
rated 
superior 
to 
ICT-workers 
in 
all 
other 
countries 
(Arellano, 
2002). 
Additional 
survey 
data, 
likewise 
from 
the 
World 
Economic 
Forum 
(2002), 
indicates that the 
quality 
of 
IT 
education 
programs 
is 
second 
only to 
Costa 
Rica 
in 
Latin 
America. 
Still, 
this 
rating 
is 
only slightly 
above 
the 
global 
average 
and 
Chile 
is 
far behind the 
quality 
of 
ICT-education programs 
in 
avant-garde 
countries 
like 
Finland, 
the 
Netherlands 
and the 
US. 
In 
conclusion, 
there 
is 
still 
room for 
improvement 
of 
Chilean 
ICT-education. 
Expanding 
education: 
Training 
and 
lifelong 
learning 
As 
noted 
by 
the 
IALS 
survey, 
the 
skills 
of 
the 
Chilean workforce 
lag 
significantly 
behind 
those 
of 
more 
developed 
economies, 
especially 
so 
among 
the older cohorts 
of 
the 
workforce. 
4.5 
million 
adult Chileans 
have 
not 
completed their 
primary 
education and 
another 
2 
million 
adults 
have 
yet 
to 
complete 
secondary 
education. 
In 
sum, 
close 
to 
70% 
of 
the 
adult  population 
is 
without 
completed 
secondary 
education. 
Further, 
500,000 
adults 
are 
illiterates, 
which 
corresponds 
to 
4.3% 
of 
the 
adult 
population 
(World 
Bank, 
2002d, 
p.4). 
In 
consequence, 
Chile 
faces 
a 
major 
challenge 
in 
elevating 
the 
skills 
of 
its 
adult 
work 
force. 
Compared 
to 
most 
Latin 
American countries, 
Chilean 
companies 
seem 
to 
be 
fairly 
proactive 
with 
regard 
to 
investments 
in 
staff 
training. 
Hence, 
Chilean, 
Brazilian 
and Costa 
Rican 
companies 
are 
among 
the 
leaders 
in 
the 
region 
in 
terms 
of 
investments 
in 
training, 
whereas 
countries 
like 
Mexico 
and 
Colombia 
lag 
behind. 
Still, 
investments 
in 
Chilean 
companies 
are 
only on 
average 
in 
a 
global 
comparison, 
far 
behind 
several 
Western 
European 
countries 
and 
the 
United 
States. 
Further 
evidence 
points 
out that 
less 
than 
10% 
of 
the 
Chilean workforce 
have 
received 
training 
(World 
Bank, 
2002d, p.6). 
In 
conclusion, there 
seems 
to 
be 
a strong 
need 
for 
staff 
training 
in 
Chile 
notwithstanding 
the 
fact 
that 
the 
country 
compares 
well 
in 
Latin 
America. Moreover, 
the 
IALS 
survey 
found 
that 
Chilean 
professionals 
are 
far more 
likely 
to 
take 
part 
in 
training 
programs 
than 
blue-collar 
workers (OECD, 
2000, 
p.4lff). 
As 
pointed 
out 
in 
a 
recent 
World 
Bank 
assessment 
of 
Chile 
(2002d), 
lifelong 
learning policies 
must 
deal 
with 
numerous 
challenges, 
of 
which 
three 
are 
especially salient. 
First, 
many 
companies, 
notably 
SME’s 
cannot 
afford 
to 
supply 
training to 
their 
employees. 
Clearly, 
tax 
rebate 
schemes 
have 
had 
some 
success 
and 
have, 
for 
example, 
led 
to 
an 
increase 
in 
the number 
of 
workers 
participating 
in 
training 
programs 
from 
approximately 
450,000 
in 
1996 
to 
more 
than 
620,000 
by 
2000 
(Araneda 
& 
Marin, 
2002, 
table 
2.1 
1). 
Still, 
the 
bulk 
of 
companies 
participating 
in 
the 
scheme 
are 
large 
companies 
who 
already make 
use 
of 
training 
in 
their 
strategies. 
Second, 
the 
current supply 
of 
training 
in 
Chile 
is 
not 
attentive to 
the 
needs 
of 
the 
small 
firms 
and 
employees. 
Even 
though 
little 
evaluation 
has 
been 
done 
so 
far 
of 
Chilean 
training 
programs, Araneda 
& 
Marin 
(2002) 
point 
out that 
there 
is 
an 
emerging 
consensus 
that 
the 
quality 
and relevance 
of 
training 
programs 
is 
insufficient. 
This 
in 
turn 
can 
be 
explained 
by 
the 
lack 
of 
information 
between suppliers and 
demanders 
of 
training, 
which 
serves 
to uphold 
current imbalances rather than correct 
them. 
Moreover, 
lack 
of 
indicators 
and systematic 
evaluations 
mean that 
suppliers 
of 
training 
face weak 
incentives 
to 
increase the 
quality 
of 
their 
programs. 
Third, 
SME’s 
do not generally 
appreciate 
the 
externalities 
associated 
with 
training 
programs and there 
has 
generally 
been 
very 
little 
interest 
in 
training 
programs 
in 
political 
and academic 
environments. 
This 
- 
89 
- 
Page  94
in 
turn 
begs 
for 
campaigns 
designed 
to 
raise the 
awareness 
of 
the benefits 
associated 
with 
training 
programs. 
So 
far, 
the 
Chilean 
education 
system 
has 
not 
been 
successful 
in 
supplying 
“skills 
for 
the 
knowledge 
economy”. 
The 
analysis 
has 
pointed 
out 
that Chileans 
lag 
behind 
with 
regard 
to 
higher 
order 
skills 
and 
hard 
skills. 
The 
supply 
of 
ICT-skills 
arguably also 
needs 
to 
be 
stimulated further. Moreover, 
as 
pointed 
out 
by 
Brunner, 
the 
low 
supply 
of 
hard 
skills 
constitutes a 
major 
bottleneck 
to 
the 
development 
of 
the 
Chilean 
economy. 
Part 
of 
the 
reason 
is 
that the 
tertiary 
technical education 
system 
has 
been 
allowed 
to 
drift 
a part, 
which 
in 
turn 
has 
reduced 
the 
inventive 
to 
enroll. Moreover, 
the 
current 
training 
system 
suffers 
from 
substantial 
flaws 
and 
is 
not 
seen 
as 
a 
strategic 
tool 
by 
employers. 
Unattractive 
PhD 
programs 
likewise 
add 
to 
the 
picture 
of 
an 
education 
system 
in 
need 
of 
reform. 
In 
consequence, 
Chile 
could 
increase 
its 
education 
level significantly 
by 
making 
PhD 
and 
tertiary 
programs more attractive, especially 
in 
the 
hard 
skills 
area. 
Finally, 
and perhaps 
most 
important, 
the 
private 
sector 
in 
Chile 
does 
not 
seem 
to 
be demanding 
hard 
skills. 
In 
consequence, 
a vicious 
circle 
has 
emerged, 
where 
the 
low 
demand 
for 
hard 
skills 
keeps 
Chile’s 
technological 
development deadlocked. 
The question 
is 
then; 
will 
Government 
intervention 
break 
this 
deadlock? 
- 
90 
- 
Page  95
Additional 
Annex 
13: 
Letter 
of 
Development Program 
CHILE: 
Science 
for 
the 
Knowledge Economy 
GOBIERNO 
DE 
CHILE 
MINISTEIUO 
DE 
HACIENDA 
Direccidn 
de 
Presupuestos 
QQ-040 
* 
28.03.03 
Santiago, 
3 
1 
MAR 
2003 
Mr. 
James 
Wolfensohn 
President 
The 
World 
Bank 
1818 
H 
Street, 
NW 
F 
1227 
Washington, 
D.C. 
20433 
Dear 
Mr. 
Wolfensohn, 
I 
am 
pleased 
to 
present 
you 
with 
this 
letter, 
outlining 
the  our 
strategies 
in 
the 
area 
of 
science 
and 
technology 
and 
the 
objectives 
that 
Chile 
seeks 
to 
achieve through 
the 
“Science 
for 
the 
Knowledge 
Economy Project”, 
currently being 
negotiated 
with 
the 
World 
Bank. 
Our relationship 
with 
the 
Bank 
is 
excellent, 
particularly 
in 
regard 
to 
investment 
in 
human 
capital 
and 
science. 
In 
recent 
years, 
the 
Bank 
and 
Chile 
have 
worked together on 
five 
different 
projects strengthening 
all 
levels 
of 
our education 
system. 
Active 
at 
this 
point, 
is 
a 
higher 
education improvement 
project 
and a 
project 
on 
lifelong 
learning and training. 
The 
Chilean 
Government 
is 
strongly 
committed 
to 
creating 
the 
conditions 
for 
continuous economic 
growth. The 
government 
believes that 
increased 
competitiveness 
in 
the 
global 
market 
place, 
productivity 
gains 
and 
social development 
is 
highly 
dependent 
of 
a strong knowledge 
base. 
For 
that 
reason 
it 
is 
paramount 
to 
place 
Chile 
among 
the 
world 
leaders 
in 
terms 
of 
production 
and 
application 
of 
knowledge 
and 
technology. 
The Chilean Government 
has 
already 
taken 
steps 
to 
strengthen 
the 
science 
base 
and the 
stock 
of 
human 
capital. 
In 
a 
joint 
effort 
with 
the 
World 
Bank, 
the 
Millennium 
Science 
Initiative 
has 
supported 
research 
in 
Centers 
of 
Excellence and 
Science 
Nuclei.  The project 
has 
helped 
revitalize 
Chile’s 
S&T 
system 
by 
attracting world-class scientists 
engaged 
in 
cutting-edge 
research. 
While 
much 
had 
been 
already 
accomplished, 
Chile 
is 
still 
lagging 
developed nations 
in 
terms 
of 
investment 
in 
research 
and 
development. 
To 
address 
this 
challenged, the Government 
has 
pledged 
to 
double 
the 
funds  allocated 
to 
S&T 
by 
the 
year 
2006. 
This 
entails  a 
significant 
increase 
in 
S&T 
-91 
- 
Page  96
investment 
from 
the 
current 
level 
of 
0.6% 
of 
GDP. 
This 
would 
be 
necessary 
to 
place 
Chile 
among 
the 
world 
leaders 
in 
S&T. 
Increasing 
government 
funding 
for 
R&D 
will 
not 
suffice. 
Bringing 
Chile to 
the 
knowledge 
frontier 
and 
improving 
competitiveness 
necessitates 
a 
strong 
involvement 
of 
Chilean 
companies 
to 
R&D. 
Improving 
quality 
of 
research, 
strengthening 
human 
capital 
and 
reinforcing 
linkages 
is 
key 
to 
achieve 
this 
end. 
It 
is 
equally important 
to 
create 
a 
policy 
framework 
that provides incentives to 
engage 
in 
R&D 
activities. 
The 
proposed 
project 
“Science 
for 
the 
Knowledge 
Economy” 
is 
fully 
aligned 
with 
the 
government’s 
aim 
to 
strengthen 
Chile’s 
science 
base 
and 
innovative 
capacity. 
The 
main 
objective 
is 
to 
support 
Chile’s 
transition 
to 
a 
knowledge-based economy 
by 
investing 
in 
the 
national innovation 
system and the stock 
of 
human 
capital. 
The 
Government 
bases 
its 
commitment 
to 
the above 
objective on 
the 
following 
guiding 
principles. 
0 
Efficient 
and 
coordinated 
science, 
technology 
and 
innovation 
policy 
framework 
with 
strong 
economic 
incentives. Existence 
of 
a coherent 
policy 
and 
regulatory 
framework 
with 
concrete 
policies 
and 
instruments conducive 
to 
innovation 
and 
human 
capital 
formation. 
Long 
term 
funding 
of 
economic relevant 
science. 
Support 
for 
high 
quality 
knowledge production 
of 
high 
relevance 
to 
the 
Chilean 
society 
and development 
of 
a 
critical 
mass 
of 
researchers. 
Support 
to 
private 
sector 
R&D 
and 
cross-sectoral 
research cooperation. 
Promotion 
of 
public-private 
linkages 
and 
measures 
that 
enhance 
the research capacity 
of 
the 
private 
sector. 
0 
The Chilean Government 
pledges 
its 
commitment 
to 
the 
phase 
I 
of 
the 
program. 
This 
phase 
will 
invest 
in 
Chile’s 
science 
base, 
create 
a 
policy 
framework 
conducive 
to 
innovation 
and 
establish new 
mechanisms 
to 
increase 
private 
sector 
R&D 
activities 
and 
build 
linkages 
in 
the 
innovation 
system. 
The Government intends 
to 
continue 
collaboration 
with 
the 
World 
Bank beyond 
phase 
I. 
Phase 
II 
will 
continue 
activities 
from 
phase 
I 
and 
scale 
up 
funding 
for 
established 
mechanisms. 
Note 
has 
been 
taken 
of 
the 
fact 
that 
launch 
of 
phase 
II 
depends 
on the 
achievement 
of 
predefined performance triggers. 
Strengthening 
the 
national 
innovation 
system 
and 
the 
stock 
of 
human 
capital 
is 
a 
high priority. 
For 
that 
reason, 
the 
Chilean 
government 
is 
dedicated 
to 
provide 
adequate 
funding,  make 
the 
necessary 
policy 
changes 
and 
establish a 
monitoring 
and 
evaluation 
system 
that 
will 
allow 
impact 
to 
be 
assessed 
on a 
continuous 
basis. 
We 
thank 
you 
for 
your consideration 
of 
the 
foregoing. 
Respectfully 
yours, 
Mario 
Marcel 
Cullell 
Director 
de 
Presupuestos 
cc.: 
Sr. 
Axel 
Van 
Trotsenburg, 
Director 
Banco 
Mundial 
para 
Chile, Argentina, 
Paraguay 
y 
Uruguay
